THE RED PAGE.
THE SPHINN
IN a dim corner of my room for longer than my
fancy thinks
A beautiful and silent Sphinx has watched
me through the shifting gloom.
Inviolate and immobile, she does not rise, she does
not stir,
For silver moons are naught to her and naught to
her the suns that reel. . .
Dawn follows dawn and nights grow old and all
the while this curious cat
Lies couching on the Chinese mat with eyes of
satin rimmed with gold.
Come forth my lovely seneschal 1 so somnolent,
so statuesque !
Come forth you exquisite grotesque ! half woman
and half animal !
Come forth my lovely languorous Sphinx ! and put
your head upon my knee !
And let me stroke your throat and see your body
spotted like the lynx !
Lift up your large black satin eyes which are like
cushions where one sinks !
Fawn at my feet, fantastic Sphinx ! and sing me
all your memories ! . . .
Sing to me of the labyrinth in which the tvvy-
formed bull was stalled !
Sing to me of the night you crawled across the
temple’s granite plinth
When through the purple corridors the screaming
scarlet ibis Hew
In terror, and a horrid dew dripped from the
moaning mandragores,
And the great torpid crocodile within the tank
shed slimy tears,
And tare the jewels from his ears and staggered
back into the Nile,
And the priests cursed you with shrill psalms as
in your claws you seized their snake
And crept away with it to slake your passion by
the shuddering palms.
Why are you tarrying ? Get hence ! I weary of
your sullen ways,
I weary of your steadfast gaze, your somnolent
magnificence.
Your horrible and heavy breath makes the light
flicker in the lamp,
And on my brow I feel the damp and dreadful
dews of night and death.
Your eyes are like fantastic moons that shiver in
some stagnant lake,
Your tongue is like a scarlet snake that dances to
fantastic tunes.
Your pulse makes poisonous melodies, and your
black throat is like the hole
Left by some torch or burning coal on Saracenic
tapestries.
Away ! The sulphur-coloured stars are hurrying
through the Western gate !
Away ! Or it may be too late to climb their silent
silver cars!
Away to Egypt! Have no fear. Only one God
has ever died.
Only one God has let His side be wounded by a
soldier’s spear.
But these, thy lovers, are not dead. Still by the
hundred-cubit gate
Dog-faced Anubis sits in state with lotus-lilies for
thy head.
Still from his chair of porphyry gaunt Memnon
strains his lidless eyes
Across the empty land, and cries each yellow
morning unto thee.
And Nilus with his broken horn lies in his black
and oozy bed
And till thy coming will not spread his waters on
the withering corn.
Your lovers are not dead, I know. They will rise
up and hear your voice
And clash their cymbals and rejoice and run to
kiss your mouth ! And so,
Set wings upon your argosies ! Set horses to your
ebon car !
Back to your Nile ! Or if you are grown sick of'
dead divinities
Follow some roving lion’s spoor across the copper-
coloured plain,
.Reach out and hale him by the mane and bid him
be your paramour!
Couch by his side upon the grass and set your
'white teeth in his throat,
And when you hear his dying note lash your long
flanks of polished brass
And take a tiger for your mate, whose amber sides
are flecked with blade,
And ride upon his gilded back in triumph through
the Theban gate,
And toy with him in amorous jests, and when he
turns, and snarls, and gnaws,
O smite him with your jasper claws ! and bruise
him with your agate breasts !
False Sphinx ! False Sphinx ! By reedy Styx
old Charon, leaning on his oar,
Waits for my coin. Go thou before, and leave
me to my crucifix,
Whose pallid burden, sick with pain, watches the
world with wearied eyes,
And weeps for every soul that dies, and weeps for
every soul in vain.
Osoae Wilde.
[The Sphinx was published in London in 1894, in a
limited and expensive edition ; and deserves to be known
better. The poem has eighty-seven stanzas, from
which those foregoing are excerpted. It is splendid in
sonority and rich with barbaric colour, a chant in
copper and scarlet. Sherard has told of the chase for
magnificent words, and of the pride with which he offered
nenuphar.. The piece is Wilde’s most original feat in
verse, and is indeed unique. For local interest Wilde may
>e compared with Daley : two Irishmen : the one weav-
ng verse into a sumptuous tapestry, the other painting
upon satin.]
THE FOR
How many people realise that the
dominant, the overwhelming factor
in Australian politics is the Asiatic
factor ? We should speak of
Asianaustral territories rather than of
Australasian. All the paltry questions
that vex us—the Capital question and the
Drink question, the Customs question and
the Labour question—are mere dust in the
balance when weighed against the great
Asiatic questions : Can you tight ? When
can you tight ? How can you tight ? Those
interrogatories are the ultimate touchstone
before which all our careful edifice of brass-
and-clay democracy will one day crumble
like a child’s sand-castle before the tide.
When Asia moves ! When Asia moves !
Asia has moved before, and all the force of
Europe scarcely availed to save European
civilization. A new Attila may not be
worsted at another Chalons. But Australia#
the lonely European outpost, the rich land
occupied by a handful of people eager for
pleasure : who will defend her in the day of
extremity ? Not that Britain which has
accepted an Asiatic alliance for her own safe-
guard, that Britain whose native strength
has been sapped by the vices of wealth and
the diseases of poverty, whose pristine spirit
has degenerated into the hysterics of city
crowds. Australia is doomed for Asiatic con-
quest as surely as that conquest is already
planned ; unless—and it is the smallest hope
—unless she can so increase and so arm her
population as to daunt an invader whom she
will never be able to defy. We have ten
years before Doomsday : we may have fifty
years, a hundred : the day may be postponed,
it cannot be averted.
“ Japan is a poor land, with many people ;
Australia is a rich land, with few people;
therefore ! ” The conclusion is obvious
to the meanest Japanese understanding.
Australia has other things to think about,
more important; whether Codlin is really
the friend, or Short'? whether overtime pay
should be one-and-six, or one-and-nine ?
whether Brown should be included iix the
next English team’? or Jones ? or perchance
even Robinson ?
And China —“ a nation of sheep ” —the
epithet is decisive. But is it ? Sheep are
the tamest of creatures—true—and therefore
the easiest to be led ; the most timid—yes—
and have you ever seen a ram in combat ?
This at least is certain : an immense sleeping
power is an immense sleeping ' menace.
Conceive the Chinese united by one blazing
sentiment, by one enthralling cry—think of
them frenzied under a frenzied leader,
rushing “ On for the gold of Australia ! ” as
the Normans rushed “On for the gold of
Ely ! ” : who would oppose them ?
The Labour Party, natui’ally.
It is inspiring to think of the march of
China being arrested, being stayed, being
turned back by a Worker leading article.
Yet sheep have obstinate attributes. Within
the last year the Chinese have exhibited an
awkward unanimity, an uncomfortable per-
sistence, in boycotting American manufac-
tures. And for what cause ? For a mere
sentiment, a national sentiment, a senti-
mental objection to the American treatment
of Chinese. Suppose—it is only a supposi-
tion, of course—suppose a Chinese invader
refusing to be persuaded by the Worker
leading article—suppose ! What then ?
Make no mistake : one would not decry
the efforts of the Labour Party to gain for
the labourer the fullest reward, under the
best conditions. But can the Labour Party
make no other effort ? The single aim that
is a Party merit may become, has already
become, a national danger.
i There is a bigger word than Labour—and
that is People. There is a greater word
than Party—and that is Country.
To Arms ! To Arms ! A rifle for every
man, and every man to a rifle. The crisis is
Now—while there is time—before the last
sands have run down the ominous glass.
Not years hence, when the enemy is
at the gates of the North ; not even
to-morrow, but to-day. Is Deakin only a
talker? Reid only a self-seeker ? Watson only
a decent mediocrity ? If not, if they have
one scrap of imagination, if they can for one
moment shudder at the possibility of their
grand-children sold as Asiatic helots, spitting
on the graves of those who Could and Did
Not, then they will not rest easily in their
salaries till this country has a Navy and
Army of Defence, with every provision to
maintain them. These men are appointed
leaders ; one looks to them to lead. If they
are found wanting, others must be found
willing. That’s your affair who read : one
can but write.
Waste lands must be populated; hearts
and arms must be brought in for defence ;
at whatever cost of individual competition,
at whatever risk of tarnishing the gilded
banners of privileged Labour. Men in
droves and battalions m/ust be brought here
and given homes to defend and weapons for
that defence.
All unawares, Australia is even now at
war with Asia. To wait for overt aggression is
suicidal. We have subsisted garrulously Ion"
enough : it is up to us to fight for existence!
GIRLS
I.
GIRLS are of two sorts : those that you can
break in, and those that break you. I he
habits of the annual are exactly the same
under all conditions, hence it is usually
handled with very little trouble. I'roni six to
sixty is about the range of operation : after that
it gets too set m its ways. Two springs move ail
alike, jealousy and pride; if you can t get them
with the one you get them with the other. No
doubt there are some fair take-downs knocking
about; still, I think I have held my own pretty well.
Just like breaking in colts, you have to study
each one’s disposition, and handle accordingly.
They all take very keen watching, having numerous
little tricks of which the novice would never
dream. You will see a pair of them signalling
with handkerchiefs or toes to each other, just
when you would swear they were absorbed m
y o u. Ah, it takes me to know them ! \ ou can
always separate two mates by praising the one s
good looks to the other; this is a method that
never fails. The only trouble is first getting to
know them ; once you can get a bit confidential,
they ’re safe. It takes a little practice to know
when to hang back, and when to be “ bold "’; but
you soon learn.
I started when I was nine; had a red-headed
cousin to begin on. Round a knobby stick suited
her best. I got her handy as a kelpie after a
while; she used to keep watch whilst I raided
Chows’ carts for fruit. We used to watch “John”
into a house and then tie a jumping-jack to his
horse’s tail, and light it. There were always
some unbroken cases left after the upset, and slie
used to help me carry the pick away before J ohn
arrived to claim his property, bile was very
strong in the arms ; that's why I had her. Her
parents moved to another suburb after six months ;
said I had demoralised her. Some people have no
gratitude.
My parents, at the urgent request of the
neighbours, also moved to a fresh suburb.
Here was new ground, and 1 made the most of it.
I had a team of six the first week we got there.
Girls always rush a new-comer. It took all my
finesse to keep them apart; still, I managed it for
a few months. Then a rival betrayed me, and I
had to be quiet for a bit.
I am very fond of fruit. Next to our place was
a fine orchard; the owner was surly, ditto the
wife; the only child, a daughter, was worse. A
perfect bull-dog in face and nature, she was
shunned by all the local youth ; I cultivated her
from ulterior motives. Thyrza was her name;
locally this was corrupted into “Towzer,” as
being more appropriate, tihe was very religious,
so I won her affections by letting her catch me at
their fruit, and then, upon her exhortation,
“getting converted.” When she was away from
home, the only way I could get dessert was with a
butterfly-net on a fishing-rod from the fence;
their retriever was too savage.
They sold the orchard for building lots in about
twelve months, and then Towzer became my bane.
She used to follow me about (of course I had dis-
owned her as soon as the orchard went) till I felt
life a burden. Standing yarning about girls with
my mates Saturday night in the main street, com-
paring points, and quite happy, suddenly one
would say, “ Here conies old Towzer!”—a warn-
ing I did n’t dare disregard. If I were battling up
to a new girl, carefully marked down as a willing
prey, and had just broken the ice with a plaintive
“Gan I come?” I would be sure to sight Towzer
fazing stonily at me from the kerb, and get
urried, and let the girl escape. If I were just
lifting one on to my knee in the park any evening,
with a view to kissing her, just at the psycho-
logical moment I would be sure to hear Towzer’s
flip-flop on the asphalt, drawing up to us like a
slowhound. Then I would bite the girl on the
ear or neck in my excitement, and lose her for
ever. I lost a stone weight in a month ; became
moody and desperate. Twice I lured Towzer out
to Cook’s River; once to lose her. That failing
(she got home before I did), I tried a second time,
with a view of accidentally pushing her in. A
passing boat, however, rescued her when going
under for the third time, so I went home dejected.
I continued to lose weight till I felt it was time
something was done, so consulted my mate,
Jimmy Macpherson. Jimmy is so Scotch that lie
swears in Gaelic, and knows every girl in the
suburbs.
He thought a while.
“Can ye no get rid of her?” he queried at last.
“Man, ye dinna goo th’ richt wye aboot it.
Coorn intae th’ gairden.”
After whisky and girls, Jimmy’s hobby was
vegetables. He had that year won the champion
Erize at the Sydney show for onions, which
e had carefully shelved in his tool-house, his
mother considering them too powerful for indoors.
He pressed four of them on me, his eyes almost
weeping as he spoke.
“ Whin ye meet Tooser agine, be weel prepared.
Tell her ye have fund graaee, an’ wesh ta mak’ it
oop. Mak’ an apintment wi’ her. Beforr ye call
just eat twa o’yon darlins, raw ye ken, an’ hae
three gude sups ae whisky affter. Goom tae
grups affter tha firrst greetin’; clasp herr roon th’
neck wi’ both arms, an’ gie herr th’ fu’ o’ yer brith
in herr nostrils. If she stan’s yon, I gie her oop! ”
I obeyed Jimmy’s instructions to the letter.
After the first couple of whiffs, Towzer began to
struggle to get loose. I clung to her iirmly,
whistling out protests of reformation .and onion,
like a colt fresh caught in the yard. Aft each whiff
she reared and kicked, vainly trying! to free her-
self. I only held the firmer. In a voice thick
with emotion and fusel oil, I besought her to take
me again to her affections, but she only answered
by bites. My breath was failing, when with a
chance snap she caught my ear. I let go with the
pain of it. She wrenched free, and next moment
I was alone.
I met her full next day, but she passed me with
averted nose. I breathed again (though very
carefully with friends for a few days till it wore
off). Towzer ever afterwards described me as
No gentleman 1 ” The idea !
FALDE[?]
MEMORANDA
THE text of the second column will be found
in the preface to the third edition of Asia
and Europe, by Meredith Townsend (Lond.
Constable; ss.net). (This is one of the im
dispensable books of the last decade, because it is
fresh and seminal,—collecting the ideas of an
imaginative mind, a generalising mind, a phil 0 .
sopliic mind, regarding the “inherent differ-
ences” between Europe and A and their pro-
bable influence upon future his*
There is no doubt that Japan ■ >1 gradually develop
great ambitions, as all other victorious States have done
but much of her ambition will b- sated by the Protec!
torate of China, which no one ca oep from her, and by
the conquest and settlement of the great system of islands
which stretches down from Nagasaki to Australia. It has
been repeatedly asserted that, seeing her a' solute neces-
sity for territorial expansion and the vastntss and weak-
ness of Australia, the statesmen of Japan jvill, as time
advances, make some great effort to conquer and occupy
the island continent of the Southern Pacific. If Australia
were left to itself, this, judging hy the usual course of his-
toric events, would become the permanent policy of the
new Power. It would possibly also be a successful policy,
for the Australians, with a want of prudence which is almost
inexplicable, have discouraged European immigration;
they do not themselves multiply rapidly; and they are scat-
tered around the fringe of a vast continent full of harbours.
They are, compared with the Japanese, a feeble folk, not
one-tenth their number. Although they are brave, they
have not been trained to war, they have as yet organized
no great armies, they object to waste money on the
huge arsenals which could provide a whole nation with
scientific weapons, and if attacked, as the Japanese would
attack them, their States being invaded one by
one, they would probably in three years be hopelessly
crushed and driven into the untenable interior.
Townsend adds:
No such catastrophe is, however, within calculable dig-
tance, unless indeed Australia quarrels with Great Britain.
The British fleet is still irresistible ; it would perish before
Australia should be touched ; and neither Japan nor China
can hope to do what a coalition of the older maritime
powers shrinks from doing. .v..
But with this addition one cannot agree. The
British fleet may be “irresistible in British w&Vs,”
but we know very well that British shores ca-Aot
be. and never will be, robbed of their defences for
the protection, of Australia. The British fleet
might “perish before Australia should be
touched,” if the British fleet were within striking
distance. But a hundred thousand Japanese could
easily be landed in Australia long before the
British fleet had finished preparing for the voyage.
J*
Political Caricatures for 1905, by F. Carruthers
Gould (Lond., Arnold ; 6s. net.) collects from the West-
minster Gazette a year’s pictures hy the most effective
Eng. cartoonist of to-day’s events. The subjects are
taken chiefly from English politics, and their interest to
Australians is necessarily limited ; but the work shows
no diminution of the designer’s fertile fancy, no loss of his
faculty of telling expression.
Jack Holliday, Stockman, a story of Australian
bush life, is written by “ Giles Seagram ” (H. J. Driscoll),
and published in a second edition (3 S. 6d.) by Vardon and
Pritchard, Adelaide. Upon a slight thread of narrative
the little book collects bright and characteristic facets of
station life on the Lachlan in “Jhe old colonial days.”
The author has some literary faculty, and has lived the
life he describes ; and “old hands” will find his reminis-
cences seducing. There are several photographic illus-
trations of scenery, including one of Burke’s grave. We
find one of the characters (a contemporary of A. L.
Gordon) saying that Gordon was “ the poet of the
Government House and the squatter,” and “ had never
grasped the true inwardness of baokblock life from the
bushman’s point of view.”
“ Lectures teach people how to forget,” recently
declared Oanon Lyttelton, headmaster of Eton. “ I have
gained nothing whatever from any lecture I have ever
heard, excepting an increased power of forgetting ; the
only person who would appear to derive benefit is the
lecturer. I have never given a lecture from which I have
not learned a great deal myself. It is clear to me that
the system of giving lectures receives its entire support
and maintenance from the fact of the benefit it confers on
the lecturer.”
G. B. Shaw’s Cashel Byron’s Profession has
been dramatised; and pugilist Oorbett is to star the
heroic part in New York. The conjunction of intellect
and arms should make an attractive play for this coun-
try. Why don’t Shaw’s dramas reach the Australian
stage ?
George Moore, formally. abandoning Ireland
and returning to despicable London, adds insult to the
tremendous injury. “ But what about the Irish literary
revival ?” he was asked. “ There is n’t any,’' said George,
gloomily. “There ’s only one man, J. M. Synge, and he
has written only one play.” “And W. B. Yeats?”
demands a satirical Saxon.
That one’s own dispraise is a different thing
from the other fellow’s, Holman Hunt shows in an anec-
dote of Millais in his new book on Pre-Raphaelitism..
Hunt'and Millais went together to the Academy to see
the latter’s Carpenter's Shop. Hunt thought it ex-
quisitely painted, but Millais exclaimed, “ It’s the most
beastly thing I ever saw ! ” Just then two fellow-students
pushed between them, stared at the picture and then
turned and laughed in their faces. Before they moved
another step, Millais had advanced, and putting his hand
on the shoulder of the least imbecile, said to him, “ Do
you know what you are doing ? Don’t you see that if you
were to live to the age of Methuselah, both of you, and
you were to improve every day of your life more than you
will in the whole course of it, you would never be able to
achieve any work to compare with that picture? Egre-
gious fools ! ”
Anyone interested in the harp and its history
should procure The Stonj of The Harp, by Grattan Flood
(Lond., Walter Scott; 3s. 6 d. net)—a model handbook.
The writer gives a complete historical account of the
instrument, and references to all. leading performers of
\yh#m there is record. The book is embellished with
pictorial and musical illustrations ; the mechanical side
is treated ; and there are a bibliography and index. One
could not have a better literary introduction.
Stepniak’s book on The Russian Peasantry, the
author’s chief work, and probably the standard book in
English on the subject, is republished in a popular edition
by Routledge, London. People who are watching the
Russian revolution will find here an authoritative state-
ment of the foundations of critical inquiry.
A L<pnd. Times (weekly) reviewer (Sept. 22, p.
298) writes: “ All Christian dogma and doctrine is, he
holds, not only the result of development, but something
inconsistent with its essence.” If this sentence were gram-
matically right it would exemplify the use of a verb sin-
gular with two nominatives joined by and ; but one holds
it grammatically wrong. The clear distinction between
the ideas of dogma and doctrine requires a verb plural.
A.G.S.

jj good Report from Qamperdoiun
THE CASE
OF
MR. E. J. SWORDS.
(BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
In the course of an interview which our repre-
sentative had with Mr. Swords, of No. 150 Mis-
senden-road, Camperdown, that gentleman said :
“Yes, I can tell you all that is of importance in
connection with my illness when I was at Trangie.”
“ How long were you residing there ? ”
“ Three years. It was due to the great heat
and the sed ntary nature of my employment (a
saddler) that knocked me up. When I got up in
,the morning I was languid, and did not know how
il should get through the day. I could get little
,or no sleep at night. My internal disorders caused
Such a bitter taste to come to my mouth that on
arising I often felt inclined to retch, but I couldn’t,
and jl was never prepared to sit down and eat a
breakfast, like a man should do. As soon as I sat
,to the, table 'and the food came in sight, a sickly
sensation came to'my stomach, and I would have
,to get up and leave everything untouched; but
datfjr in the day I was generally more successful in
, eating a little—very little, because my appetite
was we.ak—but it didn’t make any difference, as
,my digestion was gone altogether.”
“What were your feelings like when you had
, ?”
“Dreadful. I can hardly describe them. My
.chest had such a sensation' in it that I don’t know
v to say ; ,1 know I felt 1 that if I turned round
.quickly it would break in,two, and the way I
.sometimes had to gafjp for breath would have
.made you think I yvas asthmatieal. Then I also
suffered from cramps in the stomach, caused, no
doubt, by wind that gathered there from decayed
food, and if I had to go through the sarbe again I
would much rather die. Many a time I actually
sang out with pain, which also used to find its
way down my right side, and almost cripple me.
Heaven knows, I was a martyr.”
“How long were you ailing like this ? ”
“For fully twelve months, and you had better
believe that during that time I did not continue
suffering for the want of taking physic; no indeed.
I took a great many bottles of medicines, only, I’m
sorry to say, to find my money had gone for no-
thing, in some instances to be made worse than I
was before. There was one lot of advertised pills
I took which made me feel that I was going
to die. I felt so ill, and, after them, thank
Heaven, I procured some Clements’ Tonic. Ah \
How I .soon wished I had taken that remedy
before. What a year of torture it would have
saved me ; but it is no use regretting. I’m lucky
to have got off like I did, and there is no doubt
that Clements’ Tonic is entitled to all the credit,
for that was the only remedy that could do me
any good. Why, if you could form an idea of the
intensity of the headaches from which I regularly
suffered, you would say that nothing but a miracle
could have cured them ; and if that’s so, then
Clements’ Tonic must be a miraculous medicine,
for it cured mine. Besides this, it also put an
end to the giddy turns I used to have. Upon my
word, at one time I could not stoop down without
feeling as if I would surely fall over, and I have
often been sitting on a chair, and expecting to
come a cropper every second, owing to the same
disagreeable sensation. My sight had been very
dim for a good while, my eyes feeling swollen,
and as if they had a scum over them ; my tongue
used to be white with fur, too; yet Clements’
Tonic set all these defects to rights, even when
all other things had been put aside as useless.”
“ Did Clements’ Tonic cure you thoroughly ?”
My cure couldn’t have been more complete,
and I trust my experience may.: be an objeot-
lesson to all who require treatment. ■ In any case,
you may publish this narrative in any form yo.u
desire.” •> ■ 1 ■ \ ■ u,. , < (
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, EDWARD JOHN SWORDS, of No. 150
Missenden-road, Camperdown, in thfe State! of
New South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements’ Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in anyway my
statements —which I give voluntarily without re-
ceiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the Provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) EDWARD J. SWORDS.
Subscribed and declared at Camperdown this
fifth day of September, one thousand nine
hundred and four, before me,
E. BARNES, J.P.

poorness of blood and neuralgia. Beware of spurious imitations, and see the wrapper
is unbroken.

THE...
FAMOUS
“MIMOSA” ESTATE
CLOSE TO FOUR RAILWAY STATIONS.
42,720 Acres Torrens Title 42,720 Acres
FOR SUBDIVISIONAL SALE AT TEMORA, N.S.W., ON
Wednesday, February 28, 1906,
AT 11 A.M.,
In FREEHOLD BLOCKS, 320 to 1000 ACRES, and HOMESTEAD BLOCK, comprising larger Area.
This grand Estate, which has long been spoken of as one of the most suitable in N.S. W. to support a Farming
Community, is at last available for Settlement.
The Eastern Boundary is 9 miles from Temora Railway Station ; Northern, 10 miles from BeacOnsfield Railway
Station, on Temora-Barellan Railway ; South-Eastern, 10 miles from Marrar Railway Station, Junee-Narrandera
Railway ; South-Western, 10 miles from Coolanion Railway Station, on same line. All good roads.
The COUNTRY is flat, or slightly undulating, lightly timbered with Box and Pine, RINGBARKED AND DEAD
FOR MANY YEARS, with numerous Currajong Trees left for shade. The soil is a strong red loam, very responsive
to rain, and the moisture is conserved by a rich clay subsoil. All first-class Wheat Land.
The ample WATER SUPPLY consists of numerous large Tanks, and the Subdivisions have been made to distri-
bute the supply as fairly as possible. Splendid catchments everywhere.
Land-seekers are invited to send for full particulars and lithos., which will be immediately sent on application.
Thev will be met at Temora and shown over the Estate. A thorough inspection courted.
SPECIAL FEATURES OF THIS SUBDIVISION ARE
The TEMORA DISTRICT is destined to be one of the principal granaries of N.S.W., and has a great future.
The Land for sale has been officially reported on as “ Eminently suitable for closer settlement.”
There are no residential conditions or other irksome restrictions, and no inquisitorial formalities.
The responsive nature of the soil makes the average annual rainfall— about 19 inches—ample to ensure successful
crops.
yery lightly timbered, and liavimg been dead so long is inexpensive to clear. It is estimated that the whole area
could be made ready for the plough at an average of 6s. per acre.
Purchasers will be able to put in crops this season, thereby getting a return for the first vear. A large lightly
timbered area can be put under crop without clearing straight away, and the clearing done gradually.
The immense advantage to agriculturists of the timber having been dead so long can hardly be over-estimated.
Prosperous settlers all around, few of whom started with anything like the advantages now offered.

have been instructed by Messrs. BUCKNELL and MARSDEN to conduct at TEMORA by Public Auction the above

The Auctioneers urge all people seeking Land for farming to avail of the opportunity, as it is surely the best
available for agriculture.
It is the particular wish of the Vendors that the property is to be in no way over-described, so that distant
buyers can inspect with every confidence.

V. J. Daley’s “At Dawn and Dusk,” a
collection of his best verses ; bound in cloth, with

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Spiritual Bursts.
My bursts of soulfulness sublime
They do me so much good ;
I loathe all sin
Whilst I am in
An anti-mundane mood.
Mere carnal pleasures, for a time,
[ scorn as things accurst;
The grossness of my fellow men
Seems truly pitiable when
My soul gets on the burst.
I sternly chide my normal self,
I say to Me—•“ How mean,
How weak, how base,
How commonplace
Thy thoughts and deeds have been
A Vessel thou of cheapest delf ! ”
(Perchance my readers, vers’d
In rhymesters’ tricks, will tell me why
The bard resorts to “ thou ” and “thy
Whose soul is on the burst).
I put away the ribald joke,
I take but little moat,
I early rise
With smiling e; cs
The early sun to greet.
One pipe a day I calmly smoke
(Not ten, or twelve, as erst).
On clouds of faith and hope and love
Pure thoughts are borne to realms above.
My soul is on the burst.
Too soon the mood of grace must p ss
(Lord, let it linger long),
And I will be
The same old Me
Amidst the giddy throng.
Those clouds of holiness, \; s s !
Will short’y be dispers’d.
The wicked world will never know
My soul was lately white, as snow,
And went upon the burst.
Still, in its sober, sordid stars
My spirit will retain
Remembrance faint
Of what I ain’t,
But may be soon again.
When one has quaLt
The mystic draught
That slakes a secret thirst,
No fleshly forces can control
The vagrant yearnings of his soul
To go upon the burst.
VIC.
EDMUND FISHER.
Love’s Night.
Superbly in the midnight sky
The full moon floats.
Pan’s pipes are skirling clear and high
Their golden notes.
Are you content with love and me.
Here by the sombre, murmuring sea ?
Night is brief !
We’d have it endless, you and I.
Our hearts throb on in. tune and cry,
“ Halt ! Time, old thief.”
The times, sweetheart, are out of joint. . .
Let us be gay,
And dare the devil point for poii t,
While yet we may !
Life is at best so slight a thing,
And youth and joy are swift a-wing.
These fleeting years
That bring us pain and strife enough,
Leave us so little time for love,
No time for tears !
Night holds, as each glad moment flies,
Just you and me.
Zephyr goes whispering by, and sighs
Deliciously.
The pallid stars that blink and jeer
Are jealous of your eyes, my dear.
Beneath this moon,
Safe and remote from day’s alarms,
I’ve one regret in your dear arms . . .
Day comes so soon !
Day’s crowding tumult, clamor, glare
We’d hold at bay,
As Love held back the morning where
Alcmena lay.
Ah ! could these scanty minutes be
Extended to eternity !
Night is so brief I
We’d have it endless, you and I!
Our hearts throb on in time, and cry ,
“ Halt! Time, old thief !”
tas.
FRANK MORTON.
Wine, Woman, and Song.
The Austral poet, when he sings
Of little loves and amorous things,
Must needs devote each second line
To some loud eulogy of wine.
’Tis not enough to have the fair
Magnificence of eyes and hair,
With suavest curves where dimples curl,
A mouth of coral and of pearl;
The bard with all at his command
Must clutch a bottle in his hand,
And rave his ardor over-much
With courage that was erstwhile Dutch.
Is not our native girl divine ?
Have her full lips no ruddy wine
To stir his sluggard soul to bliss,
And make him drunken with a kiss?
Is Venus helpless to beguile
With warm, red blush and witching smile,
Tf swinish Bacchus there dispense
No drug to dull the sweeter sense ?
I hold the bard a sorry scamp
Whose light of love’s a spirit-lamp,
Who Cupid loads with measures odd,
And makes a potboy of a god.
VIC.
EDYSON.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 1, 1906.
THE YELLOW THREAT.
There are many reasons for fearing that it is true
that America intends to sell the Philippines to
Japan. Never at any time has there been pro-
mise of much satisfaction to the American people
from the possession of those islands. They came
unasked for, as a sort of damnosa hereditas of the
Spanish war ; they have been held at the price of
an expensive and cruel guerilla struggle, which
has angered the American pride and shocked the
American conscience ; and even at the present
time the Power in possession cannot boast of any-
thing like an effective hold of the group—the big
towns are American, over the rest the Filipino
rules, or keeps anarchy. Probably the United
States would warmly welcome a way of getting
rid of the Philippines without loss of prestige.
Only ideas of national pride, mixed up with per-
haps a little thought of duty towards the lawless
Filipinos, have prevented an evacuation long
since. If now Japan were to offer a price for the
group, the American Eagle would, most likely, for-
get to shriek about the hauling down of the Stars
and Stripes. It could be argued, with a good deal of
justice, that to pass the Philippines on to Japan
would be very different to abandoning them to
drift into a state of anarchy; and that Japan
would be better able to give the islanders
the sort of rule they want than the Americans (in
the United States there have been strenuous
objections, in some quarters, to the methods of tor-
ture and treachery which the Yankee soldiers have
adopted—had to adopt, as they put it —in
dealing with that curiously mixed savage the
Filipino). In truth, the American people would
be very willing to be persuaded that there was no
loss of dignity in the loss of the Philippines ; sell-
ing them suggests the same comfortable and
grateful feeling as getting an unexpected price for
a stolen horse with the seven deadly vices in good
working order. *' i
American diplomacy, likewise, probably recog-
nises that anyhow the Philippines are Japan’s
whenever Japan cares to take them. It is in-
conceivable that any Yankee Government
would propose to spend 100,000 American lives
and 1,000,000,000 American dollars in defending a
strin o- of Asiatic hornets’, nests against a Jap
invasion. To sell what is valueless, what is
not .you:s, and what you can’t keep anyway,
is the b st of good business, a id the
American, apart from his little weaknesses for
flag-flapping, spooks, new religions and food fads,
is essentially a keen business man. Nor is it to be
forgotten that the American Government shows a
marked tendency now to conciliate Japan. A
Japanese coolie invasion of the Pacific coast of
America, rendered possible by the fact that the
Geary Exclusion Act—passed some years ago,
• when the only emigrating Asiatic was the China-
man —does not in any way restrict such an influx,
has awakened the west of America to a sense of
danger, and the necessity of checking the flood of
yellow labor has been strongly impressed upon
Washington. But Washington has shown no
sympathy, has indeed evinced a desire to extend
the toleration of Asia so far as to modify the
exclusion laws in regard to the Chinese. The
plain fact of the matter seems to be that
Washington is frightened to face the prospect of
a war with Japan. Until the Panama Canal has
been cut, and the United States thus enabled to
quickly mobilise its fleet, the likely result of an
American-Japanese war would be the Japanese
occupation of Luzon and Honolulu, the destruc-
tion of the American Pacific fleet, the destruction
of the American Atlantic fleet, if it undertook the
journey round Cape Horn to the seat of war, and
the blockading of the Pacific coast until suc.i time
as the latent power of the American people could
assert itself.
* • * *
If the Panama Canal were to-day an accom-
plished fact, giving a short sea passage from one
American coast to the other, The Bulletin
believes that the United States attitude towards
the Yellow Peril would be vastly different. The
American learns from his local negro the lesson
that the white race cannot mix with the colored
man, and the feeling in the United States as to
the necessity of a Caucasian hegemony in world
politics is well-nigh instinctive. No one familiar
with the rigid, sometimes harsh and cruel, way in
which the “color line” is drawn in North
America can doubt but that pride of white race is
keen there; and the old gold-diggings anti-
Chinese riots, with their successor, the Geary
Chinese Exclusion Act, show that the Asiatic is
looked upon with just as much dislike as the
African. There might have been expected, there-
fore, during recent events, a strong anti-Japanese
feeling in America; that certainly would have
been consistent with the past history of the
United States, and logically of a piece with that
country’s present attitude towards non-Caucasian
peoples within its borders. But the current of
official sympathy was quite otherwise, and set
steadily in favor of Japan. Since the close of the
war, the United States has even met in concilia-
tory fashion the demands of China—backed up by
a trade boycott—for some relaxation of the laws
against Chinese immigration. All this impresses
the point that official America does not care for
the prospect of a war with the Asiatic just now.
That great new Power among the nations
fears that, without a canal making possible the
mobilisation of its navy, the task of encountering
Japan might be attended with early humiliation
and only with success after a bitter struggle.
A desire to do all that is possible to avoid such
a contingency might, therefore, very naturally
prompt a sale of the Philippines to Japan,
thus shaking off the chief cause of entanglement
in Asiatic affairs, and killing, too, the trade
boycott, which has its real origin, notin the desire
of China to secure more liberal treatment of
Chinese merchants travelling through America,
but in the sense of grievance over the stoppage of
Chinese coolie immigrants to the islands—a
stoppage which began with the American occupa-
tion.
* * * •
The relations of America with Asia affect
vitally Australia. The Yellow Peril marches a
big step nearer if the Philippines pass from
American to Japanese control. Whilst those
islands were under the rule of a white race there
was a fair prospect always that, when the Jap
Jingo looked around for another war, his eye
would light on the Philippines before it strayed
to Australia; or that in the event of an Asiatic
attack on Australia, the great Republic which
shares our speech and our democratic aspirations
would have a garrison handy to which an appeal
might be made. The withdrawal of the United
States from Asia would at once narrow Japan’s
choice of a possible enemy to one, and deprive
Australia of its chief hope, failing Britain, of
friendly interference.
* * • *
This prospect of the victorious Japan marching
down undisturbed as far as Manila should be
another spur to the careless, drifting Australian
community. When the threat is from Manila,
instead of Tokio, and the great American Power is
removed from the path of a possible Hunnic
invasion of the Commonwealth, the position
for the Australian people becomes more
serious, and adequate preparation becomes a more
pressing duty. There are two classes standing
in the way of that preparation—those careless and
unworthy citizens who refuse to take seriously any
of the responsibilities of citizenship, and those
pessimists who mutter that it is no good anyway
trying to arm against Asia, since if Japan cares to
come here as an enemy it can do so, and nothing
that we can do will avail anything. The first class
it is not worth while wasting patience to argue
with. Prone to the earth its mind is and will
remain; and it will gape at the street corners,
smoking vile cigarettes and discussing horse-
racing odds until the shells of an enemy shriek
overhead. The second class —a very big class—
calls for some more or less friendly abuse. Its
counsel of despair is just as disastrous, practically,
as the other man’s counsel of laziness. Nothing is
impossible until it is proved to be so. Australia
has a small but sturdy population comprising some
of the best fighting material in the world, it has
an enormous power of wealth-production, and,
within its own sea-walls, all the elements neces-
sary for defence —men, horses, coal, iron, copper,
and the constituents of modern explosives. Estab-
lish the factories to make weapons and ships and
locomotives; train all the manhood to the use of
the rifle; encourage a sailor and ship-building
population to guard the coast; have proper organi-
sation for a decade, and Australia could face with
cheerful and equal mind any foe, European or
Asiatic.
* * * *
But the organisation is necessary. The danger
hastens on with giant strides. With every year
comes a development to bring it nearer. The
presence of America’s outpost in the Philippines
gives a sense of comfort if not of actual guardian-
ship. If that is withdrawn Australia will be quite
solitary—the one white garrison facing the Asiatic
peoples. Now the Sibyl offers the last of her nine
books.
INDIVIDUALISM—AGGREGATED CAPI-
TAL—SOCIALISM.
The tempora.-y set-back to the State Labor party
in Westralla a little while ago was heralded by a
shriek of joy from the Tory pre.is, which has
declared that Austr ilian public opinion has swung
back to healthy Individualism. This continual
barking of the reactionary press on the subject of
Individualism is humorous. Qutside the political
columns the word is never mentioned, and the
very rags which use it most keep the largest paid
staff to vvrite up the doings and prospects of
aggregated capital—which is a very different
thing from Individualism. The use of the word is
totally misleading, and not only betrays the
ignorance of the papers that use'it, but throws
dust in the eyes of the people, and does much to
prevent a proper realisation of how transient our
present economic and industrial state is. How-
ever Australian papers are not alone in this
blunder or misrepresentation; even English
financial papers, which live absolutely on aggre-
gated capital, perpetrate dreary lies about how
Australian Democratic legislation is stifling
“Individual Enterprise.” Evidently Spruce
Myth is not alone on his pinnacle of ignorance.
* * * •
As a matter of sober history the throttling of
Individualism for which Australian Democrats
are so indignantly reprobated, commenced in
England about 300 years ago, when Eliza Tudor
sat on the throne of Bull-land, and issued charters
galore to joint-stock companies. Such examples
of Individualism which still survive are merely
the platypus and apteryx of economics; and most
of them are suckled by a joint-stock bank.
Almost all the big concerns of the English-speak-
ing world are joint-stock enterprises, and Indi-
vidualism, as far as that collection of races is con-
cerned, is as dead as Queen Anne. That being
so, papers which represent Australian democracy
as slaying a thing that was pretty dead the better
part of a century ago, are run by either fools or
prevaricators.
• • * *
There seems to be a notion among the opponents
of combined capital that the death of Individual-
ism was a bad thing, and that it would be well if
we could go back to the old simple state, when
each man traded only with his neighbor in the
village, and industrial organisation was unknown.
William Morris, the Kelmscott Socialist, was a
great man, but that mediaeval kink of his life was
an unfortunate one. The days of Individualism
were days of stagnation, poverty and brevity of
life. When Private Enterprise really did exist
“ Merrie England ” was a good place to be out of.
The bulk of the population was so hideously poor
that it wore leather next its skin, because it could
not afford shirts; even its wealthiest used rushes
and straw on the floor in lieu of carpets. The food
of the bulk of the population consisted of black
bread, peas, roots and weeds. Meat it ate when
it occasionally and surreptitiously slew the fe-
rocious rabbit or hare. Roads, excepting where the
track followed the ruin of some old Roman military
highway, did not exist. The country swarmed with
people who were partly beggars and mostly robbers;
and the nobility rode round in ironmongery
and helped themselves. In the cities, or alleged
cities, the conditions were too dreadful for ade-
quate description. When streets were paved it
was with cobble stones; the drainage was that of
the surface only; and the water-supply was a
thing of tanks and wells. The man who drank
well water usually went to heaven early in life,
for the wells were sunk amid graveyards and
worse.
• • • •
When Individualism began to die all that com-
menced to change. In the reign of Henry VII.
spasmodic attempts were made at forming com-
panies, and in the reign of that eminently-married
person Henry VIII. quite a number did come into
existence, mostly for the purpose of fitting out ships
to raid and trade. They were formed by the whole
population of some seaport combining to build
and equip a ship or two. Many of the share-
holders were artificers, who gave their labor in the
building of the craft as their contribution towards
the capital of the concern, and afterwards formed
portions of the crew. But these were all
private companies which did not issue
stock. Strange to say, the proposal to
allow men who worked under discipline to
hold shares came in for bitter opposition.
It was condemned from the pulpit as a wicked
innovation which threatened to break down the
barriers that the Almighty had set up between
gentlemen and plain men, and anything that made
Jack as good as his master was calculated to
bring down the wrath of Heaven on both Jack
and the other fellow’. Then came the smashing of
the Spanish Armada, and a swarm of, for the
time, huge joint-stock companies were formed to
seize the empire of the seas that was understood
to be slipping from the nerveless grasp of the
Spaniard. The first of these appeared in 1553; it
was formed to trade with Russia, and got the
capital by selling its shares in the open market.
It was followed by other corporations, chief
among which were the East India Trading Co.
(which afterwards practically conquered British
India), the Virginia Co., the West India Co., the
New England Co., the London, and the Plymouth
Companies, all joint-stock companies pure and
simple. Modern England began with the forma-
tion of them ; and Individualism, as a pre-
dominant system, or rather lack of system, began
to disappear, giving way to a period of aggre-
gated capital.
* • * *
At the end of the 18th century England found
itself flooded with cheap capital looted from the
treasury of Bengal after the battle of Plassey.
This money was scattered around, and a burst of
company-promoting followed. Companies were
formed to cut canals, to build factories, to exploit
old patents which had failed because there had
previously been insufficient capital to develop
them, and to buy up new ones. The inventor soon
appeared when there was a market for him, and
in 10 years the spinners and weavers of England
increased from 8000 to 352,000. The fact that the
employees were not also shareholders was respon-
sible for the most hideous chapter of English his-
tory. Aggregated capital, untrammelled, irre-
sponsible, was ruthless in its oppression of a help-
less class of toilers. The machines of the joint-
stock companies ground out heaps of wealth and
heaps of corpses, most of them being those of
children—mere babies. Nevertheless, from the
scientific and economic point of view, the move-
ment was upward.
• • * *
At the present moment, Aggregated Capital has,
under the fostering influence of High Protection,
reached its highest development in the United
States of America; and how great a nation the
combination has made let statistics prove:—lt
was not till 1870 that the United States commenced
to go in for the joint-stock company in deadly
eanu st. By means of ironclad tariffs the work of
supplying the nation’s needs was reserved for its
own population, and the population had to hustle
to do it. It hustled to considerable purpose. By
1900, a period of 30 years, the country had doubled
its population, increased its railway tonnage by
200 per cent., multiplied its interlake traffic by
31, its cotton grown for home manufacture by
three, its coal production by six, its petroleum
output by 11, and its steel by 153. Its total
exports increased by 256 per cent. ; but the most
significant fact was that its exports of manufac-
tured goods advanced 535 per cent., while imports
of all classes decreased in proportion to popula-
tion. According to the amazing logic of the Sydney
Telegraph, this decrease of imports should have
shown that the U.S.A. had no foreign investments
bringing in money, and was going to the dogs gene-
rally. But, in spite of that alleged expert, the
U.S.A. was apparently prospering, as the money
in circulation increased twice as fast as the popu-
lation, and the wealth of the country trebled, and
so did the deposits in the Savings Banks. The
gold and silver output doubled, pig-iron produc-
tion increased 13-times, copper 22-times. In 1900
the deposits in the Savings Banks amounted to
£600,000,000, which was an increase of 500 per
cent, in the 30 years. In England (which had
some centuries start and all the benefits of Free-
trade since the first half of the century), the
increase in the same class of deposits for the last
sixty years was only 300 per cent.
* * * «
The telephone, the railroad, the steamship, the
improved agricultural machinery which increase
the world’s wealth by increasing the output, are
all the result of a small section of the population
combining small amounts of capital to set an
industry going on a scale in which it can play for
big stakes. If the capital and brains of the whole
community were similarly capitalised, how would
the world change ? When we have found out how
to do that we shall have evolved Socialism.
• • * •
To-day, under the regime of Aggregated Capital
a schoolboy is better fed, better dressed, and
travels to and from school in greater comfort and
with several times more expedition than a king of
the Norman period travelled to take possession of
his crown. And yet we are only on the threshold
of economic development, and our wealth-
producing machinery is owned by quite a small
percentage of the people. Probably under a
system of State Or municipal socialism, which is
already beginning to arrive as aggregate capital
began to arrive in the days of Queen Bess, the
ordinary man will live in as much wealth and
splendor as Ned VII. and Kaiser Bill do at the
present time. The chief change will be that under
a partial, anarchic system of combined capital,
dotted over with dead remnants of Individualism,
we make everything to sell. Under the Socialistic
system we will make everything to benefit the
community. The result wili be to abolish poverty,
diminish human suffering, eliminate disease and
prolong life.
• • » »
The present is not a system of Individualism —
this is the age of Aggregated Capital, and the
system that is to reign m its stead is already
pushing its nose through the curtain. We live in
a period of change, and exceedingly rapid change
at that. Already the new system begins to
arrive, and all the vituperation of the Tory press
and the reactionary tendencies of political scare-
crows like Carbuthers cannot hold it back. It
will roll over them like a Juggernaut.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N S. Wales Railway Scandal.
As most people expected, the three men who
inquired into the N.S.W. railway coal contract
find that they cannot agree. The remarkable
tiling, in view of their attitude during the inquiry,
is that they get even as close together in the end
as they managed to do. J edge Rogers and one
colleague “ cannot help thinking ” that the Rail-
way Commissioners should not have established a
private coal monopoly in the Western district
(to which they paid for coal an enormously higher
price than the private consumer did); they almost
apologise for expressing the view that it would
The Garden in the South.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a garden in the South,
Sown carelessly with scanty seeds,
And overgrown with worthless weeds,
And soiled and spoiled with dust and drouth.
The owners, lost to hope and fears,
Rest easefully, and smugly say:
“ Sufficient unto us the day ;
Why should we care for future years ? ”
“Enough,” they cry, “if sun and soil
Yield generous measure while we live ;
Enough, if this fair garden give
Us wheat and wine and corn and oil.
“ Why should we, toiling, think about
The years to come, and till the ground,
And hedge our fertile garden round
With thorns, to keep the spoilers out ? ”
The worm may pierce the timber green,
The choking weeds grow thick and fast—
They say, “When we are with the Past
Our sons shall make the garden clean.”
Their sons— God grant when many graves
Grow green above the garden-folk,
Their sons may know no foreign yoke,
Usurped, cast down, thrust out, and slaves !
For, whoso knows that garden-land—
Far-famed, wide-acred, fair, immense
He knows that by its northern fence
A swarthy crew of spoilers stand.
Slant-eyed, thin-lipped, they hold in thrall
Their eager hands that strain and strive
Till that red moment shall arrive
That gives them lordship over all.
Then they shall climb the Judgment seat,
And trumpet forth a twofold claim
To all the country bowed in shame,
The garden-country at their feet.
First, they shall hold by right of sword—
That ancient and age-sanctioned right,
That first of laws, the law of might—
The land as theirs, and they its lord.
Then they shall say, “ Ye do but lose
What ye have made a fruitless field ;
To have and use not is to yield
To those who yearn to have and use.”
That garden in the south is starred
For good or ill, and we were sent
To make or mar a continent,
To lose like caitiffs, or like kings to guard.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.


have been a good thing for the railways, as by far
the largest consumer of co il, to have kept alive
some opposition to the Li hgow Coal Trust. The
other member of the commission, Mr. E. W. Knox,
•icf- mes k° same conclusion without any apologies.
More than that, he finds that the tests upon which
the competing collieries’ tenders were set aside
were unscientific and valueless, and that the action
of the two junior Commissioners in making a four-
years’ contract with the combine in the teeth of
the chairman s protest—in the end disre-
garding him altogether—was “in every respect
improper.’’ The matter of the tests involves, of
course, a grave reflection upon the professional
officers of the department, who carry a very
heavy responsibility in the whole matter; for
it was due to their representations that
Commissioner Kirkcaldie changed his views
and made the objectionable contract possible.
But even if they had a mind to do it, the Com-
missioners could not shield themselves behind
their expert advisers. If the expert is to be abso-
lutely supreme in his department, the Commis-
sioners are useless. Their business is to consider
one expert’s advice in the light of the advice of all
their other experts and in he light of the experi-
ence and commonsense which they are them-
selves supposed to have. And there they failed.
Whether they are in the habit of pursuing this easy
course of blindly doing what their subordinates tell
them there is no means of knowing ; if they are,
then the country could clearly get along just as
well _ without them. How much further the
inquiry will proceed it is impossible to tell. Mr.
Knox has resigned from the Commission of
Inquiry; and, from the other Commissioners,
judging by their attitude so far, the public will
have little hope of any disclosures that are likely
to lead to radical results. Apart from the impor-
tant though minor point raised by Mr. Knox
as to the quality of the professional services ren-
dered to the department, two questions remain
to be answered, and they will have to be answered
sooner or later —one particular, the other general.
The first is as to these coal supplies. As by far
the largest consumer of coal in the State, why
should the State not produce its own coal ? What
would be said if it hired its locomotives or its
trucks, instead of having its own ? The coal-
mine owner, taught perhaps by his employees,
has a large capacity for combination; and the
Railways cannot be sure of putting themselves
outside the power of any combination unless they
have their own mines. And the second question is
as to railway control. N.S.W. has adopted the
now largely-forsaken plan ot having three Com-
missioners. So long as it had one great
Commissioner and two lesser ones, that did not
matter much, because after all it practically had
only one Commissioner though it paid three
salaries. But later on, having three Com-
missioners to pay, it wondered whether it was
worth while having one big man—three medium-
sized men, it was thought, might do very well.
The result is seen here: they-have not been big
enough to set themselves against the aggressive
expert officers of special departments, and so a
contract has been made which two members of a
commission of inquiry condemn with apologies and
which one denounces without any apologies.

The British Elections.
There is no doubt now that a full measure of dis-
aster, heaped up, pressed down, and overflowing,
has been served out to the Balfouk-Chamberlain
party in Britain. The “official” Liberals have a
clear majority with their own numbers, indepen-
dent of Irish and Labor members; combined
with these —and the combination, for a while
at least, is certain—they overwhelmingly out-
number the Tories. For the life of one Par-
liament at any rate no more will be heard of
Mr. Balfour, and probably not much of Mr.
Chamberlain. The result surprises all the
prophets. Just before the elections the Outlook,
usually a very well-informed and sane paper,
pledged its reputation to the statement —“In no
circumstances can the Cabinet now obtain a
majority independent of the allied Irish and
Labor vote” ; and most other papers agreed. As
to the cause of the result, a present visitor to
Australia, a well-known publicist, throws an
illuminating light. It is his opinion, in brief,
that Chamberlain mulled the fight ; if he
had gone straight-out for Protection for the
British workman his path would have been
easier. But the appeal to “ Imperialism ”
failed. For one thing it was not easy for
the voter to see how Imperialism and Pro-
tection were necessarily connected. In the
second place the “advantage of drawing closer to
the colonies” couldn’t compete with the supposed
disadvantage of taxing food. But one typical
electorate, this publicist points out, where a
couple of years ago a Freetrader easily beat a Tariff
Reformer, and where, since, a steady propaganda
in favor of Protection had been carried on—a
propaganda in which “ Chamberlainism ” was
not allowed to obtrude—the Tariff Reformer
has won at the late elections. It is easy
to recognise the reasonableness of the view.
The British voter, appealed to in favor
of Protection on the ground chiefly that it
was “ to oblige the colonies ” and warned on the
other side that “Protection meant a dear loaf,”
would be inclined to give very little consideration
to the question, but to decide off-hand that any-
how it was little use worrying about “ the
colonies.” If he had been appealed to strictly in
his own interests, had had the fact brought home
to him that even if Protection meant a dearer loaf,
it also meant more money in the pocket, so that
the loaf could be bought, and butter withal, and
at the same time the benefit gained of a policy of
national insurance, the propaganda would
have been more convincing. It is useless
in politics to ask voters to change the
current of their ideas at the stimulus of a possible
benefit to somebody else: they want to have
assurance of advantage to themselves. But,
probably, now that the first futile attack has been
made against the citadel of Cobdenism, and it has
been shown that it is no sacred Ark, to touch
which is to perish, the Protection movemeut will
quickly re-organise for a new and wiser effort.
There is young blood in England; and it is
beginning to flow to the platform and to the
papers, and to wash away the obstructing tradi-
tions of a Chinese brand of Conservatism. One
may forecast for the Cobden cause in Britain
another ten years of life at the utmost.

Lashing the Weak Intellect.
Judge Docker, of N.S.W., who has been dropping
into hot water pretty constantly for the past 25
years, has just qualified for another dipping. At
the Parramatta Quarter Sessions he had before
him a sex-maniac, who, at 26, had already been
twice convicted of his disease. Said Docker :
“ It is evidently no use imprisoning you ; I shall
order you a whipping.” And he did—2s strokes
on top of two years of_ the gaol which his Honor
says is no good for this sort of prisoner. Some
horror having been expressed, Judge Flogger
proceeded to defend himself: “Evidently im-
prisonment alone had not deterred prisoner
from committing this kind of crime, as he had
been convicted on two similar charges. It
was not true that the man was a lunatic, though
he might be of weak intellect.” It is quite likely
that he is. And there is nothing like the lash for
curing a weak intellect. The weaker the intellect,
the more lash is of course necessary ; so when the
State gets hold of a very weak intellect—in its
hospitals for the insane—the proper course is a
whipping every morning before breakfast, or,
perhaps, in a very bad case, two whippings a day.
An inscrutable Providence bestows on one man
a weak head, on another a weak chest. We
build hospitals and homes for the one ; now, for
the other, to oblige Judge Docker, we build
triangles. By the way, does J udge Docker wear
glasses ? For weak eyes. The Bulletin doubts if
gaol would do much good. But what does his
Honor think of the lash as a cure ?

The New Land Boom.
The Bulletin remarked a couple of months ago
that all the elements of a land boom were floating
around, and that the chances were in favor of the
public being badly scratched if it didn’t beware.
Since then the floating elements seem to have
been taking definite form. The.last boom spent
itself in the building and small allotment craze.
But tho burnt child dreads the fire, and it would
be almost as easy to raise the dead as to raise
another boom around the big centres where the
old corpse is still partially unburied. The victims
of that boom were largely tradesmen and wage-
earners, and their savings were swept away in
the general madness engineered by bell-toppered
spielers. This time, grasping the chance offered
by the newspaper booming of agriculture, the
jobber has marked out the cocky and would-be
wheat and butter grower for his prey, and the
region of inflated land values is far enough away
from the old corpse to avoid the smell thereof.
Already land in Southern Victoria, and in some
parts of N.S. Wales, has jumped from 50 to 80
per cent, within the last few years, and the chief
reason of it is that little or no cash has passed—the
thing has been mostly done on paper. Men with
a few pounds buy slices of land, cheerfully sign the
pro. notes, and thank God with the air of a man
who has done good business and paid his way. The
time-payment jobber collects the deposit, calls
round for the early instalments, and smiles and
waits. The last two years have been good ones,
with high prices, and farmers have done well;
but the average farmer is afflicted with a strange
forgetfulness, and rushes into land buying without
remembering that there is a hereafter in which
low prices for produce and poor harvests are
almost inevitable. The end of those who buy
land at from £5 to £2O an acre 20 miles from a
market and hundreds of miles from the seaboard,
paying another 5 per cent, of the capital value of
the land for fertilisers to grow wheat; and who
have to compete in the world's market against the
cheap nigger and Asiatic and other people
who are nearer the big markets, is an easy
problem to work out. The introduction of
the complete Harvester to the big wheat centres
of S. America and Canada must also have an effect
on the world’s wheat prices ; and when this new
phase of foreign competition becomes felt, the
stench of another dead boom will be in Australia’s
nostrils. The whole truth is that Australia put
the plough in front of the horse. The cry “ Back
to the land ” had much to recommend it; but, in
the absence of a stiff land-tax, “ Back to the land ”
simply meant “Back to the land-jobber”; and a
movement which ought to have resulted in vast
good is likely to come to disaster.

Bent’s Lunatics.
The big public agitation got up in Melbourne
some years ago by a few medical men regarding
the barbarous treatment of lunatics in the Victorian
asylums, seemed to promise much good. But now,
after the lapse of years, things in the asylums are
quite as bad as ever. The buildings are hopelessly
cramped and out of date. Twenty years ago the
lunatics just filled the asylums. Since then the
number has grown at the rate of about 50 a year,
and the buildings have not been increased by one
square inch. Result, an awful overcrowding. At
Kew— the alleged model asylum—a large per cent-
age of the patients sleep on the floors in already
crowded wards and draughty corridors. Yarra
Bend has been condemned in toto. Beechwqrth
Asylum is antiquated and crowded. Ararat is a
grim prison, and it is crowded too. Sunbury is
being gradually improved, but the improvements
do not keep pace with the influx of lunatics.
Ballarat Asylum is fairly decent, but has far too
many patients. Yet the Government did accede
to the clamor of the reformers so far as to appoint
an expert Inspector-General at a big salary, and
promised to carry out his suggestions. Dr. Ernest
Jones, the Inspector-General, is a man chock-
ful of valuable ideas and up-to-date knowledge
regarding lunacy matters. But he doesn’t seem
to have any pluck or determination. As a conse-
quence, all his reports and suggestions have been
ignored, and Dr. Jones is content to sit tight
and let things slide. The old straight-jacket
and cold douche treatment which prevailed
in Victorian asylums until a couple of years
ago has been abolished, and in its place
the modern system of individual observation-
treatment is supposed to have been substi-
tuted. This is mere supposition, however, for
that method requires about three times as many
attendants as there are in the Victorian asylums,
and requires that they know their business
and have special training. The Victorian atten-
dants are untrained. It is admitted by all con-
nected with the lunacy business in Victoria that
nothing whatever is being done towards curing
the insane. They are herded in insanitary, ill-
built dens, and left to their own resources. One
warder (he can’t be called an attendant)
watches 40 or 50 lunatics, and generally
doesn’t even know what particular forms of
insanity they are suffering from. There are no
trained nurses to attend patients in illness and
virtually no safeguards against fire or escape of
patients. Lunatics walk out of both Kew and
Yarra Bend with such frequency that no one in
either asylum feels at all excited when a patient is
reported missing. Formal notice is sent to the
police and the rest is left to Providence. Premier
Bent has been approached time and again on the
subject, but he does nothing. His ciy for along
time was “No money 1” When he got a surolus
and was pri cteding to attract votes with it, another
attempt was made to have the State’s lunatics
at all events decently housed. But just in the
nick of time the property-holders round Kew
asked Mr. Bent to shift the asylum altogether,
and he hung up the whole idea of lunacy reform
until he had decided this question. No man in
Victoria is cleverer at finding excuses than Pre-
mier Bent, and it is clear that he doesn’t think
lunatics worth bothering about. They haven’t
gob votes.
Hospital Scandals.
As a preface— The Bulletin believes that the
majority of public hospitals, certainly of the
great hospitals, are conducted as well as is
humanly possible considering the “system.’
Doctors and nurses are usually kind and keen at
their work. That they become a little “hardened ’
to suffering is natural; they see so much of it that
their sensibilities are somewhat blunted. That,
however, need not interfere with their work—
indeed would in some respects help it, for medicine
is not a science which can lean to the maudlin and
hope to succeed. Buthospital scandals are painfully
frequent. Sydney has had two within the last few
days. A constable took to Sydney Hospital a young
woman who had been knocked down by a passing
cab. At 11.35 p.m. three resident doctors ex-
amined her, dressed her injuries, and, as she had
the money to pay for a cab, sent her home,
•where she arrived some time after midnight, very
ill and suffering severely. She was a domestic
servant and her employer wrote to the papers
complaining bitterly of her being treated in such
a “callous manner,” and stating that she was
helpless and suffering severely, and should have
been kept in the hospital. The hospital authori-
ties seem to argue that since no limbs were
broken the case was not one for hospital
treatment. The second scandal was graver.
.John Burrell was taken to Prince Alfred Hos-
pital on the night of the 11th. He had fallen
backwards down stairs while under the influence
of alcohol. According to his wife’s evidence at
the inquest, which has so far not been contra-
dicted or discredited in any way, he was bleeding
profusely from a wound in the back of the head.
On the 12th, she received word to bring his
clothes to the hospital, as he was fit to return
home. At the building, she found a nurse and a
doctor trying to rouse him, and unable to do
so. Nevertheless, he was sent home to her
that night—the evening of the 12th —in a
state of unconsciousness. On the 13th he
revived slightly, expectorated much blood,
and again relapsed into unconsciousness, and
was re-admitted to the hospital that even-
ing. He remained there till the night of the
16th, unconscious, and again he was sent home,
still unconscious. The wife called at the hospital
next day, and was told that when he got hungry
he would wake up and eat, but his wife failed to
get him to take even a glass of water. On the
19th an outside medico was called in, and
ordered the patient back to the hospital as being
in a desperate state. His wife was naturally tired
of Prince Alfred Hospital, and preferred the
Coast Hospital. A nurse from Little Bay arrived
to take him there, but declined to take charge of
him, on the ground that he would not survive the
journey. He was again taken to the Prince Alfred
institution, and died there at 5.45 p.m. that day,
and the ping-pong game with his unconscious form
ended. It is very hard toaccept any excuse, however
specious, for this incident. It lookslike sheer cruelty
or else shocking ignorance. But when a reason
is sought for these and other similar incidents it
must be found in the system. Under that system
a metropolitan hospital, and especially the Prince
Alfred, is primarily a clinic—a practice ground for
surgeons and physicians who give their services
free, partly for charity, partly for the practice they
will get. The paid medical officers are usually
youngsters fresh from the University who work
for merely nominal salaries —again partly out of
charity, and partly for practice. Naturally the
patient who is aninterestingand instructive “case”
is more welcome, and the patient with something
quite ordinary but none the less unpleasant and
deadly in the way of disease, is less welcome. The
Eatient comes to be estimated for the practice that
e gives. That is one source of scandal. The
next is that the hospital staff—doctors and nurses
—are generally much overworked as well as
underpaid. On the collection of voluntary sub-
scriptions the hospitals have to depend in part,
and this collection seldom gives adequate results.
So economy is always grinding away efficiency.
The third cause of hospital scandals is to be found
in the reaction of the first two on the per-
sonal factors. The doctor who sees nothing in
his hospital course "but experimenting and who is
also overworked, and the nurse whose nerves are
strained by too long hours, get fits of obstinacy.
What was at first a mistake is allowed to drift
into a scandal because of over-wrought nerves.
The fourth cause of “hospital incidents,”
and not the least, is the efforts of some of the
public to impose upon the hospitals. After
a dozen or so malingerers have cheated a
hospital, the time is ripe for a scandal to occur
through an honestly sick man being sent away
from the door. The ultimate conclusion to be
come to is that the semi-private hospital has out-
lived its day. There should be a hospital rate
and a health tax, and the hospitals should be run
absolutely by the State, with competent well-paid
men and women on duty, working reasonable
• hours and taught to regard every sick case as a
duty.
Fruit.
There is in N.S.W. and elsewhere throughout the
Commonwealth the co-operative butter factory
system in connection with the cult of cow ; why
not organisation on the same lines for the profit-
able utilisation of fruit? The factory system in
the dairying industry practically amounts to this :
that the individual farmer can bring his product
in its highly perishable state to a factory and have
it there turned into butter, which, under proper
conditions, will “ keep ” as far as London. Fruit
could be treated in the same way as milk. Every
fruit season the problem, “Does the orchardist
get a fair return or does he not ? ” rages in the daily
press, and a decisive negative Is generally arrived
at. Columns of “horrible examples” prove the
fact, and a reproachful glance is incidentally cast on
that economic trickster “supply and demand.”
But in regard to combating the evil —not a word.
Canada and California, the big fruit growers,
have long operated on their surplus fruit,
by drying, preserving, candying, and trans-
forming it into jam (the least profitable
process of all). Big growers can, of course,
do the same profitably here. It is the small
grower for whom the “fruit factory ” system is
suggested—the grower not producing a sufficient
quantity to make a full, scientific utilisation of
fruit-preserving methods a success. Speaking
broadly, the fruit-growing even more than the
dairying business tends to concentrate in one dis-
trict. The miles and miles of fruitful trees
streaming from Rarramatta well nigh to the
Hawkesbury in one direction, and from the out-
skirts of the western suburbs to Richmond in the
other, afford a good enough basis to start on. Let
each little nest of orchardists have a co-operative
factory with full drying, canning, candying and
jam-making plant. It would be the province
of such a factory to receive all the local
fruit each morning. The stuff as it
wus brought in would, bo cl&ssod 3.nd griidod.
Experts would deal with the bottling, drying,
and jam-making industries, with the manufac-
ture of candied orange and lemon peel and raisins,
and with the selecting and packing for the fresh
fruit market. Thus the fruit grower would escape,
at any rate in part, his two chief causes of loss—
(1) the effect of a sudden gale shredding his
almost ripened garden, and (2) the over-bountiful
season which floods the market with a very
perishable commodity, and Anally makes all
but freak fruit unsaleable at a profit.
With the factory system every bit of fruit
produced could be utilised as it is at a scientific
orchard such as Hawkesbury College. The fittest
could go to the fresh fruit market —if prices were
tempting enough. Other kinds could go to the
drying tray, the jam boiler or the preserv-
ing pan. To give the system a start an
experimental factory, purely for the treatment
of fruit, might be initiated by the State without
grave harm to the sanctity of the marriage tie,
and it could be made to pay from the jump. Of
course, a necessary corollary would be some radi-
cal orcharding legislation for the compulsory
extermination of pests by growers. Again the
dairying example might be followed (why a man
may not keep an unclean dairy, and yet is
encouraged to luxuriate in the dirtiest of orchards
remains an inscrutable mystery). Parts of Aus-
tralia are admirably fitted for fruit culture, as the
Tasmanian apple and the Adelaide grape show
now, and as the N.S. Wales orange used to show.
With effective legislation against pests, and the
encouragement of a co-operative factory system to
bring the help of science to the small grower, fruit
should become one of the great assets of the
Commonweal th.
