Labor and the Fiscal Issue.
Brewarrtna Political Labor League has proposed
that the Labor League Conference, now sitting at
Sydney, should endorse a Protective policy,
“ with a view to the better encouragement of
Australian industry and p;oduction.” The vast
majority of Australian Laborites are Protec-
tionists, but have hitherto not been able to assert
themselves because the early organisation of the
Labor political movement fell into the hands of
the waterside unions, the members of which
depend largely on the handling of imports for
their living, and are not far-sighted enough to see
the better jobs that Protection would give them.
And the argume.' - : was always available for the
“fiscal atheist” that to help on Protection would
be to favor the employer without any guarantee
that he would pass the favor on to the employee.
Labor has now, however, secured a firm footing in
the Legislature, and can always prevent sweating
if the community is well enough off to pay good
wages. But even an absolutely Labor Govern-
ment and Parliament could not prevent low wages
being paid if the community could not afford to pay
high ones. The natural ally of the Laborite is
therefore the Protectionist, to whom the Laborite
can say : Give our people decent wages, fair hours,
and an adequate old age pension, and in return
we will give you the absolute monopoly of our own
market. The Freetrade Laborite is in the position
of the cook who had to make hare soup without
the hare. Labor, when it has caught its
hare—namely, its local industries, by means of
High Protection —can, by legislative enact-
ments, force those industries to treat
their workmen and women properly. As
far as N.S.W. is concerned, Labor has already
got the machinery to make factory-work whole-
some and profitable, and all it wants now are the
factories and the employment, and high Protection
is the only thing that can give them. That i;
beginning to be recognised, though it may not be
endorsed by this conference, as the fiscal-sinkir.g
fetish is still powerful in the ranks of Labor. But
the fact that the resolution has been brought for-
ward at all is a hopeful sign. It is only necessary
for the Labor Leagues to honestly consider the
fiscal question to make their early adhesion to
Protection certain.

The Harvester Octopus and its Friends.
The Tariff Commission has had yet another state-
ment as to the exact profit made by the Inter-
national Harvester Company on the machines it
sells in Australia. Mr. R. H. Cowles, assistant
manager for the company in Australia, has fixed
the figure at “about equal to £2 per machine for
cash sales.” It will be remembered that the
famous “apple-tree” advertisement showed that
this corporation brought its machines all the way
from Chicago to make a paltry profit of £3 3s. Bd.
each. Then a brutal Customs Minister increased
the duty by £2 17s. lid. per machine, and the
declared profit of £3 3s. Bd. immediately dropped
to ss. 9d. However, the Octopus, plucky
creature, was not at all dismayed, and
promptly reduced the selling price of its
machines by £l2 10s., and thus, according
to its own statement, sold at a loss of
£l2 4s. 3d.—for the good of the Australian
farmer. Such philanthropy has not been known
since the magician went round China exchanging
new lamps for old ones—till he got that of
Alapdin. The statement of manager Cowers
that there is still a profit of “equal to about £2 ’
on each harvester is calculated to throw doubts on
the previously advertised statements of the
profits, but it is now explained that those adver-
tisements were not “ authorised ” —whatever that
may mean. Whether the International Company
paid for the advertisements which it did net
authorise, the assistant general manager did not
explain, and the. Tariff Commission did not ask
him. The publication of this evidence was fol-
lowed by an article in Sydney Telegraph, a
paper devoted to the slander of Australian manu-
facturers, which put forward these “facts”:—
1. That the Df.akin Government desires to put the
whole farming community of the Commonwealth under
further tribute to a few well-to-do local manufacturers of
harvesting machinery.
2. That the Australian wheat grower has to accept the
“ world’s price ” for his product. That price is fixed in
markets where the competing sellers represent the cheap
lands and cheap labor of the world. In the Common-
wealth the farmer is one of the victims, not one of the
beneficiaries, of Protection.
3. That the local manufacturer is “ naturally protected ”
by transit charges which the American goods have to pay.
4. That the Protectionists demand that every farmer
who wants a harvester shall buy from McKay and at
McKay’s price.
For less than a column article, that is a pretty
comprehensive category of falsities. Of course, it
is, or should be, apparent to even a person of weak
intellect that the total prohibition of imported
machines would not prevent the International
Harvester Company coming here to manufacture,
and, as anyone would be free to start a
harvester factory, the farmers could easily
start a co operative manufactory if the pre-
sent makers got out of hand. In Canada, there
was a tariff that was prohibitive, and although
the International Company’s works at Chicago
were only a few hours’ railway journey away, the
Yankee company was compelled to build a factory
on Canadian soil if it wanted to trade in the
Dominion. Fiction number 2, which avows that
the Australian farmer is not protected in his own
market, is a choice absurdity. Look up the Tariff
guide. Division IV.. and see that the Australian
farmer is protected in every branch of his m-
dustrv. He really lives on h>s Australian market.


The third Telegraph statement is flatly con-
tradicted by the Tariff Commission evidence,
which showed that it is cheaper to bring a
harvester machine from New York to .Sydney
than it is from Melbourne to Sydney. As for
Westralian ports, the difference in ocean and
coastal freights works out at £5 in favor of the
Yankee (equal to about 17 per cent.). The Inter-
national Harvester Co. is understood to be one of
the group that forms the Standard Oil Trust, and
gets the benefit of the “General Transportation
Company’s” rebate. The fourth statement is
merely a vain repetition of the first, and as Pro-
tection does not give McKay, or anyone else, a
monopoly, is a silly blunder that even a boy
reporter should not have fallen into. The
Telegraph really shouldn’t put its economic false-
hood department in charge of such inexperienced
hands.

The Commonwealth Government proposes to
establish a buying agency in London for the
Defence Department, and expects to save its cost
out of the commissions that would otherwise be
paid to the British Government. The success or
failure of this scheme will largely, if not entirely,
depend on the man chosen to run the business.
His duty will be to buy artillery, rifles and mili-
tary stores, and to keep the Department
promptly advised on all improvements in mili-
tary armament. To do this work efficiently
the man sent to England should be a thoroughly
competent Australian artillery officer, and the
appointment should be for a shortterm, four years
at the most. Outside the advantage to the Com-
monwealth, which 'would have its business con-
ducted by its own servant, would be the advantage
to the officer himself, who, it he cared to make
proper use of his opportunities, would find it a
capital educational course, of great value to him
in his profession when he returned to his ordinary
routine duties in Australia; and the selection
should specially be made of men likely to look at
the appointment from that point of view. The
mere office secretary, or business military man,
should be quite ineligible, and be absolutely
barred ; such a one would look on the appoint-
ment as a permanent and comfortable official
billet in London, and would be of no further bene-
fit to Australia; while the necessary clerical
and shipping work could easily be performed
by an ordinary clerk. The- expert knowledge
required being solely that of the artillery man,
the term of office should be a short one so as to
admit of a rapid succession of officers returning to
active employment in Australia, thoroughly posted
in all the most recent discoveries and information
connected with their profession; and in that
way the efficiency of the service will be greatly
increased at no additional expense to the Com-
monwealth.
Mr. Hillary Boucaut, of Adelaide, is at
present in Perth inspecting the W.A. Crown
lands on behalf of a number of German farmers,
who are at present settled in S.A., but are seri-
ously considering the question of migrating to
W. Australia.

‘ Wang : There was a free show in
Bourke-street (Melbourne), the other morning.
My friend. Ah Mong, was crossing at Russell-
street with a table-top on his head. In the
middle of the tram-track he paused to say “Ho
ki ”to Ah Long. It was raining, and the blocks
were slippery as glass. A tram coming down the
hill clanged its bell, but Ah Mong took no notice.
Ah Jjong skipped to one side just as the tram
struck Ah Mong in the middle of the back. I
thought a White Australia was nearer by one, but
it wasn t. Ihe board fell on the track five yards
i* 1 tram. Ah Mong started to say
Whatfor, ' then double-somersaulted onto the
board, and along they slithered down Bourke-
street hill. Ihe tram-conductor, gripman, four
policemen and myself pursued. Passing the
victoria Club, the toboggan began to slacken
speed, and the conductor sprinted and got within
a yard of it. At Svvanston-street corner the pro-
cession stopped ; and six policemen arrested Ah
Mong and charged him with furious driving, or
being without lights, or assault with intent, or
something.

If the plot of “Veronique” isn’t new, it
is at least real, and if the characters are all fami-
liar comic-opera properties they nevertheless live ;
and, overspreading all that, is the music—not
music that the cigarette weed on the step of the
tram whistles after the first night, but still fresh
and delightful, and full of quaint yet artistic little
surprises worked out mostly by the wood-wind
end of the orchestra. The setting is in harmony
with the music ; three-fourths of the time you are
in the flower-shop of M. Coquenard (Coquey, for
short), kept by George Lauri, or at the picnicking
grounds of Romainville, where Elorestan takes all
the flower-girls for a final spree. Among them is
Helene, the girl he’s got to marry (by order of his
uncle and the King), who, as Veronique, has
joined the flower girls in order to mash him'before
their marriage and make him sorry he ever loved
anybody else. Sydney hasn’t seen many prettier
pictures than these.
* * * *
Miss Margaret Thomas, as Veronique,
whose acting was never so good, scored her greatest
popular success in the “Swing Song” duet with
Haigh Jackson. But the double encore was a
tribute to Messager’s music rather than the
singer’s treatment, for here her voice (it may be
because of the swinging) was not so true as it was
in other places. But she recovered the lost tone
in the encore, and the refrain was irresistible.
Haigh Jackson, playing; Florestan (vice John
Doran, who had the part in the earlier Melbourne
production) atoned for a rather spiritless perform-
ance by the quality of his singing ; in Sydney, at
least, he has done nothing so well as the “ Letter
Song,” because into nothing else has he put so
much expression. And, barring a certain flinti-
■ ness in her love passages, in the heavy part of
Agatha, Coquenard’s wife and fickle Florestan’s
earlier mash, Miss Florence Young was buoyant
and satisfactory. Lauri supplies most of the
humor—very good humor, though inclined to run
rather much to the frill on lady’s underwear,
which may, however, be the fault of the original—
and Bantoek, as a baron turned bailiff, helped
him, and W. S. Percy supported as a sprightly
groom, who gets married during the proceedings.
#* * *
“The J.P.” is back on the Bench at
Sydney Palace, but only for one week—and the
week ends to-morrow (Friday) night. There is a
good deal in the piece that calls for atonement;
and Florence Lloyd, the best imitation young
man that any modern J.P. is ever likely
to meet, does the atoning. Florence begins the
proceedings as a female, but is plunged into \
trousers at an early hour in order to dodge the
G. J. HOLYOAKE, the English Radical,
whose death is reported.
wrath of tli« uncle of the man she has married,
said uncle (J. J. Dallas) being opposed to females
in general and any female his nephew might
marry in particular; and what to do with the
infant, under these distressing circumstances,
supplies enough to laugh about—and that is the
■shallow, frisky trifle’s purpose. Next Saturday
night the co. produces “There and Back,” a piece
new to Australia—with the stamp of Charles
Hawtrey upon it as a guarantee of good faith. It,
too, is alleged to go with a rush and a rattle, and
if it doesn’t, it isn’t likely to be the fault of the
co., which was built specially for that sort of
progress.
iK- * -* *
“Very few of us ’ave got conshunshes,
but we ve all got stummicks,” says one of the
starving characters in “Shall We Forgive Her.”
The play—it was resurrected on Saturday night
at the Criterion —is packed with epigrammatic
truths put in homely language that get great
applause from the gallery. The part of the
lady with the past, not of her own choosing,
was played well by Miss Ethel Appleton. In
the Queensland scene, where the dry, fierce hot
atmosphere is well suggested by the scene painter,
the actress was an artfully pathetic figure as she
went on her vain quest for water. It brought before
the audience plain tales from the bush, tales of
death by thirst and earth with cracks in it like
’ lips opening to pray for rain. Miss Beatrice Hol-
loway took the nice little ingenue part of Nellie
West, who would surely prove a Dora Copper-
field sort of wife. Mr. H. O. Willard was told
by much noise from the audience that he was
welcomed back to Sydney stage work. Miss
Deorwyn made a capital old lady. Messrs.
Godfrey Cass, Robert Inman and Mabel Russell
rounded off a capable cast. On Friday night
next “Two Little Vagabonds” will be revived
for a benefit performance to Miss Beatrice Hollo-
way. Next day the co. will start for Maoriland.

They carry out the move-on clause more
strictly at the Tivoli- than they do in King and
Spit streets, and thefe'is a crowd of new faces at
. the window this week. . And some old ones.
Harry Rickards himself is dealing his new pack of
songs—some of them about Leicester Square and
other places that the Tivoli doesn’t know half as
well as it knows WoolloomooloO. Manager
Rickards gives the Australian a very good show
in his theatres; but can’t he get a song or two
for himself from the same place? There is no
mistaking his personal popularity, though, no
matter what he sings or how he sings it; you
might gag and bind every usher in the place, and
the manager-vbcalist-Cbmedian-boss would still be
sure of three encores seven times a week. New
“ turns ” already here are the Scotch Meisters, and
a new turn coming is that of the Lotto-Lilo-Otto
trick cyclists; but even these won’t be able to
pack the house tighter than it has been packed
recently, when the late bird hasn’t had much
chance of perching on a seat.
** * *
A noticeable “turn” at the little Gaiety
Theatre (Melb.) just now is Brightie Smith, late
of Bland Holt’s co. Miss Brightie turns out to
be a graceful little dancer with a melodious sing-
ing voice and a very decidedly “nicer” style than
that of the ordinary music-hall Popsy, whose
“cheek” is her fortune. One doesn’t think of
Bland Holt’s as a school for variety artists, bu^
here is Brightie Smith—the kiddie of the co. be-
fore she qualified for grown-up parts—tripping on
to a strange stage to do some song-and-dance “on
her own,” and doing it uncommonly well, although
the songs she sings are of the weakest “coon”
sort, not written for a gii’l in skirts. Given a
better opportunity Brightie should sparkle.
*# * *
Young man Buonaparte, as idealised by
Rostand, is again disturbing the Austrian Court
for the edification of Melbourne audiences—this
time at Her Majesty’s. “L’Aiglon” in its
English translation is not a very great play, but
it is at least an impressive and “classy’’deed of
atonement for a previous Napoleon drama, where
an idealised Josephine is carried forward into
historical incidents with which she had no con-
nection. “ L’Aiglon ” amounts to an educational
romance, teaching an intelligent public some
things that might have been, whilst “A Royal
Divorce ” trades upon matters of general know-
ledge, in order to give an air of truth to things
that couldn’t have been. This essential difference
between the cheap British melodrama and the
poetical French play is now so well understood
that pedagogues are recommending “ L’Aiglon ”
as a history lesson for the young.
♦ •* ■* *
Miss Tittell Brune’s clever perform-
ance of the name part is marked by the same
energy and the same cough as before. The slie-
male impersonation is fairly convincing through-
out ; the nervous force is always there when
required, and, although the actress doesn’t attract
all the sympathy due to the character,, she
makes it interesting and never allows it to
go off the boil, so to speak. Touching
the changes in the cast, a large and particular
word of praise is earned by Miss Vaughan as
Marie Louise, a princess whose sins may be
forgiven her on account of her intensely haughty
lineage. She had “blue” blood, and her morals
were tinged accordingly. Miss Vaughan has
deportment and mannah enough for three queens,
and when one remembers that she lately ap-
peared as a truculent Mrs. Lead batter, at a
lodging-house where Miss Brune did the cleaning,
one is inclined to think that all the world’s a
stage, and the distribution of characters ought to
be varied every few weeks.
** * *
“Utopia Limited,” at the Princess’s, is
receiving a good deal of the patronage that might
be expected for a work of such importance. Also,
it gets further consideration from some of the
performers who have the honor to introduce a
Gilbert and Sullivan opera to Australia, In
sundry places the cleverness of the libretto is more
artistically displayed than on the first night, and
in one important place there is a new lady. Miss
Olive Goodwin, the successor to Miss Dolly
Castles, has the advantage of a tall, graceful
figure and a reposeful air. Her singing
strengthens the show somewhat on its musical
side, and her elocution is a little less defective
than the other lady’s was. But the art of speak-
ing Gilbertian lines pointedly is not easy to learn,
and Miss Goodwin is still a beginner only.
“Utopia Limited” comes very hard on- the
tongues of the co., for the songs are mostly of the
descriptive-conversational order, set to music
which lends no brightness to the lines. These
mu iical patters are quite long enough when melo-
diously rendered. In the case of Howard Vernon
they grow wearisome. The opera rarely gets away
from Vernon. He is the ruling spirit, the lunatic
master of the situation, and he makes no attempt
at subtle drollery. King Paramount is not even
quaint in his make-up. If he were to wear an
exceedingly bald head and act up to it, the
result would probably be funnier. A distinctive
chuckle might suit the part, which is clearly not
adapted to Howard Vernon, anyhow; The chorus
is not richly endowed with opportunities to assert
its excellence, but it utters its usual volume of
sound in new costumes, and helps to make the
opening scene a festive spectacle. The second
act of “Utopia’’ is in every way weaker than the
first. Probably Gilbert, at half-time, made up his
mind that Sullivan’s music was not Sullivan’s
best, and in his disappointment he dropped his
enthusiasm.
■s^
“ The Lights o’ London,” at the Royal, is
a well-timed production. It has cooling moments
when the Bland Holts travel across a snow-bound
country in a caravan and open their warm hearts
to a frozen convict. Also it is pleasant to witness
Baker and Styan splashing about in a tank at the
close of a day that has been infernally hot outside
of the melodrama. Baker is an envied hero
these nights, because his rescue of Styan
is a palpable excuse for a cold plunge.
If he were not bent on getting wet
he could lean over the side of the tank and
pull the other man ashore, but he prefers to find a
place where he can take a header. The tank is a
comparatively modern innovation in an otherwise
ancient play, so ancient as to be honorable. The
“Lights” dates back to a time when a melo-
dramatist was expected to put a few ideas and
jokes of his own into the business, all of which
were necessarily left at the mercy of subsequent
writers who have used them again and again.

A new form of musical entertainment at
the Opera House is supplied by Miss Alice
Raymond and John Kurkamp. Miss Alice
Raymond, a comely lady with an impressive chest,
blows music from a cornet what time her colleague
is conducting the accompaniment of the orchestra.
The lady, who is a shining light among brass
instrumentalists of her sex, plays a solo or two,
and then Kurkamp in the orchestra faces the
audience and sings the tenor part in “The
Miserere” (“II Trovatore ”), and other little
Gay Florestan,
A spry young man,
Who’s doing well, though single,
Must wed a maid he doesn’t know
Because his uncle tells him so—
A maid with coin to jingle
It’s time for him to marry,
He may no longer tarry ;
The shadow of the gaol walls
Across the spendthrift’s pathway falls,
And gaol he’d rather parry.
And so he weds within a week
The unknown, gilded Veronique.
And Veronique
The flowers to seek
To make the bridal bouquet
Must drop, by all the laws of Chance,
Into the fairest shop in France,
That’s kept by Lauri Coquey,
Where Florestan comes daily
To mash the flower-girls gaily.
With Coquey’s wife he’s making hay ;
The lady isn t saying nay
And shows up rather frailly.
Behind a curtain Veronique
Is meanwhile playing hide-and-seek.
And Veronique
With flushing cheek
(For Florestan’s been saying
Some things about his unseen bride—
“A frump, no doubt,’’.and much, beside)
Decides to do some slaying ;
She’ll captivate her Florry,
She’ll have him for her quarry ;
She’ll catch her courtier on the hop,
She’ll take a billet in the shop—
The shop that’s kept by Lauri.
And that is how, to help the freak,
She takes the name of Veronique.
Of course, she won.
To have a run,
A play must end in kisses;
And this one doesn’t spoil the rule
They teach in every playwright’s school
To please the mat’nee misses.
Swift Florry falls in raptures,
The easiest of captures.
So full the springs of joy both driilk,
They’ve got, you’re half-inclined to think,
Their own, and likewise tapped yours.
So. turns the plot on themes antique,
Till bridal bells call Veronique.

The “Living London” biograph made a
i tar t at the Town Hall on Saturday. It has
virtues and its faults. Some of the pictures
ltS the clearest and steadiest that have
Ten produced in Melbourne, and some of these
be f the best subjects in the series—pictures
nf market places, full of life and spiced with
? or The ordinary thoroughfare pictures are
mo numerous and hot invariably excellent as
hotogranhs. A succession of omnibuses loaded
with cockneys soon begins to pall upon the
flickered eye, and one narrow, crowded street
looks very like another. If “Living London”
were judiciously pruned the general effect would
be stronger. The film wants an editor armed with
!V pair of scissors.

In an absolute dearth of theatrical amuse-
ment at Adelaide, a Sunday evening biograph
•md concert show at the Jubilee Exhibition was
blocked by the Government, which owns the
building, to the disappointment of a 1200 audi-
ence The acting Public Works Minister explains
that these shows are only allowed on the pro-
grammes previously passing his inspection, and
the caretaker simply blocked (by turning the
lights up) items he had formally disapproved for
Sunday use —“Soot, Student, and Smoke,” etc.
The proprietors claim that they were unex-
pectedly told at noon on Saturday that animated
pictures would not be allowed at all, and “it was
then too late to make other arrangements.” So
they chanced it, and a disappointed audience was
turned out at 9.15, after making fruitless inquiries
for the “ silver coin ” admission-money.

According to the cables, “Sweet Nell” Stewart has
scored a big success in ’Frisco. The co. needed to make
a good start to get well on its legs for the American tour.
An early repulse would have been disastrous.
“Beth”: I don’t want “The Sorcerer” driven from
the boards by the spineless or incompetent criticism of
certain dailies. The work is characterised as a “mere
vague attempt to do nothing in particular;” “much
flapping of wings and a flight of some sort accomplished,
but a wobbly motion after all.” I know nearly every
drop of ink that Gilbert and Sullivan ever spilt, including
and excluding their collaboration, and I have more affec-
tion for “The Sorcerer” than for any other opera in
which either was concerned. It is not their greatest
work, but there is an endearing pastoral charm about it
that puts even a milkmaid’s blush to shame. From start
to finish it is redolent of hawthorn and primroses and
newly-laid milk; an idyll where villagers and the vicar
and the squire and the lady, and other quaint old-
fashioned types (rapidly being eradicated by pneumatic
tyres and electricity and general elections), pursue the
even tenor of their tea and quarter-day. And the critic
who misses its simple beauty ought to be a tram-driver.
The Nora Dane, whom Melbourne knew of yore as a
Oonservatorium pupil with an acquired German accent,
is now making her way in Britain as an Italian vocalist.
Aberdeen Free Press gave a glowing report of Signora
Dane-Valenti’s recital in that city. “The freedom with
which the Signora uses her voice, the fluent quality of its
tone, and the perfection with which she uses it as a
medium for every shade of expression all point to tuition
of the most skilled kind. Whether in a florid Rossini
aria or in a group of three short modern songs, there was
the same intelligent, finished style, the same truth in
sentiment. When called upon for an encore, and reply-
ing with ‘ Cornin’ through the Rye,’ her pronunciation
of the Doric was so perfect that one might have readily
supposed she was a native.” And this is the same Nora
who, when she sang an English-worded song on a Mel-
bourne platform, might have been readily mistaken for
a German. Her marriage with Valenti, a competent
teacher of the Italian method, seems to have worked
wonders. The conversion of Nora into Sig-Nora was a
complete artistic success.
Mrs. M. Henningham, the energetic old lady who ran
the United Service Club Hotel, Collins-street, Melbourne,
went under last week to her third attack of paralysis.
She has two sons in the theatres —Fred, the manager, and
Will, the orchestra conductor. Will’s wife, also, has re-
appeared on the stage, showing a shapely form with The
Players.
“ Hiphen ": I have been to see “ Merely Mary Ann.” I
regret it. It is a play the actors have to carry on their
backs. And they aren’t strong enough—all except
Tittell Brune, and she whines. All the time I felt inclined
to tell her to go and have a good cry and get it over. But
I didn’t like to be rude. I’ve been brought up not to be
rude. They used to tell me that in England boys are
never rude. It used to make me glad I didn’t live there.
Still, as a rule, I try not to be rude. So I sat quietly,
even when, for two acts, Tittell did nothing but put on
and take off gloves. And whined. Bah ! I could do
that, and I’m no good. They won’t even have me in the
Players. That’ll tell you how bad I am ! Still, I could
change my gloves, And whine. Then Lancelot talked
t°- cauai- y as Baker would talk to a man who
tried to shoot him. No wonder the canary died.
He is supposed to be a musical genius. Why,
urn- no *’ even dirty. And his linen’s clean,

“ 0.K.” : The merits of comic opera or musical comedy
can be best judged from a matinee performance. At
night, and particularly on the first night, one’s view of
the work, as a whole, is intercepted by a number of “ en-
cores,” genuine or otherwise. At a matinee encores are
avoided, and the show goes its way with the regularity of
a drama.
“S.S.”: Chows are becoming such excellent patrons of
melodrama in Melbourne that we may presently expect
to find the Mongolian quite respectably treated when he
figures in the play, much as the Jew is, who, from being
the mainstay and colleague of the villain, has developed
into a sympathetic soul who appreciates all the hero’s
virtues, and tempers his financial transactions with
Christian sentiments. I was sitting behind a row of five
Chinamen in Melbourne Royal stalls the other night, and
the most ingenuous and susceptible gallery girl could not
have been more effectually worked upon by Miss Frances
Ross’s trials, Mr. Walter Baker’s tribulations, and the
comic interludes by Mr. and Mrs. THandolt, than were
Sins How, Me, Fat, Fit, and Cat. These five cardinal
Sins laughed consumedly at the simplest capers of the
comedians, they were rapt and tremulous listening to
the mournful complainings of persecuted virtue, they
were stirred and indignant when villainy worked its
cunning wiles, and sat with- intent faces, uttering
little Chinese ejaculations expressing hatred of the wrong-
doers. And when the climax of pathos came with the
reunion of the doting wife and the affectionate and mis-
fortunate convict, three of the Chinamen wept dolorously,
and for five minutes one lugubrious Pagan sat mopping
his tears with the lining of bis hat. Obviously the imper-
turbable heathen is degenerating under the influence of
Caucasian civilisation.
J. C. Williamson’s latest recruit is Claude Hall, the
terror of South-street. A few years ago he was a front-
rank racing cyclist, and finished second to Tom Busst
when the latter won the Austral Wheel Race. Retiring
from the track, he became a singer and reciter, and for
years has been making a regular circuit of South-street
and other competitions, winning steadily all the time.
His energy in this direction was extraordinary, and when
he wasn’t trilling ballads or declaiming poems he manu-
factured a popular brand of liver pill. Why he should go
on the stage is not quite plain. He has amassed wealth
quite up to the average of the liver pill maker, and has a
stable full of buggies, dog-carts, carriages and motor-cars.
Bland Holt, a glutton for work, promises to produce
Hall Caine’s drama, “The Prodigal Son,” at Melbourne
Royal in another week or two. He started on the pro-
duction the moment the necessary material came to hand
from London, and it will be the biggest job he has ever
tackled at high pressure.
Melbourne swimmer Beatrice Kerr announces her in-
tention of leaving for England. Why doesn’t somebody
advise her to stay where she is well off ? There is nothing
for a girl swimmer in England except showing at the
music-halls, and Annette Kellermann (who really made
nothing out of her largely-advertised Channel swims) has
taken all the music-hall money available. Further,
though little Beatrice has perhaps a more neatly-built
figure than Annette, the public likes quantity rather than
neatness and is not likely to roll up to watch the diving
of an eight-stone Venus when they have been fully sup-
plied only recently with an eleven-stone Juno. Aus-
tralian swimming girls are all mad on England just now,
owing to Miss Kellermann’s success, just as every girl who
can trill “ Dolly Gray ” from a picnic van thinks she only
wants a fare to London to send Melba back to milk cows
at Cave Hill.
“ S.T.S.” : Curious that none of “ Princes? Ida’s ” many
critics seem to be aware that the piece really is what it
strikes them as —different from the other Gilbert and
Sullivan shows. The fact of its being the only one In
blank verse ought to suggest something. Gilbert in 1883-4
(after “lolanthe” and before “Mikado”), wanting a
libretto, raked up his old play “ The Princess,” and wrote
songs into it. How old that was I can’t fix, but Hilarion
was originally played by “ Maria Simpson (Mrs. Liston),”
and the rowdy Cyril by Augusta Thomson. (All six
princes were played by girls.) The opera, as I saw iD at
the Savoy, was immensely successful. Grossmith and
Rutland Barrington were the two Kings, Durward Lely a
robust Cyril, and Leonora Braham the Princess. Our own
Henry Bracy was the hero, and his girlish-looking com-
panion was Ryley, afterwards well known in this country.
“0.K.”: A Poverty Pointer, commenting on the com-
parative heaviness and stiffness of “ Princess Ida,” draws
the very wrong conclusion that Gilbert and Sullivan were
feeling tired when they made the opera. As a matter of
fact, they were too fresh at the time. Gilbert’s libretto
is tinged with the color of his popular comedy, “The
Palace of Truth,” and the music of Sullivan, as in the case
of “The Sorcerer,” is considerably suggestive of old
Italian opera. Some of it is frankly intended for a
travesty of the Donizetti class of composition. It was
not until “ Pinafore ” (in 1878) that Gilbert's Gilbertianism
took a thoroughly symmetrical form, whilst Sullivan’s
music developed a full sense of humor and a complete
confidence in itself.
Judge Docker, after all, cancelled that
flogging sentence on a prisoner of weak intellect
found guilty of a sex offence, and substituted for
two years and a flogging, five years’ penal servi-
tude. The Judge regretted that he hadn’t power
to order the man’s perpetual imprisonment—
thereby showing a return to common sense and a
serious defect in the recently-enacted Habitual
Criminals Act. Neither penal servitude for a
limited term nor flogging is likely to cure a sex
maniac ; such punishment, as a rule, only aggra-
vates his mania, and then sends him out to once
again prey on Society. Offenders of that sort
should be shut up for ever; and recent legislation
was supposed to have given the power to do that.
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners have
agreed to issue tickets to Temora at holiday ex-
cursion rates to prospective settlers on the Mimosa
Estate now cut up for closer settlement. Certifi-
cates as to bona-fides must be secured from
Messrs. Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd., 15 O’Connell-
street, Sydney.
A correspondent from Laokay, Tonkin,
Indo-China (l/6/’O5)
Laokay is on the border of Yunnan, the southern pro-
vince of China, where the great railway, Laokay-Yiin-
nansen, starts. It is a French syndicate line of about 300
miles, 60 of which are being constructed now. The
French Government gives £400,000 towards it, as the rail-
way represents the “future peaceful conquest” of Yun-
nan. Country is beautiful and picturesque, very much
like the Jordan and King Rivers on West Coast of Tas-
mania. Climate for the first 100 miles in the valley of the
Red River and the Manti River is decidedly bad, though
a white man, with care in drink and diet, can pull through
with a few attacks of malaria. The heat in the shade is
not over 99 deg., but in the sun anything between a
copper-smelter and h—l. Working hours from 6 to 10 a.m.,
and 3to 6 p.m., siesta between. The French engineers,
though they take only two meals—at 11 a.m. and 7 p.m.—
eat too much and drink too much alcohol with their
meals, and consequently often collapse and blame the
climate. On the highland of Yunnan, a plateau 6000 ft.
above sea, the climate is that of France.
The Chow coolies, mostly imported from Canton, die
like flies ; every day six to 10 or 20 have to be buried (if
throwing a corpse on a spall bank and shovelling loose clay
over him can be called burial). When a Chow gets ill his
mates drive him out of their “canya,” and the poor
wretches die all the sooner on the railway line. Rain or
heat, day or night, I see them daily crawling about aim-
lessly, with glassy eyes, some resigned to their fate, some
lifting hands for help, which is beyond me to render, as
most of them won’t even take quinine when offered. No
food is given them by their mates. All work on contract,
though we offer as much as Is. 3d. per day against 6d.
anywhere else in China. Only from 10 to 20 per cent, of
the coolies work at a time, as one day’s work will keep
them in tucker for a week. The rest squat in their
“canyas,” about two dozen in one, smoke bamboo water-
pipes (some opium), and wonder if they are ill or not.
When too many get sick in one “canya” we put a match
to it—not so much for sake of the Chows, who love filth,
and, as the French say, “Every dead Chow means one
less to fight some day in Europe ’’—but for our own sakes, as
cholera will soon break out now. What a pity that your
Australian Chow friends don’t come here to have a look
at their darlings. Domestic servants are all Annamites,
natives of Indo-Chinm a much more intelligent, but
bodily weaker race. Tlie Anuamite women are not bad
looking, and are often taken as wives by the French, and
a nice lot of half-castes are grooving up.

question is perhaps of some general interest.
The Bulletin prints—in a condensed form
—this story, which it hadn’t the least doubt,
on first reading, was unsuitable for its columns.
The great army of the Rejected has possibly some
claim that the public should be taken into confi-
dence now and again; at any rate, rejected
artists at Sydney Spring show always seem to
contemplate an exhibition of the damned. Space
won’t allow the publication of the entire work
(that would be a matter of some columns), but the
tliread of the story and samples of its eloquence

-*•»#*
The opening scene discovers Arthur Eddlestone,
swagman and shearer, “receding” from Jumble-
deen station, having, all unknowing, managed to
win the love of the haughty proprietor’s sister.
The event occurred thus :
For but a brief space had the soft, lustrous blue eyes of
Laura Marsh met the honest, manly gaze of Arthur
Eddlestone, but in that glance a life’s story had been
exchanged, and thenceforward no circumstance could he
so adverse as to make void the mutual interest of these
newly-awakened hearts.
The lady’s affection is returned —for she, be it
noted, is “ some pumpkins ” herself :
The eye of the master-artist could only drink to reple-
tion, while his brush would falter in his despair of por-
traying the ethereal radiance and entrancing form of
Frank Marsh’s only sister as she, seeming like an awakened
soul gracefully poised preparatorily to taking its flight
for a fairer world, and partially concealed by the trailing
woodbine which had transformed an angle of the veran-
dah into an incense-breathing alcove, kept her eyes fixed
with a wistful longing upon the swaying shrubs and sun-
beat path by which his steps carried the stranger further
and further away till, at last, the intervening landscape
obscured the view.
The stranger having “receded” to an adjoin-
ing station, word is suddenly brought of a bush fire
at Jumbledeen, and volunteer fire-fighters are
called for. Some grasping and currish “serving
men” are justly scarified :
One or two totally ignored the call to the rescue of the
women and children ; others inquired what would be the
remuneration for their services ; but one, while this mer-
cenary wrangle was in progress, had hastily dressed and
placed himself under instructions from the chief director
of the rescue party, and was chafing at delay which pro-
longed the suspense of waiting.
Needless to state that “one.”
Intuitively, Arthur Eddlestone, had been allotted a
mount that made him the envy of many of his
companions, who knew the quality of the steed
he bestrode. A two-mile gallop brought the first gate
across the roadway, and, disdaining to dismount from his
capable beast, the impassioned lover had humored his
mettle and ridden him triumphantly over, to the unfeigned
astonishment of the foremost knot of his companions,
whose spontaneous cheer was cut short by their hurrying
through the now opened gateway, and whose strenuous
efforts to overtake their fearless leader were unavailing
throughout the rest of the ride, for, when next they saw
him, he was singed and blacked, and heroically combating
the flames which, almost simultaneously with his arrival,
had begun to lick the palisade within which stood the
hospitable homestead of “Jumbledeen.”
The fire extinguished, Mr. Marsh with bluff
open-handedness asks all volunteers to remain and
form a sort of impromptu house-party. But all
refuse, except one.
The following day found Arthur Eddlestone installed an
honored guest.
Arrives the post-prandial hour —squatter and
swagman are in friendly converse on the verandah,
when :
The opportunity to clear away any uncertainty that
might exist with regard to himself in the mind of his
host suggested itself to the guest. “ You have learned
my name somewhat haphazard,” he remarked in courteous
'te.ms, as he laid aside his cigar, “andhave, unquestion-
ing, honored me as your guest. Will you kindly permit
n.e the privilege of proffering my card, or rather what
was my card in the not yet very distant past. As there
is a tide in the affairs of every man which demands
promptitude and exactitude, I wish to place my position
before you explicitly.”
A “low and graceful bow” follows, and the
pastoralist is handed a card :
“ Arthur Eddlestone, R. A.,” he read aloud in a voice of
astonishment. “ This is indeed a link in a strange chain
of coincidence. I should say we have long been potential
friends. Your father and mine certainly were exceeding
great friends, as often I have heard my father tell. .Some
gleam of the wherefore of your present position glints
into my mind now; but I cannot understand why you
have preferred manual toil to the manifestation of your
art, seeing that a number of your creations are the proud
boast of our Australian galleries, and take rank with
the pride of many galleries in other lands where art
is even more highly prized than here.”
In a series of crashing poly-
syllables, Arthur, later,
learns that lie has
inherited a for-
tune ; and with
Olympian calm he
delivers the follow-
ing few remarks:
“ This kindly turn
of fortune, for I
think I may rightly
deem it such, re-
moves what to me
had seemed an insur-
mountable barrier
in the path of my
most cherished hopes
and the attainment
of my dearest ambi-
tion. My brush and
palette have accom-
plished something
already, and I have
had the temerity to
hope they would
yet accomplish much
more, but have al-
ways realised the
fact that monetary
increments as per-
taining to Art
mostly accrue post
mortem, as regards
the artist, and
usually benefit other
than he. Art for
art’s sake has indeed
been a solac e co me
at all times, but it
has brought me but
little profit, whereas
what now comes to
me through no worth
or seeking of mine,
confers upon me the
freedom to aspire to
an exalted social
plane, and liberates
my hitherto shackled
spirit that it may
do homage at the shrine of a divine love.
Being assured that my love is not in vain
it devolves upon me to apprise you of pro-
bable events of the near future. An
immeasurable sacrifice is involved when
you are asked to resign the companionship
of one who has been to you the sunshine
of your childhood and the day-star of your
maturer years, and I can but hope for your
goodwill and favor in your consideration
of the prospect of parting from her whom
you so love and adore.”
This means that the gentleman would like to
marry his host’s sister: —
The heartiness of the hand-clasp and the impetuous
cordiality which greeted the lover as he paused for the
brother’s answer cemented the bond of fellowship which
already hound these two estimable natures. “ A sense
of loneness may at times beset me in the absence of
Laurie’s voice and cheering presence,” said Frank, “but
the reflection that I have gained a brother so noble and
so devoted to my sister will fully recompense for the
sacrifice I make. But when, may I ask, do you purpose
leaving me in my realm of solitude ?”
How charmingly arch is Arthur’s reply !
“ I will not pass sentence ” (laughing), “ for I perceive
we are about to be joined by a judge than whom I could
scarcely be more lenient and merciful where your wishes
and welfare are concerned.”
Whereupon enter Laurie. In a perfect flood of
verbiage the lady is swept to the altar. The cere-
mony is over:—
“ Sleeping or waking, cara mia,” said her lover, gazing
fondly into her twinkling eyes, “ the essence of Beauty is
ever being distilled for you from the choicest flowers in
the garden of Love, and no queen of fairies or queen of
the hearts of men was ever more bountifully endowed
with the graces that have won our praise and admiration
right down the ages.”
That is not the end ; but it is near enough.
Reverend Carr Smith, of St. James’, Syd-
ney, said last Sunday that under our Immigration
Act he would not have been allowed to land here
now. He came out under contract about nine
years ago. The Rev. Smith is a decent sort, and
it’s a pity that he doesn’t look before he talks.
The contract provisions of the Immigration Act
apply only to manual laborers, and a parson isn’t
a manual laborer, and doesn’t come under the Act.
“L” : Melbourne owns a bar-loafer who
cadges drinks “ on the Times instalment plan; ”
when he can’t get a shout, he snatches an empty
glass and coaxes those in the bar into helping to
fill it out of their glasses. It takes him anything
up to an hour to get a glassful, and I have known
him swallow a weird mixture of as many as 10
different liquids. As soon as he drinks one of his
cocktails he starts collecting for another. The
landlord of the pub. at which I first met him
allowed him to hang about for a few months, as
he used to amuse customers who had a crude idea
of humor.

A boy can get a University or an Adversity
training. The first mostly makes him ornamental,
the second useful. An ideal training would com-
bine both.
In one S. Australian district a private
citizen—not in any way connected with the Forces
—has made a military survey of the country,
marked out routes for artillery, lines of communi-
cation, fodder and water supplies, and, roughly,
places for position guns with approximate ranges
to points where an invader would have to pass.
He did this for his own satisfaction, during the
S’African war, on Boer principles. It is a very
rough scheme. But has any military officer in
S. A. done that much ?
* * * *
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade is now
forced to admit that the new Liquor Act can’t be
administered strictly. It is to be administered
“ with discretion,” like many another Act passed
with indiscretion. Hotel-keepers forced on this
decision by rigidly interpreting the new Act to
mean what it professed to mean. Now neglectful
administration will be utilised to neutralise
neglectful drafting. That system of half adminis-
tering half-considered laws is common, but it is .
vicious. If the Liquor Act is unreasonable, as
Mr. Wade seems to admit, it should be amended,
not suspended by Ministerial edict.
The Argus lists of the ‘ ‘ Twelve Best Pic-
tures ” show the massive ignorance of the alleged
cultured and upper classes. If the working people
fancied such things, the Argus crowd would
look down on them with horror, and want to take
away their franchise. Banal story-pictures hold
pride of place in the Argus list; these and gory
battle scenes, or traitors being tossed from the
Exhibition dome, or else “sweetly pretty” Xmas
supplement things, like the “ Dying Child ” or
“When Father Reads the Bible.” The Argus
competitions as to the Twelve Best Books,
Public Men, Pictures, and so on, have done its
respectable readers little good by exposing the
disreputably low standard of their taste.

“The Hack” : There is a
good deal of expensive laxity in the
regulations as to the use of school-
books in Victorian State schools. At
present the books to be used in a
school are selected by the head
teacher, and that leads to wire-pull-
ing. There are probably not two
schools in the State which use the
same books. If John Jones moves
(say) from Brighton to Middle
Brighton, all the junior
Joneses have to be provided
with new books ; and to a
man with a large family
that sort of thing is very
burdensome. Also, the
continual changing of
books without notice
means heavy loss to
the small bookseller,
through accumulation
of unsaleable stock.
Here is an instance of
what happens under
the present want-of-
system : A Melbourne
book firm published a
geography for a (then)
junior State school
teacher. It didn’t
take, and for some
years the unsaleable stock
drew wild curses from the
boss. Then one day the
author offered to clear the lot
at an alarming sacrifice, and
the boss agreed. A couple of
weeks later the author was ap-
pointed to a big school, and
every child who has attended
that school uses that book and
pays full price for it, too.
“ Elmore ” : I put in
New Year’s Day culling
good resolutions from old
proverbs. Some of the
results of my researches :
“Absence makes the
heart grow fonder,”
“Out of sight, out of
mind”; “Penny wise,
pound foolish,” “Take
care of the pence, and
the pounds will take
care of themselves”;
“Birds of a feather flock
together,” “Two of a trade
never agree” ; “Many hands
make light work,” “ Too many cooks spoil
the broth”; “Delays are dangerous,” “He
who hesitates is lost,” “Look before you leap,”
“Second thoughts are always best ” ; “The early
bird catches the worm,” “ All things come to him
who waits.” I believe G. H. Reid wrote the
proverbs.
Velasquez Venus —“ the Venus with the Mirror ”as it is known—just bought by the British
National Gallery for £40,000, is practically the only famous nude in Spanish art; the gloomy,
almost Calvinistic character that Roman Catholicism took in Spain did not tend to encourage
the nude in art. The Venus was painted by Royal command, and is accounted one of the loveliest
figure subjects in the world. The pose, it will be noted, makes a liberal concession to prudery.
Victorian Board of Public Health lately
gave a hint as to the cash value to the public of
freedom from sickness. In ’O5 the State had 700 ,
fewer cases of typhoid fever than in 04. Taking <
the ages of the persons affected at between 15 and
25, and putting down the loss of wages and cost
of medical attendance at the moderate estimate of
£l5 per head, the State was thus better off in one
year alone by about £lO,OOO. When the unclean
person raves about the cost of health inspection
and dairy supervision, he might remember this.

“ Gebo ” gives a reminiscence of a Gov.
Brassey party, showing how the memory of vice-
royalty lingers, and the crimson tie that binds and
so on :
It was about the time I left school to fight the world,
that I was asked to visit Govt. House, at one of the
famous Lord Brassey parties. We were ranged outside a
marquee, Ohow fashion, one behind the other. Sub-
sequently we marched in alongside a lengthy table. For
each “ scholar ” was a plate containing a sausage roll, a
sponge cake, a jam tart, a bag of cheap boiled lollies, and
a bottle of ginger beer. With dreadful threats we were
told by some autocrat at the festive table “not to touch”
anything till the word “go.” Then he timed us, giving
us five minutes to “ get through ” the lot, and every now
and then calling out how many more minutes were left.
This took place in Lord Brassey’s presence. He stood
near by and didn’t fall off anything. In the afternoon
there were amusements—conjurors and Punch-and-Judy
shows. We amused ourselves by throwing the lollies at
the latter, and many of us were publicly ejected from the
holy grounds.
* * * »
Tuesday, Jan. 23rd, when Melbourne
buzzed under a temperature of llOdeg., saw
almost a repetition of the Gippsland bush-fires of
’9B, which Longstaff has so vividly presented.
Over the Hoddle Ranges, down on the little
townships of Toora and Woorarra, swept a wall of
flame 100 ft. high. Fast as the blistering wind,
giving little chance to man or beast to escape, it
licked up everything over miles of country. Six
little mites, toddling home at noon from school,
were met by their frenzied mother and rushed
back along the track to a main road, where they
stood while she strove to keep the waves of flame
away from them. They lost their heads and
broke away—every child being suffocated. The
six little bodies were buried next day in the one
grave, while the father stood by, blind, ban-
daged and broken. Here and there others,
adults, met a horrible fate, fighting hope-
lessly. One man struggled to within two
chains of a house in a clearing near Woorarra,
then collapsed. In that four-roomed cottage were
sheltered 20 men, women and children, cringing
away from the fierce heat, and only rushing out
now and then to beat out flying sparks that struck
the building. There were tales of heroism too— of
cool-headed teachers who kept their pupils inside
and covered them with wet bags, themselves blinded
and singed in the effort to save the little ones.
Others kept the panic-stricken weaker ones in
waterholes and fought the flames and the hysteria
that meant absolute destruction. Three schools,
a church, a Mechanics Institute and houses with-
out number were reduced to ashes. So far the
tally of victims totals 14, but it is feared it will
mount to a score or more when all is known.
A story of a desperate fight attaches to the
death of one lonely settler. A rescue party, know-
ing that the fires had swept over this man’s land,
went to see how he had fared. The outhouses had
been burned, but after a long, hard struggle the
fires had been beaten off from the dwelling.
Entering this, the searchers found the settler dead
upon the floor, a wet bag still in his blackened
hand. The man was singed and burnt from head
to heel. The poor fellow had conquered, but the
effort had proved too much for him, and he had
died when the danger was past.
From “ Red Tape Junior ” :
For circumlocution N.S.W. Police Department is diffi-
cult to beat. Suppose a hairy selector named Thompson
calls on the Minister for Lands to complain that his
neighbor’s bull has been making faces at him. He can’t
be sent to blazes because “ his member is with him,” and
the member’s vote is going to be wanted. So Thompson
is promised an inquiry, and the Minister politely requests
his colleague the Chief Secretary to make them. The
Chief Secretary instructs the Inspector-General of Police.
Thompson and the bull reside in a hamlet in the south-
west, and instead of sending down direct, red tape
demands that the Inspector-General should consult the
superintendent of the division, who perhaps is stationed
at Bourke. The superintendent asks the sub-inspector
of the district for his report, which means that the
I.G.P.’s letter zigzags down to Wentworth. This official
sends down to the nearest sergeant, some hundreds of
miles away, who has to find the senior constable stationed
near to Thompson. Finally, the complaint of Thompson
reaches police-constable Murphy, Who discovers that a
Murrumbidgee mosquito on the nose of the bull was the
cause of all the worry. So, back along the road it came,
goes this letter to the 1.G.P., after a tour that a record-
breaking motorist would envy, and reaches him when
everyone has long forgotten Thompson.
CIVILISED RELIGION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ballarat, Sunday.—No formal charge has yet been
made in respect to the alleged ritualistic practices on the
part of the vicar of St. Peter’s, Start-street.
Some years ago similar trouble arose at another Angli-
can church in Ballarat, owing to the alleged llomish
practices of the then vicar, and a section of the congrega-
tion showed its displeasure by assembling in a body
and smashing the windows in the buildings on the church
grounds. —Melbourne Aon.
And is the vicar on his ritual bent,
And at our purple indignation mocks he!
Then let him meet the proper punishment
Of heterodoxy.
Drop, O ye faithful, thought of age or sex,
And hasten to the rubbish-bin to garner
The agbd egg, and possibly the ex-
Tra-ripe banana.
Appropriate the plum of moilldy hue,
And though upon your arms you feel the load weigh,
Add to it jagged lumps of metal blue
From off the roadway.
Charge ! With macadam hurled that none may miss,
’i'is sad to knock the stained-glass saint to Hinders ;
But naught we spare in such a cause as this —
Not even windows.
Not without reason should we cry a truce ;
While forth to remonstrate our pastor reaches,
We’ll therefore daub his visage with the juice
Of mushy peaches.
lleed not his loud “ Dear me !” and “ Well, well, well!”
But make the tire of squashy ligs intenser
Yea, even till lie hastens him to sell
Caudles and censer.

Until he learns to walk as v ' think fitting 1
The Heathen of such joys, I a.a afraid,
Lives all unwitting.
Benighted islander ! He knoweth not
Tne ritual pouts on which we spend our judgment;
And if he did he’d use the word that “ Rot!”
(Or maybe “ Fudge !”) meant.

The model for the Melbourne statue of
Queen Victoria follows closely the proportions of
the departed lady. In the Ma Country it is an
accepted axiom that all monarchs look majestic.
Consequently sculptors who have to image forth a
Sovereign Lady of somewhat dumpy figure hire a
6ft. Gibson Girl as model. The Melbourne statue
compromises with the conventional rendering to
the extent of tricking out the Queen in crown,
robes, sceptre and orb. Considering the realism
of the figure, the statue would have been more
appropriately finished off with the bombazine gown
and garden hat of Victoria’s everyday life. That
famous garden hat would have looked well in
marble.
* • * •
According to Detective O’Donnell, whoso
sofa was recently blown up by a bomb, gambling
in Melbourne is practically unchecked at present.
The Chows let you mark tickets, but tell you
nothing, promise you nothing; and it’s impossible
to prove anything without a fearful rigmarole of
ticket-marking and endless law fights. The open-
air two-up schools must be arrested by warrant or
proceeded against by summons, and while the
watching constables get the warrants the school
casually disperses and meanders home talking of
the weather. The betting clubs defy the_ law,
and, when tackled, enter on an endless series of
appeals—gambling steadily the while. As to
Wren’s tote-shop, it’s an institution ; and Wren
is said by some to be making £5O a day out of it.
O’Donnell’s proposed remedy is to give the police
large powers of summary arrest. But that, at the
best, would be poor and ineffective. The law
should decide to regulate gambling—it being im-
possible to suppress it—by legalising the tote on
certain licensed racecourses and for those who
like a little flutter; but anathematise racing,
inaugurate a State lottery, with a 10 per cent,
commission to be devoted to liquidating the
public debt.
LUCK.
MISS A. (chattily): “ And which of you two had the luck to win the widow?”
MR. B.: “ Well, we divided it. Jones had the luck and I have the wdow.”
Adelaide is excited in a holy way over a
young m an with a broken neck, who lies, with
mind and memory quite clear, in the Home for
Incurables. The doctors say positively that the
fifth joint of the neck is fractured, and the pre-
vious record for living on in that condition is five
weeks. Yet this case has dragged on since a
trolly accident in September, 1904 ! The patient
is paralysed as to the lower part of the body, but
is fairly cheerful. _
_ . , ~
Those Australians who are shocked at the
idea of the Commonwealth attempting to create
an Australian navy, and who declare that we
couldn’t possibly make a success of the work of
undertaking our own defence by water, are called
upon to consider the cruiser Encounter, now on
her way to join the Australian Squadron. Every
time the Encounter has been called upon to per-
form at a pace beyond a cautious hobble she has
either ricked her spine or dislocated important
masses of gear. She has been patched and altered
and repaired at a cost of about £lOO,OOO, and at
length, when it was reasonable to conclude that
she was fit for nothing but to be sent to the boil-
ing-down works, the dear old country recollects
that it has bargained to supply Australia with
certain ships at a large annual rental, and the
Encounter is carefully nursed across the water to
keep that contract.
A STERN SOUL.
JAMES HENRY : “If yer break yer engagement wir me, Muriel Agnes, I’ll buy frec-pennorth of
beer an’ drink meself inter a early grave."
Ever since this writer, on St. Kilda pier
one perspiring day in the long ago, saw a massive
lady, of flabellate habit, with a balloon-like con-
tour and a stolid tread, step on a penny-in-the-
slot weighing machine, he has had doubts about
the veracity of that device. The pointer stopped
at lOst. 51b. without a blush, and the lady got
herself carefully off, smiling like a kookaburra.
The other day a Prahran (Vic.) weights-and-
measures inspector prosecuted the penny-in-the-
slot on the ground of inaccuracy. It was ex-
plained on behalf of the slot that a machine might
go wrong, but that it could be easily repaired.
Case dismissed ; and probably the machines will
be more accurate in future.
December number of the London Magazine
op Commerce advocates an extension of the
Law of Debt, giving more ample poweis
than at present for gaoling non-paying
debtors. Better to give less law into the hands of
the creditor. Unless where false pretences can be
proved, the transaction which makes creditor and
debtor is one purely between the pair, and they
should settle it between themselves.
The Irish R.C. objection to the English
R.C.—the Cartholic. as he dubs him with infinite
scovn _shows in Michael Davitt’s letter to Dublin
Freeman’s Journal, declaring that
the as ; n ; ne stupidity of the Irish Bishops in insti-
gating Mr. John Redmond to assist Mr. Balfour to enact
the Education Act of 1902 has been the chief cause of a
triumphant Radical majority being returned pledged to
secularise schools, but not pledged to Home Rule.
Of course, the Home Rulers didn t want the
Liberals to get a sweeping victory only a
moderate victory, which would leave them depen-
dent for a majority on the Irish JN ationalists.
Now, with the Nonconformist crowd antagonised
by Balfour’s concession of State aid to Anglican
and Roman Catholic schools, the lories can t
even muster an effective Opposition tor the Irish
party to play off against the Liberals; and
Michael Davitt asks bitterly why this thing
should be just to oblige a lot of English Car-
tholics,” who are mostly against Home Rule.
The same old benzine accident again. It’s
used for cleaning gloves and polishing the furni-
ture, and the other day a Castlemaine (Vic.) man
got his wife to rub his back with it to relieve an
internal ache. A lighted candle happened to be
alongside, the benzine caught fire, and the last
state of that man’s back was worse than the first.
* * * *
There was a great time in a Melbourne
suburb the other day when a local shopkeeper
took the hint from a recent biograph picture and
inserted an inch ad. for a wife in the morning
papers. By eight o’clock the applicants started
arriving, and as he took each one into his room to
examine her as to her qualifications, there was
soon a block. By ten, he had put through about
half-a-dozen, and there were still 20 waiting.
Meanwhile the crowd had got wind of what was
going on, and a couple of hundred on-
lookers gathered round the shop and criti-
cised the applicants audibly. Then the police
arrived, and just in time, for the applicants
had started to criticise each other. One expressed
the opinion that it showed some people’s cheek
when they expected to get married with such
faces, and the lady referred to retorted that at
any rate she was a respectable woman. There
was every probability of a free fight, when the
much-sought came out and announced that he was
suited. Eighteen ladies at once rose to the occasion,
laughed contemptuously in the face of the bride-
elect, and marched off saying that the bridegroom
was no gentleman, and if they’d only seen him
properly they wouldn’t have troubled waiting.
Most of the applicants were past their first youth
and not lovely, but they included a couple of
attractive, little, fringed suburban girls of 16 or
so and a staid widow of 50. The bride was a
bright-looking, intelligent girl, and the choice of
the advertiser showed that he kept his senses
about him to the end.
* * * *
The Central Post-office Bureau has practi-
cally decided that the toll system of telephones
will be started in Melbourne and Sydney on July
1 next. Under this system, each conversation
will be paid for. The idea just now is that pre-
sent subscribers will have the right to keep on at
their subscription rates, but all new connections
will have the toll tariff imposed. When results
are known, a general system will be introduced
As a compensation to the toll payers, their instru-
ments are to be connected with a common battery.
This will mean that conversations with the
“Hello” girls will be conducted without the
innumerable rings of the ordinary phone. In
place of the bell is a tiny electric bulb, which
glows when the phone is rung up or off. lhe
“ ring up ” for inattentive subscribers will be the
only tinkle the instrument will know —a great
advantage this to a busy office.
The Blue Lake, Mfc. Kosciusko, has at
last been sounded ; difficulty before was in getting
a raft from which to plumb, as the lake is far above
timber level 5 a rough canvas coracle was brought
up on a pack-horse. The lake had the reputation
of being of enormous depth—“bottomless the
Monaro men said. And the real depth is 75 feet .
Rodin’s bust of Jean Paul Laurens, the
French painter, and Clausen’s “Ploughmans
Breakfast” have now arrived at Melbourne to
complete Bernard Hall’s purchases from the F el ton
bequest. Doubtless some person whose training
consisted in handling fancy socks will cry out in
the Akgus that something dreadful has happened
after he has seen the Rodin. Clausens work is
■just a good, solid picture, with no special mean-
ing It is an English country scene, with horses
and plough, and two men sitting under a tree-
after Constable, but a long way after. It has
some solid qualities, is conscientiously felt, and
well painted ; but the color m places is a little
cheap; and it is by no means a modern master-
piece. On the whole, Hall bought well; for he
kept to a definite scheme of buying only repre-
sentative works. Clausen is not violently repre-
sentative, but that was a slip that can be excused.
For Clausen is going to buy the next batch ot
Felton pictures, and it is just as well to humor
him. _ „
* * * *
A casual Australian story was told at an
inquest at Charlton (Vic.), a week or two back.
The corpse was named Jack. He had left his ship
at Geelong, and at Maryborough had met Bill and
Jim 'Hie three tramped together for five weeks,
and, coming to the Avoca River, tned to swim
across. Halfway over, Jack sank. Bill and Jim
concluded this to be a display of humor, and
grunted in the bushman’s attempt to laugh.
Jack’s joke was protracted ; he stayed under
water for a minute and a-half. Then Jim dived,
and brought him up for the inquest..

ing to the British public for subscriptions to
enable him to continue his “good old custom

charge has thus dwindled down to £1 a head tor
30 old men at Xmas.
• * *
In Westralia it is suggested that the vacant
Judgeship should be filled from England. The
last creation, Justice M'Millan, came from the
Cold Country. The difficulty is not so much the
dearth of local talent as other considerations.
Attorney-General Moss, who had no claims to
preferment, was offered the post so that
he might decline it. Of the other eligibles
Mr. Gus. Roe, city P.M., though a lawyer, forgot
most of his law while pearl-shelling, and the police-
court bench is not a good qualifying ground, any-
way ; Mr. “Dicky” Haynes. K.C.., is supposed
to lack dignity, and no one else, except perhaps
Solicitor-General Sayer, K.C., is supposed to be
amongst the first flight in the running. It was
the Barristers’ Board that suggested a second
importation from England. But what about the
last judicial importation from the Cold Country !
It was hardly a brilliant success.
*v* * *
J. D. Campbell, M.L.A., of Bananaland,
rather wasted both energy and cash on his trip to
England if the only thing that impressed him
there was “the disparity which exists between the
conditions of the wealthy and the poor and the
unequal treatment they receive.” Why have gone
further than, say, Melbourne to find that !
Henley-on-the-Yarra affords a type of the treat-
ment of the wealthy. Once a year some unknown
authority filches from the public the north and
south banks of the Yarra opposite the winning
post, and encloses them in barricades of galvanised
iron'to form reserves for “immaculately gowned
society ladies” (Age, 30/10/’O5) to the rigid exclu-
sion of the poor general public, to whom the
banks belong, but who might contaminate the
“nice” folk. Next day (Age, 31/10/’O5) notice
the treatment of the poor. Ten unhappy little
boys prosecuted at Fitzroy for playing m the
Edinburgh gardens on Sunday with a rag football.
Batson, town clerk and mouthpiece of Fitzroy’s
aristocracy, states that
The commmittee had made the lawn for the use of
children (rich children !) and not for a lot of hoodlums
(young Australia) to play rough and tumble upon.
So his worship the Mayor discharges the ten
criminals with a severe caution, and announces
that the bench intends to put a stop to this sort of
thing in future. In Australia, just as much as in
England, Campbell, M.L.A., will find that the
rich flourish, and the poor are persecuted.
“COMMITTED.“
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Committed to Asylum ”
Quick, curt, and precise ;
The clerk takes the papers to file ’em —
All’s done in a trice.
Out goes some poor devil, half-witted,
They’ve dealt with him well;
He’s done with at last ; he’s “ committed ”
Committed to hell.
In the yard with the rest of his brot hers
He sits in his gloom ;
They’ll stow him away with the others,
Wherever there’s room.
He’ll have company—oh,, never doubt it!
If he isn’t mad yet,
There’ll soon be no question about it,
From the chances he’ll get.
Committed ! The cross of his madness
Is nailed to his back,
All prospects of sweetness or gladness
Are torn from his track.
He’ll find his way down to his level
Before very long,
For the keeper who’ll thump out his devil
Is stalwart and strong.
VIC.
LAM.
BEAUTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beauty was horn of the world’s desire,
Of laughing water and flaming fire.
She treads the Earth, and the flowers spring sweet
Under the arch of her dainty feet.
The Spring of the year is in her veins,
Where pulses the passion of suns and rains ;
Out of the breast of a crimson flower
Her lips were fashioned in one sweet hour.
Out of the winds were made her feet,
Lovely, elusive, and white and fleet.
Out of a cloud was made her hair —
Heavy and black, with the storm held there.
What is her name? There’s none that knows.
Mother o’ Mischief or Month o’ Rose ?
Where is she going ? but none can tell,
She climbs to heaven or falls to hell.
W. A. P.

The WILD CAT
COULUMN
It is almost inconceivable that the A.M.P.
directors will pursue business in Britain and S’Africa on
the vote recently taken. They called on members to
give them certain powers, and only 95,336 votes assented.
That is, only about one in every six gave the directors
the authority they sought. True, if they stand on their
legal rights, having got the necessary majority of “ Yes”
votes over “No” votes, they can extend the business.
But, as directors of a mutual society, they presumably
vvon’t stand on narrow legal rights. That is the one
simple fact they have to wrestle with : they sought
proxies from members representing over 500,000 votes,
and all they got in their favor was 95,336. If they accept
that as sufficient authority, they would accept almost
anything.
The directors ought to say promptly whether
they intend, in view of this miserable response to their
request for proxies, to go on with a business for which
they have got only one-sixth of the members to express
favor. If they persist, then it will be quite open for the
holders of the other 400,000 votes to record their opinion
between now and March 9, when the confirmatory meet-
ing will be held. The proxies already sent in will stand
good for that meeting; presumably between now and
March 9 some definite decision will be arrived at with
regard to other proxies. But the directors, in view of
the previous persistent refusal of members and the
present miserable response to their application for
authority, ought to make an announcement rendering
any furtherappeal to members unnecessary.
“Disgusted’’ opens up a large question :— “I
am a policy-holder in the Australian Widows’Fund. We
have on our paid staff Dr. Marshall, minister of Scots
Church, Oollins-street, Melbourne ; Mr. A. Dick, chair-
man of Dr. Marshall’s church committee ; Senator Simon
Fraser, bosom friend of both ; and Rev. Keith Mackay, a
peregrinating Congregational minister. The first three
are directors; the last is chief inspector. Board fees
work out at about £6 per man per week, with £2 2s. for
the members of the local boards. Over two-thirds
of the interest goes in expenses. The A.M.P.. wants
to extend its operations. Could it not take
the Widows’ Fund over bodily? Tell me—How many
policy-holders have left the Wid'ows’ Fund Society by (a)
death, (b) maturity of Policy of Endowment, (c) 'surren-
ders, ( d ) forfeitures for the years ’O2, ’O3, ’O4, ’05.”
Something might be done with profit in life-
office amalgamation. There is a shocking multiplication
of little concerns, and a shocking proportion of themonev
which ought to reach the widow or the insurer in his old
age is supporting unnecessary directors and superin-
tendents and other people who mostly have too little of
the business to do to know much about it, and in pro-
viding rent for unnecessary offices. Besides three or four
societies that are doing a mixed medical benefit and in-
surance business, and the three great American offices,
and the Red Indian concern, there are no less than 11
offices struggling to take the life of Australasia; and
the struggle is all the mere tremendous because 60 per
cent, of the whole receipts in Australia reach one society.
There are three offices—the A.M.P., Citizens’, arid
National Mutual—which in 1904 were able to add 35
per cent, of their receipts to their funds; while the
aggregate additions of all the other offices were only 24
per cent, of their receipts, Seventy-four per cent, of the
whole life-assurance income reached these three societies ;
all the rest of them (nearly a dozen, doing life assurance
business in some form or other) struggled for the other 26
per cent, And each of these competitors has its boards
of directors (one of them alone has 33 directors), its staffs,
its offices, its brass-plates, and its advertisements.
According to latest official figures, the premium income
and management cost of some of the offices are as follow
(ordinary business only)
Premium Manage-
Income. ment.
A-M.P. .. ~ ~ £1,763,993 £237,293
Citizens’.. .. ... 209,698 31,894
National Mutual . , 487,385 128,570
Total three offices .. £2,461,076 £397,757
Widows’Fund .. .. 184,246 49,543
T. & G 65,320 18,302
Aus. Metrop. (mix;ed)., 4,652 13,436
Total three offices .. £254,118 £81,281
If, in proportion to their premium income, the manage-
ment of the latter three offices had cost the same as in the
former three, the total, instead of being £81,281, would
have amounted to just over £40,000. In other words,
about £41,000 more, which went in management, would
have gone to the policy-holders in bonuses ; and in that
case the reversionary bonuses would have been trebled.
Whether any large office would take over the
businesses of several smaller offices is, however, doubt-
ful. Similar proposals have been made several times in
America, and the reply has been—“ Yes, but only on a
re-examination and re valuation of the risks.” The theory
is that the poorer the office and the less its bonuses, the
worse its risks ; the healthy members who can get insur-
ance elsewhere get it and drop out of the poorer office,
which is thus left with the sediment. What is the rate
of surrender and forfeiture in the Widows’ Fund, to
which the correspondent above-quoted refers, it is
impossible to say ; like a good many other
offices, the Widows’ Fund blazons in its reports
the number of new policies issued during the year,
but doesn’t mention the other highly important matter of
lapses and surrenders. Note this, however : In 1903 poli-
cies were issued for £425,254, yielding an annual income
of £16,606. In 1904 the claims totalled £152,266, or only
about a third of the amount of the new policies; so,
roughly, the premium income should have increased by
about two-thirds of £16,606. Instead, however, of in-
creasing, the premium income (apart from new premiums
for 1904) actually fell from £160,848 to £159,097 ; so, in
addition to surrenders, there must have been severe
lapses. And the insurance that lapses because the office
isn’t fascinating enough to cause the policy-holder to
struggle to keep his policy in force, is more pathetic even
than the insurance which only carries a small bonus be-
cause the expense rate is too big.
If policy-holders want amalgamation, they will
have to fight for it themselves ; for the directors won’t.
The position is the same as it is in connection with the
“ Greater Sydney ” or “Greater Melbourne” movement.
You needn’t look to the 47 Sydney suburban mayors to
agitate for a scheme, no matter how good, which means
their own disappearance. And you needn’t look to the
directors either.

H. Y. L. Brown, Government Geologist of
S.A., on return from a long trip about the north coasts,
tells an Adelaide interviewer (Register, Jan. 4th): “My
opinion is, as it always has been, that the Northern Terri
tory has never had a fair trial in the way of mineral de-
velopment. They talk about the thousands of pounds
spent there by English companies. It has never been
spent in true mining, but chiefly wasted on the surface.
. . . There are some good-lookingcopper and tin shows,
but the country is a gold country.” Brown selected a
spot at Port Keats where it might be advisable to bore
for coal, and says that any that may be got will be valu-
able, of the Newcastle class.

The refusal of English holders of over £1,500,000
worth of Victoria’s 4 per cent. Treasury bonds to take
advantage of their option of conversion into Bent’s 3£per
cent. Consolidated Stock at 103 gives Australia a glimpse
of rocks ahead. The one unconscious good the Stinking
Fish crowd is doing this loan-drunk land is in scaring Bull
off supplying further tipple. Should that party’s efforts
culminate in Australia being unable to get the price of
“a permanent and reproductive ” beer at anything under
25 per cent., it will be a happy result in some respects.
Though the Daily Telegraph kindly gave it
permission, the Royal Bank (Melb.) won’t expand to
Sydney just yet. Negotiations broke off when they were
nearly ripe.
