Previous to the writing down of capital the profit made
by buying old deposit receipts at a discount was turned
into a special reserve, and used eventually to write down
assets. Latterly less buying has been done ; and what
profit has been made has apparently been used to write
down assets internally. Some “B” deposits (repayable
1912 and 1917, or earlier, after May, 1907, at the bank’s
option) have apparently been exchanged for inscribed
deposit stock: —
Dec. ’O4. Dec. 'O5.
Deposit stock .. £788,267 £791,805 Inc. £3,538
A&B deposits .. 3,581,683 3,290,988 Dec. £290,695
The reduction of A and B deposits is mainly the result
of clearances of A deposits under the. scheme, by which
about £254,000 is repayable annually. Only one more
batch remains now to be paid. In the way of repurchase,
very little has been done in the past six months; only
£3336 has been wiped out. And it isn’t because B
deposits have gone back to face value. In fact, their last
quote was 13s. in the £ ; yet that is better than the 11s.
9d. of twelve months ago.
What are the prospects for these B deposits,
amounting to just over £3,000,000? They carry 3 per
cent, and are repayable in 1912 and 1917. Take first the
advances as written down and now amounting to
£4,073,106. Allowing £6OOO per half-year as being earned by
British Consols (£195,700), London bank balances
(£303,389), and other liquid items, the advances last six
months earned gross. £104,323, after providing for bad
and doubtful debts on new business only. On the mean
of the advances this was at the rate of £2 10s. 6d. per
cent, per annum. And that is gross. Now a moderate
gross-earnings rate would be £4 per cent. To get the.
assets down to that basis the advances would have to be
written down to £2,545,700. Add premises (written
down to the same extent) £229,000, and liquid assets
£1,004,502, and you have assets £3,779,202. Deduct
£1,693,000, which is the amount of outstanding A deposits
and interest and new deposits and other liabilities which
are a prior charge on all assets f and you have £2,086,000
available to pay B deposits, amounting to £3,037,000.
These are merely estimates and valuations, of course, and
affect the value of B deposit receipts only. As for new
deposits and other first-charge liabilities, the calculation
means that they could be met and leave £2,086,000 over.
As for current business :
Dec., ’O4. Dec., 'O5.
Notes.. .. £62,568 £66,687 Inc. £4,119
Bills, &o. .. 131,543 156,934 Inc. 25,391
New Deposits 992,680 1,150,018 Inc. 157,338
Liquid Assets 1,273,291 1,247,551 Dec. 25,740

The reduction of liquid assets in the face of a fall in
advances and a growth of deposits is due to the paying-off
of A deposits (3 per cent.) mentioned above. The liquid
assets (largely unproductive) represent 87 per cent, of the
new debts, and that absolutely bars good profits, so the
dead-horse can’t be lifted that way. Yet the A.J.S.
Bank’s old depositors, and the modern gamblers in the
old receipts, have this hope : the A.J.S. plunged heaviest
in pastoral securities, and given a run of four or five good
seasons and high prices, no sort of security can recover
so rapidly.

This bank’s profits continue very steady ; but they should
show an increase in order to justify the higher dividend
which the directors unwisely began to pay at the
beginning of 1905. The amount put by last half-year to
cover the accidents that will happen ‘in the best regu-
lated banks is only 8 per cent, of the profits, which is by
far the smallest rate among Australian banks of issue.
It is highly probable that the recent profits of Australian
banks generally will not be maintained (several of them,
in fact, show a shrinkage already), and the danger is that,
in an attempt to maintain the dividend—for the reduc
tion of a div. is one of the hardest things for a bank to
bring itself to do—either business will be forced, or
insufficient provision made for doubtful accounts. Of
course the Bank of Victoria may have been making - extra
provision of the latter sort a couple of years ago or there-
abouts, so that the true profits then may have been higher
than they look ; but that is only barely possible. Apart
from this flaw, matters look fairly cheerful. Make a
comparison :

Advances have increased, though not quite to the same
extent as deposits at interest. Generally speaking, a
growth of advances, when the money is available, is a
good sign ; but prices of securities are high now, and a
management which couldn’t resist the Temptation to put
up the dividend may not be ’able either to resist
the temptation to make advances on puffed-up values.
So, under the circumstances, the growth of advances falls
under suspicion, too. From a depositor’s point of view
the position is, however, still strong, because the share-
holders’ capital is large. Debts to the publio amount to
£5,990,305 ; liquid assets total £2,105,592 ; and against
the debt of £3,884,713 not covered by liquid assets there
are non-liquid assets valued at £5,571,189. All non-liquid
assets could therefore shrink 31 percent., and the deposi-
tors’ and other public money would still be intact. By
reason of this large proportion of shareholders’ money in
the business, profits show up rather small; on the basis
of capital and reserves, the movement has been

In proportion to its own funds, the Royal of Queensland
has for many years past had very little public money to
work with. Even now, though the deposits have grown,
there is only 375. of public money in the business for
every 20s. of the shareholders’ money, whereas the Bank
of New South Wales, for instance, has about £7 10s. of
the public’s for every £1 of its own money. The bank
has to stand the racket of a slump in the securities into
which it puts its depositors’ money, and the heavier
the proportion of such money thus invested, the
larger the risk; on the other hand, the more
of the public’s money the bank can use, the larger
the profits for the shareholders. Having so little help
from public money, the Royal's earnings are compara-
tively small; recently, on the basis of shareholders’
funds (capital and reserves) profits have been at this
rate
June., 1902 .. £3 17 0 p.c. June, 1904 .. £3 10 7 p.c.

Dec., 1903 .. 3 13 0 „ Dec., 1905 .. 3 15 7 „
But in spite of these rather poor figures, the business
appears to be very sound. All assets show average gross
earnings of £4 6s. per cent, per annum; and, as a
reasonable 37 per cent, of the public’s money is still repre-
sented by liquid assets, in spite of recent movements, this
rate of earnings ought to mean that there are very few
unproductive assets in that office. The liquid assets
could pay all but £609,065 of the public’s claims ; and to
meet that £609,065 there are these other very good-
looking but non-liquid assets totalling £1,142,492. Prom
the depositors’ point of view this is a strikingly strong
position.

That is how the matter stands on a net-profit basis. But
it might be made to look a good-deal better :
Profits for the year .. .. .. .. £7333

This co. has its reserves split up as follows: —Reserve
fund, £23,500 ; dividend equalisation, £2500 ; commission
reserved account, £16,602 ; undivided profit, £993. The
commission reserved account apparently consists of com-
missions on estates in hand but not realised ; and this has
been drawn upon during the year to the extent of £6OB.
For some years past many estates have been going into
the co.’s hands as trustee, and it has apparently passed a
portion of the commission on corpus or capital value (this
commission runs from 1 to 2£ per cent.) to this reserved
account. In addition to corpus commission, the trustee
co. charges a commission of 2£ per cent, on income. In
some cases this 2J per cent, does not cover current
expenses of management; and a co. which passes the
whole corpus commission to profit and loss without wait-
ing for realisation (as some co.’s do) is therefore liable to
find itself later on showing a loss. It looks as if during
the past year this co. has had to go to its reserved com-
missions to eke out the com. on income in some cases ;
and, if any additions have been made to this reserved
account, they have been less than the aid called for from
that quarter. This, of course, throws some doubt not
only upon the reserves as shown above (seeing that more
calls upon the reserved account may have to be made in
the future), but also upon the profits for recent years as
shown above. Another way of showing the case follows :
Current Commissions Written
Profits. Reserved. Off.

The point is that the reserved commissions are not fully
earned and are to some extent doubtful; so that the
heavy increase in profits shown in the first table presents
the co.’s recent history (prior to 1905) in too favorable a
light. Nevertheless, on the published figures, it appears
to be the only way to present it. The trust-estate
balances amount to £5,829,396, as against £5,589,194 in
1904 and £2,424,413 in 1895 ; expenses were £10,905 against
£10,096 in 1904, being at the rate of just under 3s. 9d. per
cent, of trust balances, as against a fraction over 3s. 7d.
the previous year. The current profits, as shown in profit
and loss, were at the rate of £l3 15s. 9d. per cent, on
shareholders’ funds (including reserved commissions) in
1904 and £lO 11s. 7d. per cent, in 1905.

Dec., ’05..56,565 10 „ ~42,860 4,841 4660 675,000
The reserves are made up of share-premiums £466,535,
meter renewal reserve £21,036, formal reserve £84,570,
undivided profit £2859. Apart from what has been
contributed by shareholders in the shape of premiums,
the reserves therefore amount to £108,465. The amounts
shown above as written off are in respect of
meter renewals and stoves ; the latter is tantamount
to a subsidy on the use of stoves, the rents being insuf-
ficient to directly recoup the co. Besides these writings
off, and apart from meters and stoves, £16,746 was
charged to profit and loss for repairs, renewals, and main-
tenance, equivalent to lj per cent, per annum on the
capital expenditure. This amount varies little; 12
months ago, it was £16,165 ; six months ago, £16,924.
Nothing, however, is being written off the capital
value of the co.’s fixed property (mains, machinery, &c.).
Therefore if the writings-off, plus addition to works on*,
of revenue, do not fully counterbalance the depreciation
of plant through rust and wear-and-tear, then the reserves
to some unknown extent don’t really exist. The whole
capital in the undertaking returns £6 13s. 6d. per cent •
and of this capital £848,000 is 6 per cent, debentures’
which leaves the co. £ll3s. 6d. per cent, on this borrowed
money for profit and for depreciation not charged to profit
and loss. Shareholders’ own funds show a return of £6
11s. 9d. per cent. net. This is substantial, but it is small
potatoes compared with the £lO Bs. percent, shown by
the Sydney co.—a co. which hasn’t, however, borrowed to
the same extent, and probably is in the unique position
for a gas co. of having written down its fixed assets below
their value, so that its reserves rather under-state than
over-state the case. This, in fact, suggests the possible
weak spot in the Melbourne co.—the doubt whether rust
and decay aren’t getting ahead of the balance-sheet. If
they are, then either reserves or current profits will have
to be heavily drawn upon at some future time to make
extraordinary renewals.

This little co. has investments which last year returned
just over 6| per cent, gross, or 5f per cent, net, on the
capital of £6OOO. Claims amounted to only 25 per cent,
of net premiums ; at that figure or thereabouts they
remain fairly constant. Expenses, apart from income-tax
and up-keep, etc., of premises, were £1336, as against
£1259 the previous year ; expenses now amount to 50 per
cent, of premiums—50 per cent, expenses, 25 per cent,
claims, 25 per cent, profit; a nice, comfortable position.
The investments, in which it is possible to put almost
every 7 penny of the capital and reserves, are a mixed lot,
comprising mortgages, debentures, deposit receipts, and
property.

This luckiest co. in all Australia—a co. whose ferries are.-
fed at both ends by a State tramway system—is now,
adopting further means to make its profits look less con.,
spicuous. There are at present 140,000 shares, a large.-
part of them representing water, and, though the £li
shares were at about £3 in the market before any new
schemes were outlined, the remaining 60,000 shares are tot
be issued to present shareholders at par—2o,ooo straight,
away, 40,000 at the directors’ discretion. So the share-
holders will get a present of £120,000, equal, when all are'
distributed, to nearly £1 per present share. The profit,
and loss account sets the profits for the half-year at
£6986 ; but add to that £6OOO by which the reserve for
special repairs and new steamers has been increased.
Certainly a reserve like this might in some cases not be a
genuine reserve, and the additions to it might not be
counted as profit; but in this case it is obvious that,
quite apart from this reserve, large provision is made
for depreciation. The new ship Kareela, the report men-
tions, was taken over in September ; yet ship property is
in the balance-sheet now at £57,932, whereas six months
ago it was in at £61,326. So also, though large additions
to landed property 7 have been made, this item is in at
£88,408 as against £87,756 six months ago—an addition of
only £652. Into these items it is evident that profits are
being stowed. So much profit has been used in writing
down the assets that the total assets, at book value, show
a return of just upon 15 per cent, per annum on the basis
of the profits shown above; even if the co.’s £6986 be
taken (that is, assuming that the £6OOO set aside was
required to cover depreciation, in spite of the ordinary
writings-down), the return on all assets is just about
8 per cent. But the 15 per cent, is probably much
nearer the mark. There is evidence of this in another
annnounceinent; after this new-share scheme goes
through, a special bonus of 3s. per share will be paid
(March 31), as “ certain funds which have been set aside
will be set free,” the new capital being “sufficient to
provide for new boats and the furtherance of the co.’s
developments.” Evidently the “ reserve for special
repairs and new steamers ” (to which the £6OOO was added
last half-year) is to be divided. Practically the shareholders
will draw out a little more than they pay in for the new
shares ; in other words, this reserve is really going to be
capitalised—which shows that it is undivided profit. And,
if the £6OOO added to it last half-year was profit like the
rest, then the assets earned 15 p.c. p.a. as stated above.
The co. is to be congratulated on being located in a city
which is apparently content to provide such profits on
public services.

has turned the previous balance to a debit of £1775
before paying the dividend. Probably the business is as
profitable as ever ; but wear and tear is a tremendous
iteiil, and as to whether sufficient allowance is made for
it the irianagement’s word has to be taken. And in this
fcasti the management’s word isn’t as definite as it is
desirabie it should be.

But there is a doubt about some of these reserves. The
amount shown in the last line above is made up in this
way: Formal reserve £105,000, depreciation reserve
£87,511, undivided profit £89,570. The depreciation
reserve, to which £12,240 has been added during the year,
most likely covers only actual depreciation ; in that case,
the year’s profits were £BO9O, and the total reserves are
£194,570. This plan of puffing up the figures in a balance-
sheet by keeping assets at their original cost, instead of
using the depreciation fund to write them down to their
real value, is one that wants hitting with something hard
and heavy. Also as long as some co.’s provide only for
wear and tear which has occurred, while others provide
out of their fatness for the wear and tear of the future,
when things may not be so fat, one can never know for
certain what a depreciation reserve is really worth. The
Q.M.E. Co. has made some progress during the year, but
not much. A little under three years ago it plunged
into northern cattle holdings, and piled up debt whole-
sale ; and the chief feature of the year is the reduction
of some of these items :
1902. 1903. 1901. 1905.
Overdraft .. .. Nil £56,059 £118,728 £72,100
Creditors, secured £26,617 86,905 72,379 66,812
Creditors, sundry 20,136 28,952 27,013 20,998
Total.. .. £46,783 £171,916 £218,150 £160,210
The pastoral properties, including stock, into which
about £150,000, was put, were valued 12 months ago at
£165,023 ; they have now been reduced to £150,484, ap-
parently 7 by 7 sale of stock* The bulk of the debt-reduction
has, however, been effected through realising products ;
this item has been reduced from £151,795 to £116,619.
The shrinkage of the overdraft has saved something solid
in interest; and there appears to have been a general
reduction of management and general expenses —£17,429
was in 1904 charged to profit and loss, and in the past
year only £8166, which accounts for the whole of
the net profit (assuming that the net profit is the
amount of profit less the sum transferred to de-
preciation reserve). The future hangs upon the seasons
and the foreign meat market; and what they are likely
to be can’t be told from post-mortem- ing a balance sheet.
The co., in short, went into debt about £150,000 (and still
owes about £120,000) for the purpose of plunging in almost
the biggest gamble in Australia ; and there is little infor-
mation as to how the gamble is shaping. All that the direc-
tors say is, “The increase of stock is satisfactory 7 .” There
Were aboiit 65,000 cattle 12 months before. Now, appar-
ently, about 8000 have been sold, The natural increase
should exceed that; yet station properties (with stock)
are in this year at £15,000 less than in 1901. There is no
sign of “satisfactory increase” there. Or has it been
necessary to write down stock, even though they cost
only £2 per head? Possibly the products which have
been realised during the year were bought at boom-prices ;
if so, it would, of course, affect the year’s profits. What-
ever the explanation, the year’s results appear to be very
disappointing. * * *

* * * *
Great Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), needs watch-
ing. Expert Rolker said six months ago that ore reserves
were worth 80s. per ton ; but the actual return for July
(just after- Rolker’s visit) was only 665. 3d., with a profit
of £38,400. In December it was down to 51s. 3d., and the
profit was £30,000. Directors cable that “ the method of
sloping prevents the maintenance of a regular output,
and fluctuations must occur. Stoping has proved ore in
mine greater than C. M. Rolker’s estimate. But an esti-
mate of tonnage is worthless if estimate of values is ig-
nored. As to stoping, the same method has been in vogue
for two years past. Between Jan. and Sept, output fluctu-
ated between 13,6580 z. and 13,6670 z.; then it fell away to
12,5590 z., 12,4820 z. and 11,7900 z. There have thus been no
fluctuations, but a continued drop. The shaft has followed
the lode on the underlay for 300 ft. below 1270 ft., and it
is worthless all the way. The shoot is pitching north,
and, at No. 11 level, was not reached till 173 ft. were
driven, and became poor after 307 ft. of pay 7 ore. At No.
12 the shoot was entered 475 ft. north of crosscut, and
only 120 ft. were payable. At No. 13 quite 600 ft. will have
to be driven in barren country, and the split in the reef,
which cuts off values, will be reached in that distance.
By reducing grade mine may spin out for some y ears yet,
but the recent developments have been very unsatis-
factory. ,
Efforts are being niarie to boom East Fingall
and Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.). Main shaft
on former is down 500 ft., and on latter 800 ft., and cross-
cutting for the reef is now proceeding'. Both properties
are ancient wild cats, but are strongly tipped by the
London press. Gaudy reports are issued periodically,
and, last September, East Fingalls were dealt in at 6s. on
a report that a reef had been cut at 413 ft., worth 71du t.
for 3ft. wide. Nothing has been heard of that reef since.
East Fingall is in 400,000 shares of ss. each, and co. should
have a good deal of cash, as a lot of shares have been
placed within the past few months. Murchison Asso-
ciated is in 500,000 shares of ss. each, of which 50,000 are
in reserve. Mere gambles both.
* » * *
Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie, has its main shaft down
1640 ft., and has started its No. 13 level at 1523 ft. Owing
to the discovery of pay ore in Great Boulder’s north block,
the Ivanhoe is continuing the north drives at Nos. 7, b,, J
and 10 levels, all of which had been suspended, as the
country was blank in the upper levels, and the lode was
supposed to have petered out. The 14 Wilfley slime
concentrators are working very satisfactorily, and, as it is
found that four furnaces cannot treat all the concentrates,
a fifth is being added. The management is admittedly
first-class; nevertheless either extraction is poor, oi
values of ore reserves ate over-estimated, as returns
only show 555. per ton, while estimates were 78s.

■ After the mine had been worked for over two
years the 10-head mill at Hannans Main Reef was started
in November, and first crushing- panned out 540 tons,
worth 235. 4d., and the second 900 tons worth 225. lOd.
When the mine was taken over, some 400 tons were
treated for 655. a ton, and shareholders were led to believe
that this was the average of the mine. Three levels were
driven, and the first two were said to carry a reef 4tt.
wide worth 20dwt., and, in the bottom level, the reef was
said to be 2ft. wide, worth 70dwt. The crushings mock
these optimistic assertions. Mine is equipped with a 10-
head capable of crushing 12C0 tons monthly, and there is
an ample water supply ; so it appears to be the old story
of the mill knocking the bottom out of the mine.

Great Tower Hill, Leonora (W.A.), has had its
first crushing, 11760 z. from 3360 tons. Ore is woith
255. 7d., and costs on mine are 21s. 4d.,-leaving 4s. 3d.
per ton, or £730, for profit and outside exes. As co. is in
1 000,000 shares of ss. each, of which 930,000 are issued
paid to 4s. 9d., scrip as an investment is dear at any
price. Co. is successor to Standard Exploration Co., and
is ridiculously capitalised. Lode is very big, and, it
properly handled, with a 200-head mill, might make a
name for itself; but, under existing conditions, equipped
with the Paddington Consols rattletrap 40-head, it is a
delusion.

Waihi, Davy hurst (W.A.), is looking much
more promising, and is earning a profit of *4OO to £5OO
monthly. If directors continue to allow Manager Watt
to spend profits in improving the equipment and develop-
ing the mine, he has hopes of eventually earning divs.
The millis doing very good work ; for a month the 10-

20-head. The Waihi is ill a belt of country parallel to the
Pole, and is traversed by numerous reefs and leaders,

district has earned a bad name, which, to a certain extent,
it deserves. But Watt is a genuine grafter, and Ins lead

* * * *
Bayley’s, Coolgardie, now in the hands of
tributors, may yet give shareholders some sort of a run
for their money. At the north end of the big outcrop
tributors struck rich stone at 70ft., and have followed it
down to 90ft., where it is still rich. In 1899 the co. got a
hunch of 9 tons, which yielded 9940 z., in shallow ground
at the same place, but, on the gold cutting out, work was
abandoned. The present tribute party won 20/oz. from
127 tons, but the stone they are now on is much richer.
There are still four months of the tribute to run before
the co. regains possession. Mine is so patchy that it is
quite possible the tributors will get the cream and leave
only skimmed milk co.
Once famous Lady Shenton, Menzies (W.A.),
has been sold for £9OO. The defunct co. won about
120 OOOoz. from 60,000 tons, and paid £160,000 in divs.
All'the gold was won above a vertical 400 ft. The lowest
level (366 ft.) was so. poor that manager Rayfield decided
it was useless to sink deeper, and his successor, laulkner,
said ditto. The Queensland Menzies lias also been con-
demned at a vertical depth of 300 ft. Had the majority of
the Kalgoorlie mines been treated in this way, they would
have been closed down years ago. With kidney lodes it
is ridiculous to give the job best when one kidney is
finished. ° Take, for instance, the Kalgurli. The pay
shoot is one huge kidney, about 150 ft. long, and up to
nearly 100 ft. wide at the widest. The ore peters out
below 1050 ft., but, in sinking the shaft, a second kidney
has been cut at 1130 ft., and may prove as big as the first.
A Melbourne syndicate now holds the Shenton, and
should go in for deeper sinking. #
The royalty curse is having some attention
again in Vic., and in particular at Ballarat. But some-
how the people who make the complaints don t get over-
much sympathy, because the outside public knows that
they don’t attack the thing on principle, but because it
affects tlieir profits. And that takes the heart out of
the agitation so far as the public is concerned. The
Ballarat royalty kings are the Pearces. Their agreement
with North Woah , Hawp provides not only for a royaltj,
but that the co. shall use their battery. This arrange-
ment has been going on for some 20 years, and the co.
lias naid £20,722 in royalty and £63,000 for crushing,
while divs. have aggregated £36,000. The New Nor-
manby has contributed £15,000 to the Pearces. Another
royalty concern, is the Guiding Star, which holds the
nroDrietarv rights over the Star of the East and the
South Sto. Minister M«Leod has flourished the toma-
hawk over other royalty proposals, notably the Hustlers
No. 1 at Bendigo, where he refused to. allow the co. to
take a tribute from the Hustler’s Reef, saying that the
royalty ic proposed to pay was too heavy. As a result,
some Ballarat owners are beginning to wish McLeod any-
anywhere except in office. If he were a stronger man he
would now knock out the dummying crowd as well.
Thousands of dredging acres are being held without a
cent being spent, and scores of miles of deep lead country
as well. But McLeod isn’t likely to interfere m that
direction, although the one evil leads up to the other.
No tears will he dropped if he stops the royalty evil at
Ballarat. But his action won’t put gold into the stone,
or sweeten the savor of the Ballarat mining crowd. And
both those things are big troubles. '

If Bright (Vic.) is cheerful over bucket-dredging,
Castlemaine is more cheerful over hydraulic-sluicing.
Seven years have gone since the Campbell s Creek co. ;
began the game, and.learnt that it vyasn t what Maon-
landers cracked it up to be. Finally, it slung the pump
and went in for the bucket, and to-day is pay nig divs.
with the added honor of being the 'only bucket-dredge on
the field. Others, joined in the hunt, sticking ,to the
pump ; plants were gradually altered to suit the ground ,
share registers were boiled down ; and now .son 1 ® , of the
nicest little dividend-payers in the State win their gold
from the site of the Castlemaine rushes, made famous by
their richness in the “glorious fifties. In all, 73,0000 z.
have been got out of the claims in the seven..years, over
£lOO,OOO has been spent on machinery, and nearly £40,000
has been paid in divs. Not a bad record ; it shows what
can be done where the management is local and the con-
ditions suit the industry.
* * *
A left-handed gamble is going on over the Lord
Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vic.). Co. is the only one in the dis-
trict to pay. divs., over £250,000 profits having been dis-
tributed. Speculators have poked shafts down north and
south, but all for naught. Now the game is to
wait for the reef in the underlies; a patient and
competent crowd is doing this in the Lord Nelsom North.
One policy for the Lord Nelson, therefore, would be to
wait for the North to get its shaft down, and to see that
it fulfilled labor conditions, while the Lord Nelson itself
continued to plug away at its own upper forma-
tions. But the ■ Lord Nelson manager has recom-
mended going down 250 ft., and this advice is to be taken.
Of course, if the shoot pitches fast to the north,
and makes a, little west, the advice is sound. It may be
sound,| too, On the score that it is best to know one s fate.
From the evidence in the last two lifts, it seems indis-
putable that Lord Nelson' North will get the south end
of the shoot —where the gamble comes in is in estimating
how much. The Lord Nelson, by pegging out ground
north of the North, cut down the latter’s chances. Any-
way, the obvious policy for the Lord Nelson peop.e, if
they wish to keep the upper hand,, is.to build up a
reserve in order to sink still deeper, so that they can pick
dp the shoot on their new north lease. With funds in
hand and ground there, the gamble may lose much of its
risk. , . /
• * *
Specimen Hill, Bendigo, looks like emerging
from the darkness into which it went upon the eclipse of
the New Hopeful. Situated mid-way between two such
good mines as the Virginia and the New Argus, it should,
taking ordinary mining risks into account, have got on to
the continuation of the good stone worked by the Mew
Argus. New Hopeful, which is over-lapped by Specimen
Hill, went first for the formation, and got it. But not
the gold ; and there was a hustle out of Specimens
in consequence, because it looked, as if the gold
had petered out inside the New Argus boundary,
as is not unusual at Bendigo. But the Plugger-Bill
Specimen management refused to accept defeat, went on
prospecting, and now in the crosscut west at 1835 ft. nice
gold has been cut. Specimen Hill therefore looks like
justifying itself as a position claim. Should the shoot of
gold be of any length, divs. will come soon, and the mine
ought to have a good life, for there is 800 ft. in the line of
the formation to work. With Virginia, Specimen Hill,
and New Argus in the div. list, a nice trio of produceis
will help to burnish up the fame of the golden city.

The welcome stranger in ,J. P. Madden’s office,
when he had sickened of the I .yell field and copper stocks,
was the Cock’s Pioneer, an alluvial show up Eldorado way
(Vic.). For years its electric plant was a bewilderment
to the board and a spectacle to the yokels. Dawe and
Madden came to the rescue, got the plant at work, and
then steered it into the haven where the dividends
come from. Gold and tin are the products, and
as the ground is rich —about the best worked
by hydraulic pumps in the State —divs. are hand-
some. None the less, costs bulk high, and a modern
plant of the Oastlemaine type would probably be far more
effective than the electric plant in use. Costs last year
had to stand a charge of £2OOO for clearing the mine of
slime after the big floods. Even cutting that expense
out, the fact remains that expenses are heavy. One good
trait of the management is that in its prosperity it doesn t
overlook the manager and the men. That is the charac-
teristic which won for Madden his popularity with the
staffs of his mines. It’s a heritage from poor J. P.
Lonergan.

Geological-surveyor Dunn wants to vitalise the
New Mariner, Steiglitz (Vic.). Claim paid rattling divs.
in Auld Lang Syne from Sailor’s Reef— named so because
a sailor, while sitting on a log, picked up a bit of quartz
which carried gfold, and afterwards located the reef. Ena
of divs. came when a big 1 fault cut the reef off like a Knife.
Reginald Murray and a host of others have suggested
ways of finding the stone. Now Dunn makes out that
the country is the same as the No. 2 zone at Bendigo, and
he drops tears of sorrow because no crosscut was diivcn
east or in the direction of the anticline. The surprise
directly, if Dunn moves about much more, will be to find
a spot in Victoria that hasn’t got a syncline or an anti-
cline. But all these geological finds haven’t added any
more to the gold yield of the State than have Bradford s
“corrugations,” to which Dr. Gregory lent a more
willing ear than does Dunn. After all, there doesn t
appear to be much more than terms dividing Dunn and
Bradford.-

It is still a case at Broken Hill Block 14 of much
puff and little profit. Great things are always just going
to be done ; but the awkward fact remains that, but for
the carbonates, the mine’s figures would be very poor, and
there are only odd patches of carbonates in the mine.
Block 14 was absurdly neglected at one time ; it has since
been as absurdly boomed.

Broken Hill Water Supply Co. is in more trou-
ble ; the South and Sulphide Corporation, which, after
the B.H. Prop., are the largest consumers of water from
the co.’s mains, have pegged a lease and propose to
provide for their own supply. To some extent the
Broken Hill Prop, already does this, but its dam
capacity is small. The ways of the Water Go.
arc peculiar. The mining cos. complained that, owing to
insufficient mains, the supply they were getting was un-
satisfactory ; the Water Co. replied that the new main
required would cost £3500, and it would be laid if the
mines would pay three-fourths of that amount. This
proved the last straw ; the two cos. most interested
pegged ground for a reservoir of their own, about six
miles from Broken Hill. They have paid the Water Co.
about £lO,OOO a year between them, as an average, for
the past 10 years.' , , .

The first drill in the C.S.A., Cobar, hasn’t got
very far vet; but as far as it has gone, things seem all
rio-ht This hole was started in a sump sft. 6in. below the
main east crosscut. In the bottom of the sump the lode
assayed 33'3 per cent, lead, Boz. 13dwt. silver and a trace
of gold ; at the deepest point in the bore-hole for which
assay results are available (10ft. to 12ft.) the resnlt is 4'6
per cent, copper, 11"2 per cent, lead, lSdwt. silver,
20gr o-old. The investor can’t afford to keep his
eves’ off these bore results for long. Drills cer-
tainly don’t tell everything, and sometimes give wrong
impressions; but they ought in this case to give some
reasonable sort of an idea on the crucial point whether
the workings so far have been in a narrow zone of
secondary enrichment. If they are not, but if the rich
ore lasts down, then the present quotation is no price for
these shares. _ „

“ Myponga ” : Par. (B. 18/1/’OG) re extraction
of gold from sea water brings to mind the boom of 20
years ago at Ballina, Richmond River (N.S. W.). Men had
been making wages gold-getting on the beach north and
south of the Richmond Heads, and the idea infected
local people that the gold came from the basaltic rocks
which form the north head of the Richmond. In next to
no time .so many notices of intention to lease were pasted
on the front of the little post and telegraph ottiee that
they were pushing' one another for space. Excited groups
used to stand for hours on the post-office verandah dis-
cussing their chances of making a million or so and going
to Sydney to live, and every Sunday numbers went and
inspected their mines- Visitors also began to arrive and run
down to the Heads, and see how matters were going. But.
nothino- was done and no fortunes have been made.
About'all that remains is an old gentleman si periodical
visit to the ’beach, where he shakes his head and still
sees visions. - <’ • ' '

Broken Hill Mines owe a great measure of
success to the slime saving’ appliances of their mills. The
slimes contain the richest part of the mineral particles.
Several processes were tested but it was found that the
Luting Vanners gave by far the best results. The mines
have installed Luhrigs as essential parts of their
appliances and these Vanners are erected in some
instances to save material that has been lost off other
tables. There are several hundred Luhrig Vanners erected
and in course of erection at Broken Hill, and they are
extensively used in all up-to-date reduction works. W.
& J. Lempriere, Melbourne, are sole agents.*
« * * *
The electric plant of the Federal Palace Hotel,
corner Collins and King Streets, Melbourne, furnishes a
good example of a thoroughly successful electric installa-
tion, in technical as well as in commercial respects. This
plant generates all electric current required for the hotel
lighting ; it consists of a 20 horse-power gas engine belted
to a “Ganz” 12 kilowatts dynamo, a “Ganz” switch-
board containing all the controlling and measuring instru-
ments, and a storage battery with 62 cells. The generating
machinery runs continually during the day time at its
full capacity and highest efficiency ; the surplus current
being stored into the battery ; the latter supplies all
necessary electricity required during the night; the
battery forms also a substantial reserve in case of acci-
dents. Compared with the previous method of working a
saving of £4OO per annum has been effected by this plant.
The contractors for this plant were The Austral Otis
Engineering Company, Ltd., corner Hanah and Kavanagh
Streets, South Melbourne, as sole agents for Ganz and
Company, Budapest, Hungary.* # i 3 d

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and everv disermtion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Wilfley Ore Concentrating Tables. Important
notice to mine managers and users of Concentrators.
The Supreme Court of Victoria has decided in the action
brought against N. Guthridge Limited that the well-
known Wilfley patent is val d, and has been infringed by
N Guthridge Limited by the manufacture and sale of
tables which they call the Card Concentrating Table.
The Court granted a certificate of the validity of the
Wilfley patent and ordered N. Guthridge Limited to pay
damages and costs of this action, and g'ranted ail
injunction restraining N. Guthridge Limited from in-
fringing the Wilfley patent. This is to give notice
that the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited will
enfore its rights against all persons buying or selling or
otherwise dealing in or using such infringing Concentrat-
ing machines. Edward J. Rigby, Attorney for the Wilfley
Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited, care of Austral
Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne.* [b.

Aboriginalities.
Orthos : A grape-vine in my garden is
attacked yearly by caterpillars, which destroy
more than half the crop. They are hatched
when the leaves are shooting, and destroy the
grapes whilst in flower and even when of the size
of peas. But this year nearly the whole crop has
been saved through the agency of a small beetle
(about if inch long and greyish-brown in color),
winch attaches a thread-like' tongue, finch long,
to the end of the caterpillar, and sucks it as dry
as tinder. This insect might, on this account, be
great benefit to vignerons.

“Prooshan”: “Elmore” (B. 11/l/’O6)
errs in supposing that ants kill aphides on peach
tiees. The ants seen crowding up and down peach
trees are those that “milk” the aphides by tickling
them with the antennae till they emit a sweet fluid
much relished by the ant. Some ants breed
aphides in their nests, attending to their eggs and
c irefully feeding the young.

AN ABORIGINALITY ILLUSTRATED.
When Jim Orcher was going to ride on a rough
horse . he’d carry a cigar wrapped in paper in
his left-hand pocket, and some matches in his
right-hand pocket. When the horse was buck-
ing he’d throw the reins round his neck,
unwrap the cigar and light it. One afternoon
Jim struck a pretty rough customer and was
just about to strike the match when the horse
gave a terrible buck, bursting right out of
everything. Jim landed on his feet, and, just
as the horse lashed out, he struck the match
on his hoof and lit the cigar. A good rider
Jim was.
“ Hexarn ”: He alleged discovery by
American professor as to race mixture (8., 4/l/’OG),
in Argentina I had personal knowledge of this
case : A native girl of Scotch parents had a child
by a Brazilian nigger, and afterwards married an
imported Caledonian. Her first child after mar-
riage—a girl—was perfectly white, but had pure
nigger features. Photo I saw would have been
taken for that of a full-blooded native. Brazilian
dead at the time, and not another nigger within
10 or 20 leagues.

“Hamer”: I saw inaugurated recently a
method of shouting which, if it spreads, will bring
the red face of Bung down in sorrow to the beer
barrel. A lavish shearer had gathered in a collec-
tion of loiterers, and after vaunting his generous
intentions till the publican’s face shone with holy
joy, ordered, and paid for, drinks by the bottle.
Bung kicked the dog through the bar door, and as
he turned to get the bottles I heard his tears
tinkling among the glasses.

Waj : A little English parson struck
the wilderness out at Angledool. He wished to
visit a station in the vicinity, so they mounted
him and enjoined him to stick to the track closer
than a brother. “Get on a track,” they said,
and it viust lead to somewhere if it takes a
month.” As he didn’t turn-up at his destination,
they set out to search, and two days later they
round him following a track. “ I must be some-
where near New Angledool Station,” he said,
for I ve been following this beastly track all
day. Why, d it all, man ! ” they said,
you re on the racecourse/” “Well, really”
said the lost shepherd, “that beastly building
ovali there did seem a little familiah.” It was the
grandstand; he had passed it about a thousand
times.

“ Roily ” : Some unsuccessful private
attempts have been made to introduce krine
bumble, and wild lime trees into Moree. It’s
now up to Boretown’s experimental
farm to have a try. The trees all grow
within 30 miles of the town and extend
into Mungindi and Queensland. The
lnne (very like a small Lisbon lemon)
makes an exquisite spicy jam and a tip
top summer drink. The krine bush is
distinctly ornamental and remarkable
for its light, feathery, gracefully-
festooned leaves rather than its
plum-like, magenta-shaded fruit, the
fl esh of which is hard and acidy but
eats well with sugar. It is also exten-
sively used to color jellies. Bumble
trees grow in clumps and are very hand-
some, compact and shady. Suggest
well-pruned orange trees with their per-
fectly straight trunks and thick, glossy
foliage. The fruit, mostlv green but
sometimes dull gray when dead ripe,
grow to the size of an ordinary orange
and peel in the same fashion. Inside
they resemble a passion fruit but
are harder and sweetly insipid in taste. Their
ctner beauty is a peculiarly penetrating aroma in
ui IC T i P l^® a Ppl6 and rock-melon scents are
blended, lhe naapan also grows in N.S.W. but
is more distinctively a Queensland fruit. Has
particularly glossy leaves and varies from vine to
shrubby bush. It bears an opal, finger-length
fruit of a pale yellow shade, that opens pomegra-
nate-fashion when ripe. Pods are full of a jelly-like
substance and black, peppery seeds. The flowers
are beautiful. White and starry, and scented like
a ripe apricot.

“ W.P.8.” : Deformity is as much the sub-
ject of unfavorable notice amongst animals as
amongst humans. When there is in a mob of
cattle a beast with some defect or other, such as a
limp or a hunchback, the creature always has a
look of ashamed self-consciousness, and keeps by
itself as much as possible. In travelling a mob
such an animal is constantly being molested by
the others. Once knew an old bull that developed
bad ‘ lumpy-jaw,” and the cows used to discuss
the poor old chap over their afternoon cud, and
poke unmerciful borak at him, till he became so
depressed that he cut his throat on a barbed-wire
fence.

“E.”: They are making a noise over a
young Mallee (Vic.) Anglican parson who preached
on a recent hot Sunday in flannel trousers and a
pink cotton shirt. What would they have thought
of the Rev. Thomas Streeton, who preached to a
Forest Creek congregation in the days of Jim
Crow in a beer barrel ? The Rev. Streeton was an
ordained C. of E. parson who had abandoned the
pulpit for the pick, and in company with a profane
ex-butcher had struck it rich and thick at Jim
Crow. A few godly miners of Forest Creek,
hearing of this, interviewed the parson, and
persuaded him to agree to preach on the following
Sunday morning. A baker’s big tent was cleared for
the occasion, and when the Rev. Streeton arrived
he found his pulpit ready. An ingenious digger
had brought a big beer barrel, reared it on one end
on a stand, and cut a slice clear out of it. Stand-
ing in this cutting, the holy man had a place for
his bible before him on the remaining portion of
the bottom of the barrel, and there, with the odor
of sanctity quite overwhelmed by the fragrance of
bygone beers, the reverend preached his sermon.
Ihe collection was taken up in a pint pannikin,
and panned out 7^oz.

“Tas.” : The versatility of the Australian
parson! Have been to church twice for ten
Sundays, and noticed how the appeal for brass is
made. Six: “ worship God in the collection.”
Two made a request that all present would
“now contribute their tenth.” Two desired us
to praise the Lord with our pockets.” In two
instances we were asked to “now present our
widow’s mites.” Five times “the deacons will
now wait on you for your offering.” In one case
the plates were just passed without any intro-
duction, while in another we were told to ‘‘give
as you have been prospered.” The most striking
appeal was when we were asked to “ cast of your
superabundance into the treasury.”

“ Tommy J.P.” : That part of West-o’-
Darling lying north of White Cliffs and Wilcannia
(and including lilpa, Louth, Wanaaring, Tiboo-
burra, Milparinka, and Yalpunga) is still
droughty. The frontage country is bare of grass
or herbage, a grim, grey, melancholy dust-heap.
In tins part of West-o’-Darling the Big Drought
didn t break up. The year’s rainfall was between
three and four inches. The Darling frontage
country wants flooding again; it won’t recover
fully till it gets another flood.

Ihe steamer Naming reports at Port Ade-
laide having seen an island, with a lighthouse
right in the deep water of the Great Australian
Bight, 200 miles west from Kangaroo Island. It
melted away after a time, but was a perfect mirace
while it lasted. A large globe in the Adelaide
Public Library was discovered, some time back to
have Albion Islandl” marked in a similar posi-
tion. Ihe Board of Governors discussed whether
they ought to be responsible for the accuracy of
their maps, and, while they were discussing some
practical visitor quietly scratched the islet off the
face of the waters. It is sup:. >sed now that some
old-time mirage must have been responsible for
the English geographer’s Albion Island.'

“ C.X.G.” : Last summer in Southern
(Queensland I saw the famous “ gourd ’’ duel-
catching trick worked off by the blacks. Wild”
melons were first cast into the water and allowed
to drift about for a couple of days, so that the
ducks got quite accustomed to them. Then a
nigger hollowed one out, cut eye-holes in it, put it
over his head, and swam out to the ducks When
close up he allowed himself to drift among them.
I hen suddenly a duck would disappear, the niir
having caught him by the legs, drawn him under
and fastened hi mto a string round his waist. The
other ducks, thinking that their friend had dived
for a choice morsel, would go on feeding, and the
Y keep at it until he had enough. Then
the flock m ash ° re without even having alarmed

WHALIN’ UP THE LACHLAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I know I’ve been a mug, mates ; •
I’ve felled the Gippsland tree,
With camels crossed the desert,
And I’ve peeled the spuds at sea ;
But splitting, packing, peeling,
The sweat’s ahout the same,
And I know there’s nothin’ in it
Now I’ve found a better game.
Whalin' up the Lachlan,
Graft is only play;
Whalin’ up the Lachlan
All a summer’s day.
Come, mates, we’ll fix our camp there
By bend and sandy bar ;
Oh, come whalin' up the Lachlan
Where the lazy fishes are.
Some like to stew in fact’ries
And some to sow and reap ;
And some like steering Wolseleys,
A-shaving stinking sheep ;
But some there are, sundowners,
Who let such matters be,
And care not for the morrow—
And that’s the sort are we.
Whalin’ up the Lachlan
Q lit yard and axe and plough !
Whalin’ up the Lachlan —
Come join the loafers now !
We’ll fill our pipes and yarn there
Till time shall canter by,
Whalin’ up the Lachlan
Under a starry sky.
LOUIS ESSON.

Joseph Abood, with a yellow turban twisted
round his head, appeared at the Murwillumbah
(N.S.W.) police court, the other day, to indig-
nantly inquire why he had been summoned. Evi-
dence was-that the goods which he hawked were
carried in two tin trunks and thus thrown across
the back of his horse. From chafing, the animal s
sides were in a shocking condition, lne i.iyl.
fixed the fine at £5. the heaviest penalty. ‘ that s
a lot of money; I could buy another horse for
that,” muttered the defendant. “ I wish I could
make it £20,” said the P.M., and Abood then
began to realise he was in trouble. I hey are
cruel beasts to animals, the Asiatics—except the
Chinaman, whose horse is always in plump con-
dition.

Winton, West Q., rainfall, for 1905' was one
inch and 71 points, and most of it fell m one
week. Early this year an inch and a quarter fell,
but 25 inches would be welcome. Winton is on
the vast plains that stretch from Break-of-Day to
Setting Sun, and its principal products last year
were dust storms and dead swagsinen.

“ Reiby I feel for society twinklers and
grand panjandrums generally. To meet your name-
sake as a poddy-calf or bandy-tomcat hardly calls
for gratitude, and immortalisation in a floricultu-
ral-eum-horticultural catalogue is an unkind cut.
You pick up an innocent-looking booklet and
read : “ George Reid, a charming shade of purple
with crimson tips ; should be set in a large pot
and watered daily ; a magnificent creeper.” Or
“Lady Smarks, deep scarlet throughout, blotched
with reddish brown requires much feeding ; very
hardy and a vigorous climber.” And then there’s
the inflated cattle-pumpkin or horse-radish that
grows mightily in the name of King Ned or the
angel Gabriel.

Settler Pickersgill, at Archie’s Creek
(Vic.), is the possessor of a snake-hunting cat.
Pussy frequently drags in a damaged black snake
to provide amusement for a clutch of kittens, lne
snakes are not always dead, and the other day the
housewife, going to a cupboard, narrowly escaped
the fangs of a crippled “ tiger the cat had
stowed a way >on the bottom shelf for future
reference.
THE FIREWOOD MILLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a life that isn’t pleasant, and a graft that’s heavy!
too,
Where the firewood mills are busy putting curly billets
through.
And you’ll findthe men that live it mostly derelicts and
fools
That lack of grit has beaten, or a tyrant habit rules.
At times you’ll, maybe, happen on a man of healthy mind
That grim ill-luck compels td take whatever he can find,
But by far the greatest number, are beyond the sting of
ills,
And are callowsras the billets they are chopping for the
mills.
You will hear the axes ringing in the morning, ere the
light, " .
And you’ll hear the crosscuts clanging when the mopoke
calls at night,
And haulers loudly shouting, loudly cursing all the day,
And Hogging struggling horses through the swamps and
through the clay.
And the splitters curse the millers for their roguery and
greed,
But the millers never heed them, for they haven’t any
need.
O ! the heart that’s only human with the keenest pity
fills
For the sweated, struggling toilers at the little firewood
mills.
The millers find the tucker and, of course, deduct the
cost
From the wr r'-s of the toilers, and if any time is lost,
As there isn’t apy credit, then a “ settling-up occurs,
And it mostly leaves the grafter “ still in debt,” the boss
avers.
Then in apparent pity, maybe, half-a-quid he’ll lend
With, “ I won’t be hard upon you, here’s a bob or two to
spend.”
And the loot is spent on liquor from the hidden wayback
stills,
And the fool renews the struggle at the little firewood
mills.
And the bosses put advertisements in “ Wanted columns,”
so
That men upon their uppers to the mills in dozens go.
And though the graft’s abandoned by the, men of force
and grit,
There are always human ruins who are glad to stick to it.
So here you’ll find the drunkards and deserters of their
wives,
And the derelicts and “bad lots” slowly grinding out
their lives ;
And enough to make you stoical and deaf to crying ills
The sordiness and squalor of the little firewood mills.

“ W.P.8.” : Re the recent burning of the
soda-nitrabe-laden barque Itata at Newcastle
(N.S.W.). Hftd a volume of water been turned
on to the burning vessel a good part of the city
would have been blown to Hades. Water alone
poured on nitrate rapidly generates gas and causes
violent explosions ; but a mixture of water and
nitrate will act as an extinguisher. As it was, the
explosion caused by the sea-water that got through
the rivet-holes of the heated plates: was felt at
Raymond Terrace, 16 miles away.
“ D.M‘D.” : Was on the bridge with the
captain of a steamer leaving Byron Bay, t’other
day, when a cry from the jetty barely reached us
. “Passenger left behind!” “Confound it.
snorted the skipper. “Sure to be somebody of
importance, too. Have to send a boat, I suppose.
The boat was sent, and the captain fumed until
it returned with a mangy-looking Chow deck-
passenger, That skipper’s pious observations are
ringing in my ears yet!
“Itan” about irrigation possibilities in
Bananaland :
In many places in this State there are facilities for
water conservation. The Warrego river, e.(f., rises > n the
high country beyond Angathella, south of Tambo. lbe
distance from the source to a point eight or ten miles
below Cunnamulla is 350 miles, and throughout that belt
the finest wheat land exists. In flood time the Warrego
is a stream of great proportions, but all the water runs
to waste. An outlay of £lOO,OOO would lock up ten
thousand million gallons. A single 10ft. overshot dam
erected four miles below Cunnamulla would throw the
water back for 30 miles, giving a stream three chains wide
and 10ft. deep at the narrowest point and the shallowest
depth. Near Barcaldine, a handful of wheat, farmers
make the industry of wheat culture pay with bore water.
The conserved water would be sufficient to supply
hundreds of farmers. j

Ex-Cornstalk writes from Perth (W.A.) :
I am in Groperland, dragged to this place by the false
reports about the splendid land to be obtained free. -I
came over four years ago, and started at once to f
land (for market-garden and orchard purposes). I have
been looking ever since, and cannot get one acre of suit-
able land. I could get it if I went to some of the land-
boodlers, who have grabbed every acre suitable for agri-
culture. Wherever one goes along any of the railway
lines all the good or middling land is.in the hands of the
land boodlers, and their price is from £5 to £5O per acre.
The Ohows in and around Perth grow vegetables in the
swamps, and the rents they pay to the and cormorants
range from £2O to £4O per acre annually.
There is more trouble at S.A. Outer Har-
bor works; and a prospect of 500 men being
thrown out of work. The.contractors have alleged
all along that the Government plans, on which
they were working, were all wrong, how they
declare-that the only plan to make the channel
navigable is to build a continuous wall on the
northern side of the river for a great distance
' westward—as suggested in John Coade’s report on
the matter in JBBl. The Government won t hear
of this, and wants the contractors to go on dredg-
ing the channel till further notice at their own
expense. Govt, also-wants to charge the con-
tractors for 80,000 cubic yards of drifted silt on a
part Of-’ the eliahnel not yet touched. Ihe con-
tractoVs hay© struck until they can get satisfaction,
and things atd at a standstill.

At a recent meeting of Petershpm Bowling
Club the new Liquor Bill was fully discussed,
and finally it was decided to absolutely dis-
continue the sale of intoxicating liquors.
Future matches between the Petersham and
still unregenerate clubs will be looked for-
ward to with unusual Interest.
THE RABBIT PESJ.
The proposal to Invite Dr. Danysz, of the Pasteur Insti-
tute, to come out and organise his measures of extermjj
nation by spreading contagious disease amongst the
rabbits, may be regarded as practically certain to be
adopted. It will have to be determined whether the germ
used in the extermination of the rabbits is, under the
climatic conditions existing in Australia, fatal to other
animals or to human beings. This, of course, is vital to
the success of the proposed tests.— Dally paper.
Dr. Danysz: " Now, then, vlll someone sooply me wlz
some human beings to experiment wlz ? ” (Doubtless
the names of many who can be spared for the purpose
will occur to our readers.)

FROM COLOMBO TO SYDNEY.


PERSONAL ITEMS.
Add Kidston to the list of Australian
Premiers who have committed poetry. The
Bulletin printed some verses of his 13 years ago.
Just deceased Sydney commercial magnate
Littlejohn was a great arbitration man. Many
guineas flowed to his pockets for arbitrating in
c immercial disputes.
Thirty-three members of the new Maori-
land Parliament are native born.
Randolph Bedford is off to North Queens-
land again in search of copper, or copy, or both.
New Bananaland Premier Kidston has a
Scotch brogue so thick that his fellow-country-
man, Philp, asked him some time ago to stop
haranguing the House in a foreign tongue.
Captain H. J. Holland, of the Adelaide
S.S. Co., who died the other day, was the British
official who accompanied Chief Cetewayo to Eng-
land after the Zulu war. He had been in the
Australian coastal trade many years, and never
' had a mishap with any of his vessels.
Late Hugh Nelson, President of Queensland
Legislative Council, was the most consistent
opportunist Australian politics have so far pro-
duced. To make room for Morgan he had to die
or be dismissed, and old “ Never mind,” who all
his life hated political violence, quietly died.
Sir Mancherjee Merwanjee Bhownagree,
defeated at the British elections for Bethnal
Green, is the gentleman whom Salisbury once
designated “the blackfelloW,” and thus caused a
broth of a row. Bhownagree is a Parsee, a Uni-
versity graduate, and of some note in Anglo-
India.
Charles McVeigh, lucky Queensland miner,
left Sydney by the R.M.S. India for his seat in
the House of Commons, to which his native
Donegal elected him. He was a big subscriber to
Irish Nationalist funds for many years, and,
though he will add nothing to the oratory of the
cause, his vote will be straight and emphatic.
Richard Jebb, known in Australia as
author of “Studies in . Colonial Nationalism,”
passed through the Commonwealth on his way to
S’Africa last week. Mr. Jebb is on the editorial
staff of the Mobning Post, a paper which now
represents much of the forward thought of Eng-
land. A colleage of Jebb there is Spenser Wilkin-
son, the British authority on foreign politics.
The contracting for the S.A. Outer Har-
bor works seems to be a somewhat worrying
business. The original contractors were Waring
and Rowden. After the first 12 months their
health broke down and they had to retire. Then
Baxter and Boyne took it on, and after six months
Boyne succumbed to the strain and died. Now
Baxter has it on his own, and he doesn’t look
too happy.
Young Daly, of Dalyston (Powlett River,
Vic.), is a hard bov to kill. A few weeks ago a
mate shot him in the head with a pea-rifle.
Young Daly let the bullet stay there. Later on
he was snagged while diving, but the snag refused
to hold him. Then he was bitten by a snake
when swimming across the same hole. Amateurs
scarified and sucked the bite, and young Daly
got better.
SIR DO-THE-THING-PROPERLY JENKINS.
Re Malouf, the Syrian immigrant. Malouf
is a tribal name, and is in Syria considered some
pumpkins; therefore the gentleman from Asia
Minor frequently adopts it for trading and other
purposes in this country. There are two sects
among the Syrians—the Maronites and Druses,
each hating the, other virulently. Syrians make
splendid strangers.
A Gippsland (Vic.) farmer whose home-
stead was bush-fired last week paid a high tribute
of praise—as he thought—to the heroism of an
Italian employee, who practically saved the family
from destruction. “.Clavarino acted like a true
Briton,” said the grateful boss. Interesting to
hear the views of Clavarino on the subject. Pro-
bably he had a patriotic notion that he was acting
like a true Italian.
Deceased J. Marfcineau Pillars, of Sydney
and Melbourne, was son of Rev. James Pillars,
who conducted Unitarian services in Sydney from
1864 to 1873. A couple of years later the Rev.
Pillars was killed by falling over the cliffs at
Watson’s Bay. His widow married Charles Bright,
the well-known journalist, lecturer and life-assur-
ance officer who died in Sydney a short time back.
Mrs. Bright is still a figure on the lecture platform,
spiritualism being her chief topic now.
Vic. Premier Bent lias a “double” in a
night watchman employed at one of the Melb.
City Corporation yards. The likeness is very
striking, even to the vast lower chest, and the
double finds it a cause of much annoyance.
During the Chinese-labor
war several daily paper poets
burst into pathetic verse over
the alleged fact that Queen
Victoria was frequently to be
seen weeping and bemoaning
the fate of “ my poor soldiers.”
Horace B umbold’s recent diplo-
matic reminiscences tell this
rather different tale :
Par from evincing' the discourage-
ment, the desire for peace almost at
any price, attributed to her, the
feelings dominant in the Queen at
that period seemed to be more of
deep resentment at the insolent
challenge of the Boer President, and
at the unfortunate turn taken by
our military operations. The Queen,
in short, was in no meek, despond-
ing mood ; she was very keen, very
angry, and very determined.

named Bent visited our town-
ship the other day and I was
enabled to examine the animal
at short range. Somehow, I
haven’t thought very highly of
Victorians ever since. A com-
munity that keeps THAT at
the head of affairs must surely
be sadly deficient somewhere.
Strange how these things happen. At an
informal Perth farewell to Bigjohn Forrest before
his departure for London, ex-Premier Daglish
brought up the High Commissioner question, and
strongly urged the claims of John for the post.
It is whispered that if Mr. Forrest gets the ap-
pointment, the British Government will give him
a peerage—a safe enough step, for John has no
children and enough money to guarantee that he
wouldn’t wear his title on an eleemosynary barrel-
organ.
Appearance of accused W. P. Crick in the
Police Court without arrest repeated an incident
in one of the great political trials in Ireland.
Alderman Darley, grandfather of N.S.W. Chief
Justice, was Police Magistrate in Dublin when
the order was given to take action against Daniel
O’Connell and a number of others for sedition.
Darley had authority to arrest, but, instead of
doing so, sent letters to the accused persons invit-
ing them to attend at his private house and
arrange for the necessary bail.
Arthur Morgan, who has allowed himself
to begin the process of political ossification, is
still on the cradle side of 50, and had years of
fight left in him. And an elegant fighter he was.
His first hustings address was to the cookies of
Cowan Creek, near Warwick, on behalf of his
father, James Morgan, who was down with a
broken leg, and whose seat was being fought for
by a little Hebrew, Horwitz —backed up by the
late W. H. Groom and the MTlwraith party.
Arthur fought gamely, but Horwitz won, and the
bitterness of defeat killed James Morgan, worn
out by weeks of pain and suffering with a limb
that refused to knit. Both Groom and Horwitz
went over to the Liberal party later, but Arthur
Morgan never buried the tomahawk that he had
sharpened for those two.
That John Burns would be included in
the new Ministry was forecasted three months
ago by R. 8. Smythe, who has a conditional
contract with him for an Australian lecturing
tour.
“ O’F.” : When I came across a biogra-
phy of early Australian Governor Thomas
Makdougall Brisbane I expected to learn
something of this country’s remote history.
But the Australian portion of the book con-
sists of one chapter, and that is mainly filled
with an extract from Brisbane’s diary, which
seems to be an exercise in the art of saying, in
thirty-seven different ways, that he Went to
church. The rest of the chapter is taken up
in recording that the Rev. Samuel Marsden
was pleased at the advent of so devout a
Governor, and that Brisbane once took his
Australian-born son to church and had him
christened Thomas Australius.
The late Hugh Nelson was much liked
by officials in the Queensland departments.
For many years he never travelled any dis-
tance by train or steamer without Jesse
Hooper, a railway steward. On one occasion,
many years ago, Nelson and the then Gover-
nor had started to steam from (Jairns to
Maoriland on a holiday trip. Nelson took ill,
but the Governor did not think there was
anything very serious the matter. Hooper
knew there was, and said so. NeverthffeiS
the boat steamed on. A few hours later
Hooper approached the Governor and told
him emphatically that the Premier would die
if they did not return and secure medical
aid. Accordingly the steamer ran back to
Cairns, which the doctor there said they had
just reached in the nick of time.
H. M. Hyndman, the English Socialist,
and a recently-defeated Parliamentary candidate
in the Cold Country, is about to pay a visit to
Australia. He made a sojourn in Melbourne in
’O9, when he was a contributor to the Pall Mall
Gazette. He was at Cambridge University with
Purves, K.C. (Vic.).
The question again arises as to the identity
of the first gold discoverer at Yilgarn (W.A.).
Recently a deputation asked W. A. Mines Minister
Gregory for an annuity for veteran-miner R.
Greaves, who is now past work and impecunious,
on the ground that he was the first discoverer.
According to the Minister, however, the Mines
Department reported that it was prospector
Anstey who lead the original Yilgarn party, of
which Greaves was a paid member, and that
Anstey had got the Government reward. An
investigation is to follow.

J. F. Hogan deals with J. B. O’Reilly in
a recent Advocate. O’Reilly, as Fenian,
entered the Hussars, and was instrumental in
“ poisoning” the minds of 15,000 soldiers. After
getting away from Westralia he wrote a novel
called “Moondyne,” foretelling the Coolgardie
and Kalgoorlie gold rush.
Allan M‘Kinnon, for 12 years cox of
the Newcastle (N.S.W.) lifeboat, crossed the bar
the other day, aged 58. He joined the boat’s
crew, 34 years ago. His record of shipwrecked
crews saved from death on the Oyster Bank and
thereabouts is a long, heroic one. Some notable
rescues were those made from the ketch Jonathan
(August, 1882), steamer Lindus, barque Adderley,
and in 1904 from the French barque Adolphe.
The heroism of the last feat was recognised by
the French and N.S.W. Governments, and by the
vessel’s owners.
N.S.W. Judge Docker, who recently gave
a lash sentence to a “looney,” is Wont to plume
himself on doing what the general sense of the
community condemns. Time and again his sen-
tences have been referred to in Parliament in
terms of unequivocal condemnation. But his
Honor has never minded. The late J. P. Abbott
hammered Judge Docker in Parliament on one
occasion; W. P. Crick on another, George Dibbs
on another, and Levien and other members at
different times; Judge Docker took these animad-
versions as ducks take water on their backs.
A Supreme Court Judge has to be ap-
pointed in Victoria. There are half-a-dozen men
at the Bar anxious to fill the post; but Premier
Bent is in trouble. Thera is an honorary Minister
named Mackey who is tired of being honorary ;
but there is no billet for him. Mr. Davies, how-
ever, is Solicitor-General, and he would like to be
a Judge. Mr. Bent’s plan is to appoint Mr.
Davies Justice of the Supreme Court, and give Mr.
M ickey Davies’ portfolio. He is faced by one
obstacle, however. Mr. Davies is a solicitor, and
a solicitor has never yet been raised to the
Victorian Bench ; and anyway, he is too up
to 70 to be put up—he lias been in practice in
Melbourne 43 years. Also, the cool, calm, strong
Mr. Irvine wants the job.
Bent’s team of Ministers, leaving out
Thomas himself and Swinburne, has about as
much spine as an oyster. The most spineless of
all is Mr. Samuel Gillott. He will take respon-
sibility for nothing. Though he is a lawyer, he
refers all prosecutions to the Crown Law Depart-
ment for an opinion; then he generally refers
that opinion to barrister Cussen, and then lays
the lot solemnly before the Cabinet, and asks it—
which is Thomas and Swinburne—to decide. The
most trivial matters, from the promoting of a
policeman to the engaging of a junior laundress
for a lunatic asylum, are minuted by Samuel
and referred to the Cabinet. No one who has
seen the man walking down the street could help
diagnosing his character. He looks as though he
felt he should refer the advisability of each timid
little stride he makes to the Cabinet. When he
stood for election last time, he used to dissolve in
tears at the votes of confidence and the nice
things that were said of him.

Do please cease your railing at Real J. No
man less deserves it from a democratic paper.
Real worked himself up from the carpenter’s
bench to the judicial Bench by his own talents
in spite of every opposition, lie reached the top
rung at a time when not only the higher branch
but even the lower one of the law was something-
extra in the way of a snob. And he never kicked
the ladder from under him. Will walk with and
talk to any poor man on his way to business or
church, and not in the “ haw-haw ” style of some
we could name, but as a cove and a brother. He
is head and shoulders over any of his judicial
brethren intellectually as he is physically.
He was no political hack jammed into
office to be got rid of, but accepted
his billet at a great financial loss,
urged thereto by Bench, Bar, and
Government, backed by the whole
community. A vote throughout
Bananaland would put him at
the top of the poll as the most
able and upright of the Judges.
Others wouldn’t he thought of
except by themselves and a few
Lodge brothers. Real looks every
time for the absolute right in every
case. That is why he continually
jerks up counsel. When the sub-
stance and fibre of evidence are sifted
between the teeth of both Judge
and counsel the right of a case is
more likely to be got at than if his
Honor sits back and winks and
blinks while the respective Buzfuzzes
drone out their particular views,
concealing unpleasant things, and
thereby giving jurymen false impres
sions. Better that counsels’ balanced
periods should be ruthlessly spoiled
than that the truth should escape.
Therefore, dear 8., stop railing at
Patrick Real, and oblige 590,004
Queenslanders.
' Tom Glassey, for years
leader of Queensland Labor
party, is a neglected and forlorn
politician nowadays. The old
man was a main factor in the
creation of the party he left in
a fit of pique and mangled
vanity. But he has paid for
his pique in seeing younger
ex-comrades rise to the top
place—Kidston, for instance;
for Glassey’s name was known
and esteemed through a large
part of the Labor world before Kid-
ston was heard of even in his own
State. The new -Premier would like to give
his old leader a show in the Council, but Glassey
still has boundless self-confidence and boiling
egotism, and wants something bigger than a seat
in the Council.
Diaz, the great President of Mexico, is
now, at 75 years of age, active as a gymnast,
and seems to be good for another 15 years of
busy life.
The Dowie craze seems to he nearing its
end. Population of Zion city has decreased from
6000 to 3000. Five trustees have been appointed
to manage the city. “Profit ’’ Dowie himself is
sick to death with paralysis and bronchitis, and
has gone to the Bahamas, hoping that a mild
climate will do what faith doesn’t.
Late David Lee, Melbourne’s first and
only city organist, used to take credit for haying
made the match between Fitz Gibbon and Micky
Dawson’s daughter by introducing him to her at a
mayor’s ball, and advising him to court the
heiress. It was fairly late in his life when
Fitz Gibbon decided to relinquish bachelor joys.
As part of her dower, his wife possessed the
“Waterman’s Arms” in Little Collins-street,
which, on her decease, fell to hubby’s lot. Every
Monday morning the frugal Fitz., who in private
life did not display the lavish and profuse
prodigality that marked the chairman of the
Metropolitan Board of Works, called at the bar of
the “Waterman’s Arms” to collect his weekly
rent. On those solemn occasions Fitz., it is said,
would order a threepenny beer, and drank it in
solitary grandeur.
Mr. G. E. Brodie, for some years Chief-
Inspector of Accounts in N.S.W. Public Service,
has been promoted to the position of Director of
Charities. Mr. Brodie, as Chief-Inspector, was a
demon as Government prosecutor before the P.S.
Board in matters involving irregularities. His
motto was that he and his staff were “watch-
dogs not bloodhounds,” and this text he
signally failed to live up to., No bloodhound
on a hot scent was more eager than Mr.
Brodie on the trail. The qualifications which stood
him in good stead in his old position—the flinty,
remorseless d isposition—would seem to be strangely
out of place in one whose new work will be among
the human Wreckage filling the Government poor-
houses. On paper there has been no crueller
appointment ever made. But who knows ? Han-
son, his predecessor in the job, was also a Treasury
inspector. The effort to turn himself into a man
with a heart, instead of one without, seriously
affected his health. Perhaps Brodie will be able
to do it and live. What kink has got into the
Public Service Board’s mind that it must make
this appointment from the blood-and-iron In-
spectors of Accounts—men in whom the milk of
human kindness must be dried up before they can
get on the staff!

HEALTHY LIFE.
Professor F. H. Griffin,
M.D., wrote :
“ I only wish every soul in the world used
Kutnow’s Powder ; they would experience
great benefit. Four of my family besides
myself use it regularly.” It is a.splendid
corrective for liver disorders. It is as mild
as the purest water, but as certain as the
rising of the sun. It is delightfully refresh-
ing as a health-draught, and removes the
severest headache almost instanter.
Professor L N. Love,
M.D., wrote s
t( The great surgeon, Professor Lawson
Tait, M.D., himself a sufferer from a gouty
diathesis, was not only one of the world s
greatest surgeons, but an able physician and
a skilful therapeutist. For years he found
Kutnow’s Powder of great value to him per-
sonally and in his practice, and this remedy
is one of the few he conspicuously endorsed
over his own signature. He realised, as all
careful, observing practitioners do, that it is
necessary to cleanse the system frequently,
to remove the debris of undigested food, and
to hasten the elimination of uric acid to
properly guard against serious disease. Kut-
now’s Powder stimulates the activities of the
bowels and kidneys. A dessert-spoonful in
a tumblerful of water taken immediately on
rising in the morning cleans out the bowels,
acts as a diluent, and flushes out the kidneys
in a most satisfactory way. Thorough
elimination is salvation, and there is no
better eliminator than Kutnow’s-Powder.”
Late Professor of Clinical Medical Diseases of Children

College of Physicians and Surgeons.
How to Secure Both.
How many people are there to-day m the British Isles who could
answer the greeting —“How are you?” by saying, “Very well,
thanks ! ” „
The percentage who could truthfully say, “ very well, thanks,
is very small, but really (with care) everyone ought to be always well.
Good health is your birthright, but, unfortunately, you have an
organ to keep in order called the liver, an unruly member.
If you have a strong, active liver, you will lead a strong, active
life, but you must keep the liver up to the mark.
If you find a difficulty with the liver, be careful to avoid drugs,
mercurial pills, and all drastic remedies —they hurt.
Adopt Kutnow’s Powder, the liver encouraging remedy ; it acts
gently, yet, nevertheless, does its work with extraordinary efficiency.
If you wish to excel in brain-work you will find a dessert-spoon-
ful of Kutnow’s Powder of immense benefit. The same applies to
every action of our daily life—work, play, sleep—no matter what
you do, if you would do it well you must keep the great organs of the
body in perfect working order. The stomach needs cleansing, the
liver stimulating, the kidneys flushing, and the bowels dealing.
Herein lies the whole secret of health, and Kutnow’s Powder does
this for you, and does it perfectly.
