A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 30, 1906.
My Dear Arini,—
The Lady Northcote has forwarded to the hon.
secretary of the women’s committee for the Victor
Daley Memorial Fund a cheque for ten guineas.
• Further, she conveys through Captain Greville
her great sympathy with the movement to make
some provision for the poet’s widow and young
children, and incidentally to mark his grave with
some suitable monument. The Lady Northcote
had, long before Victor Daley’s death, read and
admired his “Dawn and Dusk.” Women’s com-
mittee for the fund is in course of formation. The
hon. sec. will be glad to receive subscriptions
addressed to her at The Bulletin office, and to
hear from ladies willing to work on the com-
mittee.
Queensland Governor Chelmsford threatens to
gain some Australian experiences of the bush, and
talks about a trip to look over The Edge. He
made a start the other day by drinking tea out of
a pannikin at Eton Vale sale, where a crowd of
buyers had assembled to participate in the crum-
bling up of another section of that big land mo-
nopoly. Lady Chelmsford broke the ice—the ther-
mometer hovered about the century at the time—
with her maiden speech recently, by telling the
women of the Toowoomba Benevolent Society that
they were wise to keep their show out of debt as
they had been doing.
One of the funniest things that struck the Blue
Mountuns (N.S.W.) this summer was the “sum-
mer school,” run by the Australasian Students’
Christian Union. Large numbers of Pharisees
were present, leading the simple and strenuous life,
sleeping on spring mattresses and stretchers in
tents. The rural roads of Lawson were cumbered
with the paraphernalia of an effete civilisation
providing a 20tli century adaptation of the wan-
derings of the Israelites. The Hittites, Jebusites
and Amalekites who were summering on the
mountains in the usual felt hat, singlet and
trousers, looked on with mingled scorn and amuse-
ment as the sacred relics tiled slowly past, and a
hot Christian Unionist, weary by the wayside,
struggled with a climbing collar with liberal curse
citations from the minor prophets. Disgraceful
orgies on lemonade and buns took place after-
noonly in the nice white tents. Piety never seems
to go arm in arm with a sense of the ridiculous.
What a crowd of handsome Australian women
are in Europe at present ! If Mr. Coghlan could
get them all together as an exhibit it would be a
good advt. for the Commonwealth. There is Miss
Elsie Anderson—l hear that when she enters a
restaurant in Paris the gallant Frenchmen rise to
have a better view of her—Mrs. Ernest Watt,
Mrs. Sep. Levy, Lady Rupert Clarke, and Mrs.
Jay, and Mrs. Herbert Marks. These last two were
Pilchers, daughters of the K.C. They have a
great time exploring Paris, and confess that they
didn’t know real conservatism or genuine, iron-
clamped nobility till they got to know of— of, mind
you —French aristocrats. Women of the French
aristocracy, that is. As to knowing them to speak
toJ-Hyell, some day the Australian beauties may
speak to archangels face to face, but unless there’s
a huge levelling-down in Heaven the French
aristocrat and the Australienne will never meet.
For that matter the former would not know the
King of England, One item that made them
patch their breath was the sight of the Faubourg
St- Germain dwellers coming down to Benediction
to the swell churches, in evening dress. Painted,
dyed and “ transformationed” women ; but they’d
dip at the stake sooner than meet on terms of
equality other than their own tiny “ set,” or move
from their places on the Tribune at a racecourse
except to go to their carriages.
Concerning the beautiful Australiennes abroad,
you’ll see in Sydney, say, on a fashionable first
night, as beautiful women (married women, we
haven’t so many remarkably pretty girls), as you’ll
see in any other country. And this is the age
of beauty women. As a London doctor put it
recently :
The golden age of beauty, owing to a more healthful
manner o£ living, has arrived. Physiognomists, physical
culture experts, and beauty specialists see an extra-
ordinary change for the better in national looks. Both
men and women of the upper classes are improving in
feature, in coloring, and in physique. Women are
attaching importance to the necessity of preserving and
perfecting their looks. Powder and paint are out of date.
Massage., electricity, and muscular -manipulation have
taken the'r place ; complexions have improved because
of the rage for fresh air, and the carriage has been im-
mensely benefited by physical culture. Brighter eyes,
clearer complexions, and a more erect carriage are the
three main directions in which appearances have im-
proved. r 1
Some months ago a male Maorilander came over
here to Sydney to have two deep lines that curved
from his nose to the corners of his mouth removed.
With them his beauty was not of the fatal order.
A rnan physical-culturist in a barber’s saloon here
worked a little while every day for five weeks on
those lines, principally with massage and air-suc-
tion' pups, and as the Maprilander gave him a
liberal money present on leaving, as well as what
he paid the chief of the saloon, the footprints of
Time must have been satisfactorily erased or much
softened, was, in our mammas’ early youth,
when one put one’s frightsomeness down to God,
and wouldn’t dream of trying to stipple up His
alleged handiwork.
A returned Australian, amongst other chat, tells
me he was at Monte Carlo when Lord Villiers,
our Jersey’s son, broke the bank. Suppose it’s
an bid story here, but I hadn’t heard it. There
was an old Yankee looking on enviously, a man
so mean that his confidential clerk said of him
that “ whenever he makes a rise in Wall-street he
runs out and faints on the sidewalk so as not to
wear out his office floor falling on it.” The
noble'Jersey sprig, raking in £30,000, beat Mr.
Frank Gardner’s breakage of the same bank a
few years ago, when he won £16,000 in 15
minutes by staking on the number nine.
When we hear of an Australian—apparently a
man of some culture, if not of “culture rare”—
making a mild splash in London, we usually know

now in London who has a house in Park Lane,

Miss Nance O’Neil has appeared in San Fran-
cisco in “Monna Vanna.” You may remember
Maeterlinck’s play, in which the beautiful wife of
the head of the army (of Pisa) goes oyer by night
to the tent of the conqueror, a splendid barbarian
clad only in her cloak. That she shall come and
in such guise is the only stipulation of the be-
sieger, and in return he will raise the siege and
feed the starving city. San Fran, was chiefly
concerned about the kind of cloak Miss O’Neil

A girl who got engaged to a member of a
squatting family just before leaving Sydney for a
visit to India in Royal-progress time, took off
her engagement ring after leaving Adelaide, say-
ing that she wasn’t going for an Indian holi-
day labelled and ringed as the prospective
property of someone who wasn’t Lord Kitchener.
Until that warrior is married, no smart girl
visitor to India will wear her engagement ring.
She might be the Sirdar’s matrimonial destiny.
Mr. Secretary-to-the-Post-Office W. Gray, who
accompanies Minister Ward, of M.L., to the
Postal Conference at Rome, is a picturesque
widower, who has been in the service 40 years.
His father was Secretary to the department before
him. The paternal-looking chief is light, guide,
warning, and dictator to the Telephone Exchange
girls, and he must till the quadruple bill with
satisfaction to them, for they don’t approve of his
intended absence.

kittenpillars trespassing on their doorstep. These
were quickly disposed of (by the bees) and pushed
over the edge.” From Mr. Byron Moore’s book,
“Her Royal Highness Queen Bee and other
Stories,?’ written for the Children’s Hospital
(Melb.). Isn’t “kittenpillars” quaint? Even
middle-aged children—so many children under 12
are middle-aged—will enjoy this book and its
70 illustrations. Latter are done for love, by
Miss Hope Evershed, of Launceston, a teacher at
the Technical School there, who has a gifted
pencil for outlining the small child and the big
dog, and Her Beejesty receiving the homage of
workers and drones. Last book, “The Cruel

A London correspondent, writing of the Victorian
League which is run by Lady Jersey and aims at
making the colonists known to each other and to
English people: “Lady Jersey and other English-
women think they know better than any Aus-
tralian authority Who’s Who, and won’t con-
descend to ask help or information from women
who could give both. Consequently they make
such blunders. One Sydney lady met her -hus-
band’s tobacconist at a ball in Mayfair, and a
Maoriland lady met her grocer at the same
(League) function, and on her return to M.L.
found her butcher boasting of having been to a
garden party at Osterley Park. Colonists of cul-
ture don’t come here for this sort of thing.” The
horror of it! Fancy the wife of a Tobacco. Trust
magnate meeting a mere retailer, and Steak being
admitted on nodding terms with Beer !
The axis of the earth sticks out these days at
Bong Bong, and the hat of someone named
Osborne hangs on the projection. Osbornes are
as numerous in that region about this time as are
bush fires elsewhere, I believe some 30 Osbornes
are. members of the Australian Club, and some 40,
a,ll cousins. answer the society roll-call at 8.8.
The progeny runs to the masculine gender, and
there are not -many girls, or else they are very
self-effacing, or live in London. Mrs. Pat Osborne
is the smartest woman of the clan. Now that old
Pat Osborne is dead and Currandooley empty—
the Henry Osbornes live in a wing or a feather of it
—Bong Bong is the only neighborhood where any
country-home entertaining is done after the old
crusted English manner —elaborate dinners, white
tie de rigueur, and the family butler in evidence.
The house-party form of entertainment is almost
unknown elsewhere in Australia. The Charlie
Fairfaxes, the Oswald Watts, and Ewan Frazers
had or have houses at Bong Bong, and the holiday
Government House is in the neighborhood. As
well as the race meetings, dinner and bridge
parties, attractions include a famous annual pigeon
massacre for a £lOO prize. The pastoral custom
of eating lunch on the Bong Bong course in a
species of gumleaf bush house still prevails. The
drowning locust or beetle which has plunged to
its doom from the adjacent roof only adds a dis-
tinctive and gamey flavor to the iced hock-cup.
Other guests face the mid-day worry from the
top of a coach or “ bus ’’—the “ chic-est ” thing, by
the way, in English country race meeting con-
veyances. The man who hands you a cup of tea
and two boiled beetles is pretty certain to be an
Osborne, and it isn’t safe for you to ask this man
who holds your doped tea for you while you run
a chamois over your blazing countenance about an
eccentric-looking Unknown. He is always an
Osborne, and a cousin of the interrogated.
Mr. Goring Thomas, somewhat known in Syd-
ney, is now in Canada. Miss Louise Mack (Mrs.
Creed) is in Florence doing newspaper work.
Miss Alice Mack (Mrs. Pilcher) is in London.
That’s all the locations I can think of just now.
Lovely woman is prone to attach herself to all
sorts of pet-freaks, from little curly dogs to little
tame men. The quaintest pet-story of the season
comes from Maoriland :
A Miss Swares, of Slippery Creek, Manurewa, in South
Auckland, has a school of fifteen tame eels, which live in
thi ereek, but on occasion era -1 out and slither about on
the g rass. All the eels have names bestowed upon them by
their fair patroness or proprietress, and they come up to the
bank of the stream when they hear their names called, or
whenever the lady’s shadow is cast across the water. . She
brings down small pieces of beef with which to feed them,
and the slippery creatures snake out on to the grass and
have their meals there, while their fair mistress coos to
them as if they were babies. Sometimes the eels show
their agility by crawling through the lady’s bracelet, held
an inch or two above the ground. The slimy things are
quite fond of Miss Swares, and would follow her for miles,
only the grass is not damp enough. She is now teaching
them-a sort of “eefine ” steeplechase, in which one eel
follows the other through the bracelet, like a string of
horses through a circus hoop ; and to dance upon their
tails to the music of a tin whistle.
That Slippery Creek eel-farm deserves a visit
. from some enterprising “side-show” proprietor.
Laurence Phillip and Col. Kenneth Mackay
have collaborated on a song, which goes to London
for publication. Mr. Mackay, I hear, has a
volume of verses —some of which have appeared
in The Bulletin— almost ready for publication.
Philip Lytton (he, by the way, it is asserted, is
going to build a theatre at Katoomba, and take
his company to perform there at acceptable
times) gave “Lady Windemere’s Fan” to
Sydney on two broiling nights, when a windmill
wouldn’t have raised a wind. But a packed
audience of good quality came, and was grilled on
the silver grill of Oscar Wilde’s epigrams.
“ Purity and goodness. What' have men like us
to do with such ! A well-thought-orit buttoh-hole
is much more effective,” declared a harmless local
colt. And “ The world is simply crammed with
good women. To know them is a middle-class
education.” It gave him a jolt to say it, even
after many rehearsals. Miss Oriel Hobson played
Lady Windermere, Miss Fletcher jvas the talky
Duchess, and Miss Bernard, a Tasmanienne of con-
siderable presence, acted Mrs. Erlynne. They did
very well—for amateurs. 1 Men, including Mr. Lyt-
ton himself, did very badly—even for amateurs.
Legs and arms of the male amateur actor look like
fatal accidents. In last scene of the second perform-
ance one of the mummers broke down ; the frailty
was due, ’tis said, to the presence (in a box) of
Mr. J. C. Williamson.

A true fish yarn comes from Wollongong
(N.S.W.). The local light-keeper seeing a lqng-
fish swimming leisurely along the harbor, har-
pooned him. The astonished fish left hurriedly
for mid-ocean, and, as the light-keeper had the
harpoon line tied round his arm, fish and fisher
left Wollongong together. Luckily three men in
a boat saw what happened, and, giving chase,
towed man and fish to land. The captured king-
fish measured f>£ feet in length.
“O’F.” : A married slattern, living near
my dwelling, spends a great part of each day
leaning over her unpainted front gate, bare-
armed, unwashed, and with her -hair in perpetual
curl-rags. Behind her stretches a “garden” of
rags and bones, broken crockery, arid bottles,, and
a brown patch where she empties out the tea-
leaves before each meal. Several dirty children
and a dog sprawl on the greasy verandah, and,
when they are not making too much noise, the
passer-by hears the phonograph in the “front
room ” singing “ Home, Sweet Home ” through
its nose. But nobody who is even slightly ac-
quainted with the gate-leaner can pass the house
without stopping. Any local Mrs. Smith or Mrs.
Jones is promptly buttonholed for a gossip, and
entreated to wait when the phonograph reeprd
runs out, while the music-loving lady rushes ipto
the house and sets the machine going once mqre.
Out she comes again, passing a table covered with
unwashed dishes, and. stumbling over a crawling
baby that badly needs a bath. Then conversation
is resumed, with the remark • “ i can’t never ’ave
enough er that lovely ‘ ’Ome, Swee Tome/ ”
“ H.G.” : Learned London correspondents
of Australian dailies have recently been explain-
ing why Russia has “ adopted the American word
‘hooligan’ instead of the Australian •larrikin’.”
Another injustice to England. The learned per-
son evidently confuses hooligan with hoodlum.
Hooligan is pure English, and is derived from
Patsy Hooligan, a Cockney of Irish descent, whose
name is still revered as that of a great prophet
through every tough quarter of the city. Vfh.en
in London, a couple of years ago, I ; was
being shown through Lambeth by one of “ the
boys”—a stunted lad with a suetty face and a
furtive look. He led me through the costers’
barrows of Lambeth Walk to a tiny opening in
one of the walls, and paused before a dirty,
disreputable house, in a small count, with the air
of a devout Moslem at the tomb of Mahoined.
“D’ye know what this ’ere ’ouse is?” he asked.
“No,” I replied, thinking it was a resort of Shak-
speare when he frequented the locality. “ That’s
whur Patsy ’Ooligan was born—’itn as give ’is
name to the ’Ooligans.” Subsequent inquiry
confirmed the story. Patsy was the leader of a
South London push, and eventually killed a
“John,” whose body he concealed iri a mud-cart.
The discovery of the body led to the crime being
traced, and Patsy was found guilty of man-
slaughter. Sentenced to life imprisonment, he
died in gaol after five or six years ; but he did not
live in vain. He has given a word to 40 million
Britishers and 100 million Russians.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 30, 1906.
My Dear Yarbie,—
.-A Himalaya passenger complains of the annoy-
ances caused by having people known as per-
on board. Firstly, the Jersey pair, a
aide or two, half-a-dozen notable satellites,
and Mr. Willie Kelly were upsetting the humble
voyager’s peace of mind, Then, at Fremantle,
the liner was kept half an hour waiting for
the Bigjohn Forrests. They, it appeared, had
missed the train to the port, and the whole ship
had its programme altered in consequence. When
the stout Federalist and his missus did at last
arrive, they were followed by a niece in festive
raiment and two maidens, in vestal white, bearing
great floral trophies. Then the Falstaff of the
party took a position at the head of the gangway
and cheerfully shook all fare welling hands like an
Eastern potentate in a sudden spasm of condescen-
sion. The voyager found the whole business try-
ing, and dreaded four weeks more with such a
shipload. A wise She goes to the head office and
carefully scans the booking-lists before embark-
ing. Then she knows the best, or the worst, to
be expected, and can act accordingly.
One sad social tragedy has occurred at a seaside
hotel. A girl managed to attach herself to a
large imposing chaperon in order to get her fiance
down for the New Year jollifications. Everything
was arranged satisfactorily. “It is nice to have
your own boy to fetch and carry at these picnic
places,” explained the joyous one to her green-
eyed mates. “Besides, he’ll be useful to all of
you. Don’t hesitate to put him to use. He’s in
training.” And the engaged one gave the girls an
artful wink. Well, one minx found him soattrac
tive that she couldn’t spare him for anybody else.
Down at that hotel for four days a lonely, wretched
girl wept on the shoulder of a large, tall chaperon,
while another flaunted with a deserter. Then two
sorry souls quitted the place and left the scandal
of the new love to the wrecking cause. The
maidens who saw all are taking to hear t the moral,
“ Never loan your chappie to your pals ” for picnic
purposes.
In announcing the approaching marriage of the
Rev. Charles Moore, an English naval chaplain,
and Miss Catherine de la Pole Clarke, the Eng-
lish Naval and Military Record and Royal
Dockyards Gazette (call it the “ Rec.,” for short)
refers to the bride as “elder daughter of his
Grace the Archbishop of Victoria, Australia.”
It’s a wonder nobody suggested the “ Archbishop
of Victoria” title as a way out during the Carr-
Clarke dispute as to who was Archbishop of
Melbourne.
The Rev. Hughes, who is rather famous for his
High Churchism at Eastern Hill, let a little light
in on the subject of his domestic life when, in an
attempted robbery charge the other day, he told
the court that he endeavored, to the best of his
ability, to rise without awakening his wife, but
unfortunately he couldn’t always manage it. The
fashionable congregation which crowds the Rev.
Hughes’ tabernacle was horrified at such a dis-
closure—it mostly rises and sets in one-half of the
house, out of hearing and sight of the worser half
which inhabits the other.
All the she-sports from the neighborhood of
Colac and Camperdown flocked into the hub for
the Polo match of Tuesday, and once more were
prevented by the weather from attending. The
Polo Club has suffered this season from most
uncalled-for furnace blasts and frantic dust-storms,
but Tuesday’s top-score temperature and scorch-
ing typhoon were the most outrageous experienced
on any day. No audience transpired, but two
valiant teams of steaming horsemen faced the field
and rode their small neddies at the fray with
praiseworthy grit. There was any amount of grit
in the air.
The Waxellency told his Frankston admirers
the other day that theirs was the loveliest spot on
earth, and more to that superlative end, and
worked by flowery metaphor up to the climax that
there it was he and his wife had done their
wooing. Loud applause and cries of encore. But
the Waxellency couldn’t encore, and further-
more had to extend the word of regret with an
apology for the dame’s absence. The shivoo had
been got up in her honor, but illness prevented
her starring. The cheerful Justice did a respon-
sive gush for both. Frankston is a pleasant sea-
side resort, but it has to swing the censer of
incense before the Madden clan to a foolish extent.
Accordingly it is shunned by many who object to a
family, however plump and prosperous, being
treated idolatrously. The sea-side tripper wants
a place where there can be some admiration for
her figure and her clothes, not a spot where the
crowd is always Maddening.
How naive the star mummer is ! Miss Tittell
Brune was down at the George Tallis beach
cottage, the other day, and all Carrum, Aspendale
and Mordialloc hummed with the tidings. (These
places hum at any old thing, from a bottle washed
upon the beach to a new calf in the district.) In the
sunny noon the lady went out for a dip in a fetch-
ing rig. The bathers circled round, and an army
of camera-owners begged respectfully for a snap-
shot. “ Why, by all means, take me, all of you ! ”
said the actress, and the whole salt population of
the sandy vicinity rings with the story. The
worshippers tell it with tears in their eyes. The
graciousness ! The sweetness ! The amazing cor-
diality of the consent! As if it were not the
lady’s business to act and to pose.
Victoriennes are pleased to hear of Colonel
Stanley’s extension of term as R.A.A. com-
mander. They/emember him a few years back as
one of the Collins-street bucks and a reliable polka
partner. He was so spry, young, and generally
attractive, don’t you know, that it came as a sort
of shock to learn that he was over 40, or whatever
the age is, and had to get a time extension.
The bagman has a fetching way with the sex.
What makes him a successful canvasser for orders
makes him also a successful suitor. One of his
sort faced the altar a few weeks back with one of the
handsomest of our dazzlers. Another charmer who
took the cake at the only smart assemblies of the
tripping season for two rosy winters, and had all
the young professionals hustling for her pro-
gramme, turned aside from half-a-dozen present-
able eligibles to mate with a young bagman who
hadn’t more than his last week’s commission to
wed on. No woman seemingly can withstand the
fascination of the man who can use the resources
of his trade to deliberately “canvass” her.
Tremendous trumpet-blasts have sounded over
the Blake-Jerrard matrimonial booking. The
girl was globe-trotting with her mother. They
had quitted their South Yarra eyrie a length of
time when they fell in with a military Irishman,
a Henry Claude Blake, with some powerful letters
after li’is label. He is Pursuivant-at-arms and
has foine Emerald Isle connections and lofty
kindred.
The Austral Salon rises out of its midsummer
damp to nibble a farewell scone and sip the
favorite brew along with the Dowager of Clive-
den, who climbs the gangway a tenth time for the
Cold Country almost immediately. The Salon
has always kissed the hem of the dowager’s dress,
she submitting without expostulation.
Miss Maggie M‘Cann, the one Scotch balladist
to make “Cornin’ thro’ the Rye” pay in Aus-
tralia, is off to Uncle Sam's Land, where she
believes the, Caledonian crowd to flourish like a
green bay tree. Before going, all Scots hereabout
will have to put their enthusiasm over the jovial
balladist into cash form and gather at a great
auld acquaintance concert in her cause.
The stories of Annette Kellermann’s fat salary
at the Hippodrome had solid foundation. Papa,
who has managed for the brown nymph, has
cabled out for the remnant of the family, and it
goes foursome (Mama and the three kids) in
February or March.
An extra splash was made at Her Majesty’s for
the Gr.G.’s party on Saturday. Not only was the
crimson carpet spread up the steps and out to the
entrance, but the centre portion of the circle was
hung in scarlet , and gold. The Northcote dame
drew a great spider-grey train into the gorgeous
pen, and his diminutive Ex. was helped to an
equal tail dignity by a trio of show-glass caddies
at his heel. The spider-grey was continued on
the upper-story of the dame, and honey colored
trimmings were as golden shafts on the web-like
raiment.
At the opening of the A.N.A. Show last Satur-
day the girl-machinists and factory experts, ranged
about plying their tools of trade, captured the eye
of the public. Their spruce dresses and aprons,
be-ribboned tresses, and clear, brisk look of health
astonished those folk who don’t know much about
factory hands. No swagger city shop could show
a gross of better-conditioned, better-dressed girl.
They turned out cigars, boots, and collars while
you waited, bending over their fast machines as if
a very fiend of impatience or haste possessed
them. Fancy any factory hand who puts in eight
hours of the most strenuous toil, with every nerve
and sense concentrated on the despatch of it, find-
ing time to dress the thatch trickily, and to coax
the figure ipto Paris-plate lines ! Doesn’t it seem
as if—to tinker with Tom Moore’s sentiment—
you may rack, you may torture the sex if you will,
but the patssion for “mashing” will cling to it
still!
An odd couple doing the rounds of the A.N.A.
show were Miss Yinia De Loitte and her veteran
wooer, Mr. Howard Vernon. The little mummer
wore fussy white chiffons and an unbecoming hat
that seemed somewhat of an extinguisher. Howard
Vernon towered a couple of feet above his pro-
mised mate. The pair went along enjoying the
gaping of the crowd.
Dr. Evans, dentist to Napoleon 111., in his
memoirs, just published, gives a hint on husband-
seeking :
One day before Napoleon’s affections were announced
the empress’s mother, the Countess of Montijo, pushed
her daughter into the water that the emperor might see
what a beautiful figure she had when she was “ rescued ”
with her light dress dripping and clinging to her.
As a device for figure display it was ingenious.
The Superintendent in charge of the Victorian
Detective Force told the Police Commission last
week that a good detective ought to be able to
join in Conversation with anyone and go anywhere.
He hadn’t many of that class of men in the force,
he admitted. Of the lot there were only about
half a dozen “whom he could send to, say,
Government House.” But surely any policeman
has “culture” enough to go to Government
House. Consider the people who go there !
Mary Ann chose a curious way of leaving
an Auburn (Melb.) home a while back. The
whole family had been for a seaside holiday.
They drove up in a cab, found the front door
open, and entered. Everything was in splendid
order. Preparations had been made for a meal
for the home-comers, and the kettle was just on
the boil, but Mary Ann was not to be found on
the premises. Uneasy feelings as to her where-
abouts were not settled until a week later, when
“the missus” heard from a tradesman that the
girl had become a wife. Too timid to give
notice, she had packed her dress-basket (taking
nothing but her own property), waited till she
saw the cab drive up, and then scooted at top
speed through the back gate.
The latest domestic yarn. A Mary Ann of
excellent qualities and disposition, a paragon in
short, left her Mistress for. matrimonial reasons ;
and on that distressed Marm expostulating and
reproaching her, Mary Ann let off steam. She
said she had taken no “ nights-off ” for six weeks
and demanded no holidays because she was
engaged and saving up. “It takes time and
energy to get a trousseau ” said Mary Ann. After
that the grieving Mistress wept no more. Her
social feelings were outraged by thought of a help
who embroidered pillow-shams and plied a decor-
ative needle in off-hours. So Mary Ann and
her Glory-box departed. A month later came
a small collection of wedding cake, and a paste-
board bearing the information that Mrs. Blankety
Blank (nee Mary Ann Drudge) would be at home
on the 3rd Wednesday. Then. madam took a
small fit.
The Rev. M. Noylan, of Essendon, has been on
a globe trot and extensive tour. Ihe other day
he told a pressman that he had a violent passage
across the Atlantic, when “ two or three unfortu-
nates were washed overboard.” Two or three! Such
naive unconcern on the parson’s part! And yet
he sometimes delivers this Matthew quote to his
congregation: “Are not two sparrows sold for a
farthing ? And one of them shall not fall on the
ground without your Father knowing.” “Two
or three souls washed overboard,” says his reve-
rence, affably.
Captain “ Kettle ” Collins is credited with a
desire to be appointed buying agent in the Big
Smoke for the Defence Department as a stepping-
stone to a better perch later on, when the High
Commissionership is an accomplished fact. The
gentleman generally has his eye on a good thing
for himself. Can there be any connection between
this rumor and Bigjohn Forrest’s trip to the Big
Smoke?- It was the Western Falstaff who installed
Mr, Collins in his secretarial berth. Now it seems
the arrangement is to be Bigjohn Forrest as H.C.,
with Captain Collins as his factotum. A precious
“Johnny ” administration that would be !
The success of Miss Bess Morris as an elected
associate of the Royal Society of Miniature
Painters in London recalls all kinds of im-
pressions of that industrious young painter,
notably that her mysticism embraced a belief in
re-incarnation. Miss Morris was fond of telling
her friends that in a former period of existence
she frequented the earth as an Egyptian. Written
in this way the thing looks like a joke, but the
artiste had a weird face and an intense manner, so
no one used to laugh. Everyone was duly im-
pressed.
Mrs. Langtree and her daughter are off for a
time abroad. The little circle of flutterers that
kept Toorak going strong is slowly breaking up
for matrimonial and touring reasons. Miss Jean
Langtree was one of the principals in all the revels
that enlivened Toorak last year.
After all, the woolly Osborne clan has not raised
an inventive genius. The new, visible-to-all parts
Scratching Board that made a notable debut at
the Terang Races is, I’m told, the invention of a
newspaper proprietor who is in no way connected
with the sheep clan.
Mrs. Tom Smith has hied back to the Big
Smoke with her little daughter Edith in tow.
The ewe-lamb is to be given every opportunity of
travel and, ahem !—the prettiest company. Miss
Edith is one of the tiniest girleens in Toorak, with
a huge capacity for having a good time. Two
elder sisters have married well, and that’s a
cheering consideration for a younger sister.
Miss Maggie Neill and her vocal sister have
sent out prosperous-looking pictures of themselves
from Durban, so that the elocuting r6le and the
tootle as raised in these parts must be employed
with success on the S’Africans. No worried plat-
former would have the patience or the playful
thought to get photographed in a smart frock and
a sweet smile if she wasn’t doing well.
The Oarty-Salmons are the one Federal couple
who return during recess to the electorate the
doctor represents and show joint and concerted
interest in the doings of the rural populace. The
pair do the district thoroughly, helping along
charity affairs and evincing concern in every
sparrow’s chirp throughout the electorate. They
may be smiling up their sleeves all the time, but
none can swear to it. Nobody wants to swear to
it. After all, the laugh is on the side of the voters,
who get so much personal acknowledgment of
their votes.
The value of a genteel appearance is constantly
being manifested. Very often it is the only
weapon available to the gentle fraud, and then
the gulled beat their breasts and sadly mourn
their want of perspicuity. But they are deceived
over a,nd over again. Latest proof of this is
afforded by a story from a certain village, where
some select dwellers pass the summer. These
choicest pebbles on the beach observe closely
the formalities of Toorak. So when a nice widow
occurred in their midst, and sported an infant
child at her cottage, the Cream called, after
soliciting some satisfactory details as to her
personality and domestic effects. The Cream
found the widow charming, and left many cards.
Thou came the denouement. A gossiping male
pointed out the cottage as the residence of a
Melbourne Benedict’s protegee, and told such a
precise story of her identity that all the dwellers
snorted violently, and are now planning a des-
perate programme of action by which the
“person” shall be affronted. The newcomer
said nothing, advertised nothing, pretended
nothing; she merely looked attractive, interesting,
and very guileless. And now the pasteboards of
the choicest lie waiting her consideration as to the
Second Tuesdays and Fourth Mondays of the
month.
Alarming news comes of a romantic and
Quixotic couple from this side, whose doings last
year were a rude shock to their acquaintances.
They “followed the man from Cook’s ” over the
hotel furniture and through the corridors, till even
the lift-boy snorted contempt and the hair of the
housemaid curled over her thatch with horror,
The pair made an extravagant display of wealth
at the start of their wedding trip, but the end
thereof was a pitiful anti-climax. Every penny
has been spent. They now look for assistance
to the bride’s people in Melbourne.
Yours Affectionately,

Legal Notice.
WHEREAS by an order of the High Court
of Justice Chancery Division made in the
Matter of the Trusts of the Settlement dated the
9th July 1872 made on the marriage of Alfred Jobp
Orme with Fanny Bax and in an action of
Nethersole v, Rouse 1905 O. 1203 the following
Enquiry was directed. Whether Edward Greer)
the Father of the Defendant Winfred Mabel
Green is living or dead and if dead wheq lie died
and who is or are his legal personal representa-
tive or representatives. Edward Green if living
or if dead his legal personal representative or
representatives and any person claiming to he
interested under the said Enquiry are by them-
selves or by their Solicitor to enter their names in
a Book kept for the purpose in Room 286 Royal
Courts of Justice Strand London England and
also on or before the 27th day of March 1906 to
come in and prove their Claims at the Chambers
of Mr. Justice Farwell and Mr. Justice Eady at
the said Royal Courts of Justice or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from
the benefit of the said Order. Tuesday the 3rd
day of April 1906 at 12 o’clock noon at the said
Chambers is appointed for hearing and adjudi-
cating upon the said Claims.
Dated the 15tli dav of December 1905.
SAMUEL A. M. SATOW, Master.
NOTE.—It is believed that the said Edward Green left
England in or about the year 1889 for Australia. He was
last heard of from c/o The Revd. Alfred T. Puddicombe,
Goulburn, New South Wn'es.
WARREN MURTON & MILLER,
45 Bloomsbury Square,
London, England.
Agents fer Richard Wilks, Solicitor, Deal, Kent, England.


POLITICAL
POINTS
Prime Minister Deakin should apply for
the Federal capital area as an improvement lease
through Mr. Willis. Then Mr. Carruthers
wouldn’t dare to make any objections.
Maoriland Premier Seddon now proposes
to move for trade reciprocity with the Common-
wealth.
Q. Premier Kidston still retains the
management of the finances, and announces a
continued policy of economy.
E. G. Jellicoe, the one-time Maoriland
barrister, who lately unloaded a barrowful of
Stinking Fish on the steps of the Commonwealth,
has gone under at the English elections, losing to
Conservative F. E. Smith by 709 votes.
“ Bungo ” : I have a splendid line of guns,
500 (imported), to offer the N.S.W. Secesh party.
The guns are single-barrelled flintlock, and were
last used by Hongi in his raid on the Arawas in
1822. Directions for use: Keep the other end
pointed from you.
The Labor candidate for Egmont (M.L.)
last elections put up a record for that province.
He carried his swag over 250 miles of the worst
track in M.L. ; he rode 120, drove 100, travelled
by steamboat 400, and by train 700 miles ; making
a total of 1570 miles. And he didn’t get in.
ELEVATION.
“The British have been noted for their power of raising inferior races over
whom they have exercised authority.”— Argus.
A BIT OF THE EARTH (interrupting choir-leader): "Say, Mister, you
haven't raised us much.”
There is a frank openness about Yic.
Premier Bent’s cultivation of his Brighton con-
stituency. With the connivance of the “reformed ”
Parliament he is spending tons of State money in
making an electric train from St. Kilda to
Brighton, and a second Suez canal through the
park at Elwood to the sea, to take the East
Brighton drainage.
Premier Kidston was by far the best
choice for Morgan’s mantle. He is firm, shrewd,
and canny withal. The basis of his political reli-
gion is that the State must pay its way. He
takes a deep personal concern in the fact that
in a few years Bananaland has loans amounting
to many millions falling due, and he sees that the
better the State’s credit is made by a process of
honest financing the more easily will the renewals
be obtained. Kidston was first elected as a Labor
representative, and he claims that, if there has
been any straining at the bonds between himself
and the crowd which professes to be striving
after something in the moon, it is none of his
doing.
Premier Price, of S.A., lately telegraphed
Premier Bent, of Vic., offering to distribute
tender forms for the Victorian loan in Adelaide,
and do what he could to make the flotation succes-
ful. The help wasn’t needed ; but fancy Premier
Carruthers, of N.S.W., making an offer of that
sort!
The name of Reuben Keirl, which figured
. prominently in the Melbourne newspapers some
, years ago, has cropped up once again. Premier
. Bent has created a Government billet for Reuben.
The gentleman travels round Victoria with a bio-
grapli, showing tit-bits of the land purchased for
closer settlement.
With all his booming revenue and bloated
surpluses, N.S.W. Premier Carruthers cannot
find funds to properly house the uu rtunate
cancer and consumptive patients in the Liverpool
Asylum. But money has been appropriated to
build a new morgue, properly brought up to date.
Which is a delicate hint to the sick poor.
The most disappointed persons in Quet s-
land at the present time are the Philp party, Who
hoped that the retirement of Morgar trom the
Premiership would lead to internal dissension and
the collapse of the coalition. With his capacity
' Kidston should hold the party together until its
work is completed.
“B.” wants to know: If she must wail,
why does she call herself the “Mother State” ? In
most families I know mother does not bawl for
Johnnie’s share of the lollies, nor does she cry be-
cause Dorothy won’t give her her dolly. Let
N.S. Wales cry for Dorothy’s dollyif she wants to,
but if she does, please ask her to drop the name
of “mother.”
j Tom O'Sullivan, Bananaland’s new Minis-
ter for Works, is, though a lawyer, of broad
,democratic views. Last session he did good ser-
vice in the Fossil House in helping the liberal
.measures introduced by the Morgan Government.
O'Sullivan resigns his seat among- the Fossils and
will stand for the Warwick electorate vacated by
ex-Premier Morgan.
Chanter, M.H.R., has a slight grievance.
•At the request of the anti-Federal mob, his name
has been boo-hooed in public and in print as a
“traitor” because he voted for Dalgety as the
Federal capital. Mr. Chanter doesn’t mind being
hooted by that sort of crowd: he takes it as a
compliment; but he doesn’t like to get unde-
served compliments, and so points out that he
was not in Parliament when the site was chosen,
and that in the first Parliament he voted for
Tumut.
Charles Atkins deserves credit for having
discovered the true meaning of a Vic. Chamber of
Manufactures. Until he came along and shook up
the old concern, the Chamber seemed to exist only
for the purpose of objecting to the Trades Hall, and
snorting at the Factories Act. It talked Capital
y. Labor. Atkins gave it the new character of
a Union for Local Industry and Increasing the
Output of Wages. Some of the manufacturers are
astonished to find that in their Protectionist
capacity they are sworn friends of the working
man.
“Lil”: In the course
of boarding-house peregrina-
tions I have met with several
working-men’s wives who
have been induced, by the
dazzling prospect of becom-
ing acquainted with the wives
of the beknighted, to listen
to fatuous Women’s League
lectures about the threatened
purity of the ’ome and the
attempts to destroy the
sanctity of the hen-roost. I
have observed that these
women, whose vote and in-
fluence is bought by Lady
Hyphen’s smile, invariably
belong to one of two types —
the married, boarding-house
slattern with “no encum-
brance,” or the lady in lodg-
ings whose one or two encum-
brances have to look after
themselves as best they can
while their mother wastes
her time and her husband’s
money in all sorts of foolish
gadding about. It is the
woman who is too lazy to
look after the cleanliness of
a home who gets agitated
by the Tory anxiety for its
“ purity.”
General lan Hamilton
is trenchant, obliquely, on
the English, in his new book
on what he saw as attache
with the Jap. army in the
war. He says surrender men
would never be able to stand
before no-surrenderers. Yet
he th inks no Japanese general
equalled Kuropatkm. It gave him a pang to see
white men, Russians, made prisoners by darkies-
This might be due to his Sepoy experience.
Present Q. Commissioner of Police took
up his duties with a great reputation as a strong
man. Recently the traffic came under his con-
trol, and it was announced, among other changes,
that under the new regulations no persons would
be allowed to stand on tramcars. But Mr.
Badger, the monopoly’s manager, objected, and
the police promptly climbed down.
The Federal Public Service Commissioner
recently promised that he would give some of the
back-block officials a chance in the civilised parts
of this country; but so far the threat is hanging
fire-. Some have been stuck out at the back
of beyond for 15 and 20 years and see no
sign of a change. Unless a man desires it three
years should be the limit of service in the “Num-
ber six district.’ ’
Telegram to Sydney Daily Telegraph
about its pet capital site :
Carcoar, Saturday.—Early potato crops at Lyndhurst
are stated to have turned out a complete failure owing
to the want of rain last mouth. Corn crops are, how-
ever, reported to be. in a healthv condition in the same
locality. Rabbits are stated to be almost as thick as a
plague of grasshoppers ; poisoning is having little or no
effect on their numbers, and many owners have given up
hope of ever conquering them.
Seems rather a nice place.
One of the most hopeful resolutions of the
N.S.W. Labor Conference now sitting was for the
appointment of a committee of five to formulate
a comprehensive land and financial policy. This
emanated from the Executive. Hitherto, it has
always been a weak spot in the Labor party’s
armor that it forced on the reforms and left it to
the other parties to find the money. If the com-
mittee makes a good job of its work, a lot of
voters who have hitherto fought shy of the Labor
party on account of its financial irresponsibility
will be won over.

FINNEY : “ Quinn’s widely, is it, ye say? Why , I
asked her myself ten days afore she married
wid Quinn. Be me soul, it’s a narrer e
O’ve had!”
The directors of the Byron Bay (N.S.W.)
Co-operative Butter Factory—far-sighted men—
have put two big heavy boots on colored labor in
the dairying industry. Some of the farmers tried
to increase their big cheques by employing Hindus,
whereupon the directors gave notice that no cream
would be received from farms on which colored
men were employed in the milking yards.
At that sick “secesh” Sydney meeting
there was distributed a strongly anti-Federal
pamphlet (printed by the N.S.W. Government
Printer), “The Position of N.S.W. as a Partner
in the Australian Commonwealth.” Now, who
authorised that, and who paid the cost ? Is it to
be allowed that Premier Carruthers should use the
State’s resources to bolster up his little party
games ?
“ Caber” : The ways of tffie politician are
dark -and devious. Scarcely two months ago,
Bananaland Assembly passed a Monopoly Tax
Bill, and sent it along in due course to the Lethal
Chamber, which promptly booted the Bill down
stairs. Then Premier Morgan balanced himself
on the apex of virtuous indignation, threatened
that the Bill would again be sent up, and, if they
again rejected it, that the Chamber would be cast
forth into outer darkness. Now Morgan has
accepted the Presidency of the Chamber he was
going to destroy; ■. Was his declamation all a
ponderous joke ?
Melbourne Herald bowls out the latest
of the Argus’ Stinking Fish yarn—that the “pre-
ference to Britishers ” clause in the Contract Im-
migrants Act was only inserted through the tire-
less efforts of the wearied Opposition, whose mem-
bers sat up all night and forced the amendment
on the Ministry with early dawn. As a reference
to Hansard shows, Dugald Thomson had no
sooner made the suggestion than the Prime
Minister accepted it, and it was finally carried by
31 votes—2o Ministerialists and 11 Oppositionists
—to six. The whole debate filled only seven
pages of Hansard.
Sydney newspaper headings of last week :
Railway Inqciry.
Resignation of a Commissioner.
But, alas, it wasn’t the resignation of either of the
two railway commissioners, who forced on the
country a disastrous coal contract; but the resigna-
tion from the Royal Commission inquiring into the
scandal of the only one of its members with a
courageous appreciation of his public duty. This
retiring commissioner, Mr. Knox, resigned (so it
was first announced) on account of “pressure of
business ”; but he had very good reason for re-
signing owing to the unpleasantness caused by his
zeal.
An amusing boycott has been going on in
Brisbane for years. Thynne, Leg. Councillor,
offended the proprietors of Brisbane Telegraph,
and the order was given, “the name of Thynne
is never to be printed in this paper.” Thynne
looms largely in the local eye, and is fond of
airing his eloquence, but he is reported as “an
M.L.C.,” “a visitor,” or “another person pre-
sent.” Even when others refer to Thynne in
speaking, the embargo is still enforced.
When Mr. R. H. Cowles, assistant general
manager in Australia for the International Har-
vester Company, was asked by Mr. Wamsley at
the Tariff Commission if “the Standard Oil Com-
liany ” had any interest in the ships which brings
lis company’s goods to Australia, he replied: “I
am perfectly certain it has not.” And Mr. Cowles
was right. There is no such corporation as The
Standard Oil Company ; therefore, it could have
no interest in anything. There are 32 Standard
Oil Companies, such as the S.T.O. Co., of Ohio,
of Illinois, of New York, and other States. And
these 32 companies and a number of others, such
as the General Transport Co., are merged into
the Standard Oil Trust. And, legally, there is
lib such thing as the Standard Oil Trust. It is
not a corporate or legal body. It is only an
illegal combination of firms which have handed
control of their business over to a secret board.
In dealing with the agents and representatives of
the American combine this fact must always be
kept in mind. Tlie foreign Harvester companies
are.mixed up in the same manner, and the Inter-
national Harvester Company of America and the
International Harvester Company (of the world)
are in a sort of a way distinct. But there is no
doubt about their common aim to destroy Austra-
lian industry.

The value of closer settlement is strikingly
illustrated at Willaura, the centre of a new wheat
growing area in Southern Vic. The cartage of
the wheat sacks required for this year’s, grain
brought in more revenue than the cartage of the
whole wool clip in the squatters’ best days.
Melbourne railway offices are suffering
from a plague of pigeons, a wild family having
settled among the statuary over the door. Now
and then they lay eggs, and, as some of the nirds
are bad shots, these roll over the cornice and
smash on the footpath below. The other day, an
egg came down just as Premier Bent was entering
the building and exploded with considerable sound
at his feet. “What ho ! ” cried Tommy, “ what’s
the blooming matter ? This ain’t election time.”
When it was explained where the egg had come
from, Thomas looked up with admiration. “My
oath,” he said, “they’re intelligent birds an’ no
error.”
Lord Rosebery lately made a strong ap-
peal to the town council of Ayr to save Burns’s
Auld Brig, which for some time has been giving
way to old age. He wrote :
There is a terrible rumor current that the Auld Brig of
Ayr is condemned, and is to be demolished, and I cannot
help writing to you, as an Ayrshire man, to ask if such a
calamity cannot be averted. I would urge you, in the
name of Burns and his traditions, not to contemplate re-
construction until preservation is proved to be impos-
sible.
Which is a lot of fuss to make over one of the
inanimate subjects of Burns’ song. But where is
Rosebery’s solicitude for the Scotch crofters and
lassies—of whom also Burns sang —driven out of
Scotland by Freetrade and Landlordism ?
Opposition leader Joseph Cook will never
lead anything but an Opposition. He has some
ability, much industry, but his manner is against
him. Cook shows his teeth at the slighest provo-
cation. One of the “border-line” members of
the Reps, is Tom Brown, who beat B. R. Wise
for the Canoblas, and has been Canoblas’ choice
since ; he fluctuates between tbe Freetrade party
and the Labor party, and confesses a divided
allegiance. The other day Mr. Brown wandered
into the newspapers with some harmless remarks
on the political situation. Promptly next day
Mr. Cook snarled fiercely at him. As a matter of
tactics, Mr. Brown should be nicely buttered;
then he might slide softly into the Reid camp.
But Mr. Cook spreads thorns around his citadel
and warns off recruits.
Prime Minister Deakin speedily pricked
the bubble of anti-Socialism when Chambers of
Manufacturers’ delegates presented their resolu-
tions, the other day. They were all quite sure
that Socialism was inimical to the best interests
of the Commonwealth, but not a solitary delegate
could say what Socialism was. Also, though
they “opposed all proposals for the nationalisa-
tion of the producing and manufacturing indus-
tries,” they entered no protest against any State
Government making its own engines (as Victoria
does), against State Agricultural Departments
regulating and supervising certain branches of the
export trade, or against the same departments
experimenting in the growing of cereal and root
crops, and afterwards teaching the producers how
to produce with profit. Also, they assured
Deakin of their cordial support to his anti-Trust
Bill—to come on next session—calmly ignoring
the fact that this same Bill is a “ gross interference
with the liberty of the subject ” and an “undue
restriction of private enterprise.”
OUR INDEPENDENCE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"Japan does not wish to dominate Australia ; that is
an alarmist cry. Such a powerful ally will enable us to
maintain our prestig'e and independence.”—Mr. John See
at the A.N.A. Exhibition on Anniversary Day.
With naval aid,
Which isn’t dear,
Although it’s paid
For every year,
Why feel afraid
And quake with fear
Preposterous !
Our Noak’s Arks
We need not arm !
As See remarks:
Why feel alarm ?
Some well-fed sharks
Do little harm.
Why certainly I
The Jap is kind
As well as smart.
Ilis noble mind
And gentle heart
Might surely find
Their counterpart
In Fairyland !
Let’s praise the Japs,
In sackcloth girt!
Take off our caps ;
Crawl in the dirt !
And then perhaps
They’ll never hurt
Australia.
W. T. GOODGE.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
On Sunday last, in Sydney Harbor, a motor-
launch was cut down by the steamer Manly. The owner
and his wife were injured in the impact, and died from
shock and the effects of the immersion. Four children
were rescued in an almost miraculous manner. A little
ten-year-old girl was kept afloat on a cushion. The six-
year-old boy was held up by his brother for the 15 minutes
that it took the steamer to get back and lower a boat.
The other girl was kept up by a life-buoy. Probably the
parents would have been rescued also but for the fact
that they had been smashed in the collision. It seems
that the steamer and launch were both travelling to the
same point at different angles. One witness said the
captain told him he thought they were quite clear, till
they disappeared from his line of vision, but by
that time they were close to the apex of the tri-
angle—and then the disaster. This is one of the
accidents that always accompany new conditions.
When most of the ferry steamers were built the only
craft they had to dodge were sailing boats and other
steamers. Rowing boats never troubled them, except in
close proximity to the wharves, where speed was
necessarily slow. The sailing craft was easily noticeable
with its spread of canvas and reasonably high mast.
Steamers and steam launches had their whistles to draw
attention to their presence, and, for the conditions of the
time of their building, the steering boxes were quite suit-
able. But the conditions changed with a bang. In a few
months a big mosquito fleet of motor boats sprang into
existence, and they are übiquitous from Ryde to the Heads.
These motor launches lie low in the water, low as a
rowing boat many of them. The most any of them carry
is a flagpole, and scores of them have not even that.
Neither have they any whistle or other appliance to at-
tract attention, and to see this class of craft it is neces-
sary that the wheel-house of the ferry steamers should be
right in the bow, located so that the captain can see the
foam curling away from under the forefoot. The motor-
boat may be a nuisance, but it carries human lives, and it
has to get its dues. It is necessary that regulations should
be framed to compel ferry companies to place their steer-
ing-boxes where the officer in charge can have an abso-
uninterrupted view of the water in front of him.
This is necessary in the interests of the ferry-steamers as
well as those of the motor-boats, for if a 40ft. oil-launch
ran full tilt into a crowded ferry-boat, she would pierce it
like a torpedo, and bring about the harbor holocaust that
Sydney dreads. Some of the ferry-boats have their steering-
boxes well arranged, and until the rest get them similarly
placed, their steamers will be at the mercy of any drunken
or incompetent boat-motorist who happens to be in a
launch large and heavy enough to do mischief, and will
be slaughterous to little launches.
• * » *
Dr. _ Piers Hatton is going to have trouble to
maintain his speed supremacy of Port Jackson. An
80-h.p. flier is being built at Berry Bay, and late owner
Gorrick of the Standard 111., which the doctor bought, has
announc'd his intention of adding to the money obtained
for the Standard 111. to produce a boat that will carry a
broom at the top of her flagpole.
* * * *
Neither the wind nor the water favored the
rowing events at the Sj'dney Anniversary Regatta, but
for motors it was ideal. To everybody’s disappointment
Dr. Piers Hatton’s Lady Marjorie did not start in the
handicap for boats with from 7 to 11 miles an hour speed,
and the event was won by the Hercules by seconds. In
the event for boats over 11 miles an hour no one cared to
take on Dr. Hatton’s Lady Eileen (late Standard III.),
which ran over the 13-mile course in 53 minutes.
* * • *
W. J. Duncan’s 18-footer, Mona, had an after-
noon out on Friday at the Anniversary regatta, where
the sailing events are always the more interesting. In
the All Boats Handicap, with 4£min. start, she
romped away from the fleet easily. Chris Webb’s crack
crew were in the Sydney Australian on scratch, and
running' past the whole fleet, came gamely at the Mona,
but had no hope of overtaking her, and were finally
beaten by 25sec. In the General Handicap, the Mona
was again on the same mark, and Chris Webb once more
on scratch. Passing the four boats in front, Mona went
first round the flag-boat, with Sydney Australian right at
the tail. But Webb came on in great style, and at the
line was only a second behind Duncan’s streaker.
* * * *
A dingy contest between boats representing
Sydney and Botany resulted in the Ma city getting an
unholy doing from its smellful suburb. They were
cynical dogs, the Botany sportsmen. They bought the
best boats Sydney had, hired some of the best skippers
to navigate them, and scooped the pool. It was the
story of Narses and the Goths over again.
* * * *
A stiffish breeze from east-nor’-east and floods
of sunshine were the conditions under which the Prince
Alfred Yacht Club raced over a course from the historic
Fort Denison in Sydney harbor, round a mark steamer
live miles to windward of South Reef, back to Farm Cove
in Sydney Botanic Gardens. It was one of the “ legs ”of
the club championship, and, in addition to money prizes
of ten, four and two guineas for place boats, Coleman
Burke gave a trophy. Of the six starters Magic was on
scratch, and the limit boat (Scotia) had 7min. 6sec. start.
They swept away on a flying start, and by the time
Bradley’s Head was looming close had worked into two
lines of three each. Passing Watson’s Bay, Petrel had
worked far in front of the fleet, and was the first to dip
through the frowning harbor gates. Outside the sea was
smooth as a billiard-table, and the wind falling. As they
thrashed away towards South Reef, Petrel led round the
steamer, with Culwitlla and Magic (53sec. and smin. Issec.)
behind, and as they turned the spinnakers swelled out
and drove the yachts with bird-like motion for the Heads.
Petrel still rushed ahead, with Culwulla right on her
tiller, but passing Parsley Bay she went in front, and
shortly afterwards jibed across the Petrel’s bows, cut
down in front, and led over the line. When the rating
allowances had been adjusted it was found that Petrel
had won, with Culwulla and Magic second and third.
This victory placed the Petrel so far in front of the other
competitors that, no matter what happens in the last
race of the series, it will not deprive her of the champion-
ship.
• * * »
The Maoriland built yacht Rawhiti did not
set the harbor on fire in the All Yachts Handicap at the
Sydney Anniversary regatta, finishing second last in a
fleet of eight. She is an ocean boat, pure and simple,
and likes the wind with plenty of weight in it. On
Saturday she was a thing of beauty and an object of
desire outside the Heads, when her vast area of white
canvas made her look like a silver etching on a sheet of
cobalt.
• * * *
Why, after winning the toss in the jubilee
match against Victoria, N.S.W. skipper Noble chose to
bat on a soft wicket is a mystery. Perhaps he thought
more rain would fall and make the thing worse, or per-
haps he wanted to give Victor Trumper a chance to
bolster up his toppling greatness, for that erratic
batsman adores a soft wicket. The batsmen were
in trouble at the start, and the great Noble retired
hastily for none. Then Trumper came to save the
situation, and never did batsman have a narrower squeak
than he. A fast one from Saunders curled across the
wicket and just shaved the off stump, but the bails held.
Then Trumper broke loose, getting off the chain with 19
off Collins in one over. Cuts, drives and back-cuts all
came the same to him. He was a dancing dervish, a bats-
man possessed, playing strokes no other batsman ever
dreamed of before, and landing the ball in all
manner of unexpected places, including the roof of the
northern pavilion. He smacked up 101 in an hour, in-
cluding a six and 16 fours. Later on it looked as though
Cotter was iroing to repeat Trumper’s performance. He
started with a terrific burst, scoring 21 off his first over,
landing the leather over the fence in a manner that made
his batting as dangerous to the general public as his
bowling is to the batsmen. In 18 minutes he bashed 35
off the Victorian bowlers, and then a fast one from Collins
broke through his guard and Cotter was out.
* * * •
The match ended in the defeat of Victoria.
With a wet wicket N.S.W. notched 263 in the first
innings, and Victoria responded with 215. N.S.W. then
knocked up 394, and the Victorians started their second
effort 442 behind. Notwithstanding a game effort by
M’Alister, who made 128, the side only managed to notch
297, leaving N.S.W. victorious by 145 runs.
* * * *
The dry rot that has fallen on Victorian cricket
is beginning to alarm the authorities, and it is proposed
to try the electorate system as a remedy. At present the
best players are mopped up by the crack clubs, and when
all the good players are in two clubs it means that most
of them are shelved indefinitely. The advantage of the
electorate system is that each district must just do the
best it can with the material it has got, and the result is
a lot more earnest endeavor and bigger scope for good
players.
* * -* *
By virtue of a recent re-arrangement, a hit over
the paling fence at the Sydney Cricket Ground now
brings six, instead of five.
* * * *
Saunders bowled remarkably well on Friday,
and late in the day even, when the wicket had recovered
from the rain, he was turning the ball. He secured the -
first seven wickets at a cost of 84 runs, the “ bag ” in-
cluding Mackay, Trumper, Noble, Duff, Diamond, and
Waddy. His delivery, too, was fair enough for anything ;
and it looks as if the selectors were unnecessarily nervous
on that account when they left him out of the recent
syndicate that visited England.
* • * -
J. R. Maclcay’s total of 902 runs for N.S.W. in
first-class matches this season knocks all previous indivi-
dual efforts for the State into a heap, and spreads itself
out at the splendid average of 112-75 per innings. Mackay
came from Uralla (N.S.W.), where he made mammoth
scores in years gone by.
* • * •
Charlie M‘Leod, another member of the last
Australian XI., has announced his retirement from
cricket. He is the sixth player to leave before being de-
cisively though gently deposited on his ear on the mat,
the others being Howell, Kelly, Sid Gregory, Hopkins and
Newland. The next Australian XI. will be quite a new
combination.
* * * *
Evidently the crack cricketers are born and not
made, and there is apparently something in the Sydney
air that breeds ’em. The country districts never have any
show against the metropolis, and when the Sydneysider
travels he quickly rises to a position of importance in
local cricket wherever he may settle. An instance is
that of K. H. Quist, an ex-Sydney cricketer, who captained
Westralia against South Australia, on Saturday last, and
p>ad@ top score for his side (47) in the face of fielding
described as “ very brilliant."

* * * •
Horse-racing may still be the sport of kings, but
that of millionaires and the worst people generally is
motor-racing. Alfred Vanderbilt’s latest bid for the
fastest car in the world is a 50 h.p. whizzer which is being
built to a special design in New York, and will bear the
name of the “ Herald.” The stipulated speed is 152 miles
an hour. Remarkable as it may seem, quite a number of
drivers applied for the position of chauffeur, and a peppery,
absinthe-drinking Frenchman named Paul Sartorio
secured the job. Nobody in this office, at any rate,
desires to deprive him of it. The new car will make its
first appearance on the Florida Beach track, where the
cars race over the sand so swiftly that they haven’t time to
sink into it.
* * • *
That long-looked-for motor meeting really did
transpire at Aspendale Park (Vic.) last Monday, before a
4000 audience. The motor-bike handicap saw some fast
travelling, but nothing of a record-breaking nature,
O. De Fraga, of Sydney, winning the five miles in
6min. 3 2-ssec., or at the rate of 49-5321 miles
per hour. The heavy car Five Mile contest,
for cars over 14 h.p., brought out seven starters,
including G. Hobbs in the Mercedes which cut such a
speed in the Dunlop Reliability. The starts he had to
concede were frightful to see, the limit being 1060 yards.
R. Edols in a 16-h.p. Talbot was on the 950 mark, and
contrary to all expectations the Mercedes was slow
getting away, while the English car started with a
bound, and shrieked round the track in 7min.
16 4-ssec. Kellow’s Beeston-Humber was on the 850
line, and the Talbot just managed to maintain the 100 yd.
start, while the Mercedes roared up to within 50yd. and
secured third place. But for his inability to get through,
it is just possible that Hobbs would have given the winner
a great tussle at the finish, in spite of the slow start. The
handicapping was very faulty, but it was all guess-work,
owing to lack of previous performances.
♦ * * #
The world’s record made by the Italian, Anzani,
on his 4£-h.p. Buchet motor-bike has been broken at last.
Giuppone, another Italian, mounted on a 6-h.p. twin-
cylinder Peugeol -machine, flashed over 63£ miles in the
hour on the track at the Princes’ Park, Paris.

Victorian League of Wheelmen is using the
mailed fist on the unregistered riders. Fifteen
have been disqualified for life, and, to cut deep, the
League has included the trainers who prepared the men.
Fae victis!' Which being translated into the vulgar
tongue, means: Rub his nose thoroughly in the gutter.

Already 500 racing licenses have been issued by
the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen for 1906. How the
scorcher increases in the land 1
* * # •
Ivor Lawson is again announced to leave for
’Murka or other foreign parts, present week. Contrary to
expectations he did not appear at the A.N.A. meeting in
Melbourne, and his absence did not wreck the meeting, as
Henri Mayer. Walter Rutt and Floyd MacFarland were
there. Rutt won the first heat of the Gold Stakes, a £lOO
event, to be decided over half, one and five miles with a
grand three mile final, but Floyd MacFarland won com-
pensation in Five Miles scratch race, which he captured
easily. Writer has an idea that the long rider will
waive the appearance money and have a try for the
Sydney Thousand.
* * * •
That ardent Sydney motorist, W. J. O. Elliot,
has now struck a mode of life that must be congenial.
He runs tourists and business men who want to be inde-
pendent of the railways and coaches, through the
Northern Rivers wilderness. The tourist, or debt-
collector, or whatever he may be, sends along a list of the
places he wants to visit, and Mr. Elliot works out a time-
table, and generally manages to adhere to it. Altogether,
Elliot has proved that as long as one does not want to
break records, the motor-car, in the hands of a careful
driver who knows his business, is as reliable as an ordinary
passenger train, and has the added advantage of mobility.
Evidently in the near future the motor-car, when the
cost falls somewhat, is going to be a big rival to the
State railway system.

coming, Nigel Barker shall represent N.S.W. at the

» • * *
A horrible Sydney holiday fatality was the
death of a man bathing: in George’s River. A shark
attacked him, tore one hand off, and practically stripped
his back of flesh. The man straggled to the shore and
died almost immediately. Many years ago a party of
surf bathers at Oamaru, Maoriland, were astounded to
find a shark suddenly among them. As a rule the shark
is a clumsy, cowardly brute, which a fair amount of
shouting and splashing will scare off. This brute was
different, and when the bathers started splashing he
attacked them. They streaked for the shore, and, when
in comparatively shallow water, one man was gripped by
the leg. His mates held on to him, and the shark tore
the limb off at the knee, and then caught another man by
the arm. The tide was running out, and next moment
the shark was aground. Both his victims were rescued,
although the one who had lost his leg died almost imme-
diately, and the other went mad. The shark remained
high and dry, and the vengeful humans brought firewood,
and, piling it round him, roasted him alive on the sand.
They say that the sharks abandoned the Oamaru beach
for years afterwards. But that idea probably arose from
the fact that no one dreamed of bathing in such a
dangerous surf,
* • • *
Kieran Memorial Fund will benefit to the
extent of about £5O by a smoke concert given by the
National Sporting Club of Sydney, Director Brennan
gave the free use of the hall and shouldered whatever

worked as hard as if it were their own benefit they were
grafting for,
* * * *
It seems that pushball has got a footing in
Victoria, and is threatening to supplant bowls among the
old chaps. At Daylesford recently an ex-M.P. competed,
and the combined ages of himself and the nag he rode
totalled 80 years. The man was 58 and the steed 22.
* * * *
One of the attractions of Sydney National Park
is retired world’s champion sculler, Bill Beach, who does
duty as ranger and takes to the new billet as if born
for it.
* « # *
When a stallion fails to prove a top-notcher at
the stud, his value depreciates woefully. Two years ago
when the Sylvia Park (Auckland) stud was dispersed, the
Melton sire, Seaton Delaval at 1700g5., was considered
cheap. T’other day he went for 500 gs.
» * * •
It is difficult to see how the A.J.C. Committee
could have done otherwise than uphold the decision
whereby the owner of the racehorse Dalgety loses the
chance of racing the colt for a twelvemonth. The horse
had been leased to a Randwick trainer, and the trainer,
jockey, and horse were disqualified. The owner of the horse
was miles away, and had no difficulty in proving his entire
innocence. But, as his horse was disqualified, he was
directly punished by the verdict. Hence his appeal,
which was unanimously dismissed ; the A.J.C. committee
held that if an owner was absolved from all conse-
quences of the actions of a trainer, a wide door
would be opened to the unscrupulous; and there are
trap-doors enough already for the punter to fall through.
In this instance the verdict is a heavy one for the colt’s
owner, and as horses are leased under similar circum-
stances by the score, the decision has caused a flutter
among sports.
* • , * *
One-time much-followed Ike Earnshaw, who,
during Mr. Hordern’s turf time, was his private trainer,
threatens to again become the power he was on the race-
course. A. J.O.’s two-days Anniversary meeting saw five
from Earnshaw’s stable win. Five stables gathered the
12 events of the programme thus: Earnshaw 5, L.
Wootton 3, R. O’Connor 2, and S. Fielder and T. Payten
one each.
• * • *
Trainer-owner Wootton’s phenomenal luck.
Apart from vvhat the bookies had to pay him during last
V.R.C. Spring meeting he has scored remarkably since, in
Sydney, with other people’s hot-wanted nags. Victorian
Jimmy Wilson’s Fabric had only been bought from that
astute turfite a few weeks before appropriating Sydney
Tattersall’s Cup and a huge sum in bets. Then came
Hollander’s win, also those of Sinimeran, The Pet and
Dromedary at A.J.O. meeting: last week, all at the first
time of asking- since Wootton acquired them. Simmeran
cost 250g5., The Pet 200g5., and Dromedary 75gs.

Recent shutting up of famous Wellington Park
(M.L.) stud reminds that in 16 years 427 yearlings bred
there brought 76,158g5. Best year was 1892, when 17
youngsters realised 7315g5.

Newcastle’s ancient Ryde was bought by trainer
E. Donovan early this month for 11 guineas, and last
week won the Oamden race meeting double—Flying
and Oamden Handicaps.
* * * *
Wholesale prosecutions for keeping common
gaming houses have caused much lamentation among
Maoriland bookies, and already a strong exodus has set in
for Australia.
* * * *
As Skuthorpe would not agree to Martini’s
terms re the saddle, Martini has now accepted Skuthorpe’s
challenge to find a man who will ride Skuthorpe’s buck-
jumper, for £lOO a-side. Martini’s money has been de-
posited at this office. His man will ride in an ordinary
hunting saddle, without pads of any kind, provided Sku-
thorpe’s men will do as Martini’s do, hold the horse till he
is properly saddled and the man properly mounted. The
match is to take place in the presence of press representa-
tives in Sydney;
* * * #
Reported from England that Russian wrestler
George Hackenschmidt is suffering from miner’s elbow,
brought about by using that part as a fulcrum with which
to lever opponents on the floor on to their shoulders. Con-
sequently, his return match with Terrible Turk Madralli
for £2OO aside is off. Although the Russian won easily
last time they met, the Turk is said to have improved a
lot under scientific training and dieting, while the Rus-
sian has gone off, and the damaged arm is likely to fail
him at any time.
* * * ■»
They take their football seriously in Wales.
All Cardiff went to see the big match with the Maori
team ; and, after 45,000 were admitted, the gates had to
be closed. Wales won, by the merest margin, and then
all Wales went mad. The South Wales Daily News
(Cardiff) came out on the Monday with 14J columns of
small print, three lots of pictures, and a bit of verse, con-
cerning the match. Altogether, it was a great day for
Wales ; and one is inclined to overlook the fact—as care-
fully chronicled by the same paper—that “ a ver3' large
percentage of the people became decidedly tipsy.” By
the way, wasn’t it at Cardiff that Evan Roberts, the
evangelist, got half the people to swear off football and
burn the unholy leathern affair altogether?
* * * *
For a long while the French have strenuously
objected to the provision that made it necessary to
change the venue of the Gordon Bennett race every year,
and that fixture has, it is now announced, been aban-
doned for good. Its place will be taken by a classic race
run over French roads yearly, and controlled exclusively ,
by the French governing body.

The bookmaker is becoming a plague at the
Sydney National Sporting Club. 'His continual “ Five to
four ! five to four! ” is wearisome. What influence he
has on the fights is not, so far, apparent, but if he has
any it is a certainty that it is for the bad. Anyhow, in
the interests of the club, which is a good one and worth
upholding, the bookmaker should be vigorously sup-
pressed. The fact that during the last few months there
have been rather more fiascoes than usual would seem to
indicate that he is beginning to get some fine work in.
LIKE A SOLID LUMP
HOW FOOD CAUSED PAIN
AND DISTRESS IN
THE STOMACH.
SEVERE INDIGESTION
CURED BY
MOTHER SEIDEL’S
SYRUP.
Mr. Thomas Brown, a carpenter,
pf Grote St,, West Adelaide, writes
under date, March 9, 1905 : —“ For
several years I suffered frequently
from severe attacks of indigestion.
My food seemed to settle in a hard
solid lump in my stomach ; causing
much pain and distress in my
stomach and right side, It Became
extremely difficult for mg t© Stick to
my daily work. I tried several doc-
tors, but their treatment did not help
me, At last on the advice of my
friend, Mr, W. James, the well-known
blacksmith @f Currie Street, I used
MotherSeigel'g Syrup. Three bottles
of it completely cured me. That was
a year ago and I have been well ever j
since.” '
*** Your food will become nourish- !
ing and pleasant and you will have j
no pain or distress in your stomach f
if you will use Mother Seigel's
Syrup , either as a cure or as a pre-
ventative. You can get it at all
up-to-date Chemists' shops .
A Fine Old Pioneer Preacher.
The dwellers of the bush and small townships of
Australia have often been regarded as a Godless
race and of recent years greater efforts have been
made for the propaganda of the Gospel through
the agency of associations. The increased facilities
of travelling by the extention of railways and
better means of communication, have vastly
assisted in reaching those parts which 50 years ago
were almost without religious teaching. Among
the poineers of over half a century ago is one who
still remains an energetic worker and ministers to
six congregations extending over a radius of 42
miles in a scattered district of Victoria. He is the
veteran Rev. Thomas Beecher Swift, of Alexandra
(Vic.), who for over 55 years has labored in the
vineyard of his Master. It is to such men as this,
that the Scots Church owe their proud position in
Australia at the present day. On October 13th,
1905, he celebrated his 84th birthday and received
congratulations from all parts of the Common-
wealth. At this green old age he still retains his
strength and vitality and enjoys splendid health
which he attributes to total abstinence from
alcohol and tobacco, and to the occasional use of a
well-known household remedy. “I have been a
Preacher of the Gospel for 52 years,” he writes on
May 19th, 1902, “and am now in my 81st year. I
suffered for a long time from costiveness and
consequent indigestion. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills were brought under my notice some two
years ago. I took one pill every evening, and soon
under that method my costiveness and indiges-
tion were relieved and my bowels became as
regular as the morning. They assist nature in
the discharge of all her functions—increase the
appetite, produce sound sleep, and give elasticity
of spirits. I always preach three times on
Sundays, and drive long distances to my appoint-
ments. I enjoy life and the Lord’s work to which
He called me more than 50 years ago.’’
Three years later he writes to us again as
follows : —“Referring to my previous letter, I can
still endorse the merits of your remedy. I was
suffering from constipation and indigestion for
many years. I tried many remedies but they all
lost their effect. But with Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills they are as efficacious now in assisting
nature as they were four years ago. I cannot do
without them, and one pill every night assures me
to be right for the next morning. Although lam
in my 84th year every fourth Sunday I drive 38.
miles, and on other Sundays 25 miles to fulfil my
appointments and conduct three services every
Sunday. My health was never better, thanks to
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills; my life never
brighter, my days never happier, and God’s work
under my care never more prosperous than at
present. lam sending you my photograph, and
am indeed glad to be able to state that Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills still maintain their standard of
excellence and efficiency.”
Mr. Swift has received many inquiries regarding
the above letters and he assures us that he is only
too happy to reply to anyone who may write
him.*
