The German mail steamer, lying at Circular
Quay, was the only important merchantman in
port which failed to decorate on Anniversary Day
-“Australia’s national holiday.
Richard Butters writes ;
In your issue, 28/12/’O5, there is a paragraph dealing
with the Bathurst libel case, Webb v. Wilton, about 35
years ago, which states that “T. T. Wiltoh Was im-
prisoned in default, but after a few days Mr. Webb drove
up in his carriage and ordered his release.” It is quite
true the Wilton Bros,, owners of the Batiiurst Times, by-
reason of a harsh and intolerant Act, now abolished, were
adjudged guilty and imprisoned in default, at the
Instance of Edmond Webb, a wealthy Bathurst store-
keeper, remaining in prison six weeks. Webb sent them
an offer of release, if they would apologise. This they
refused to do. Their numerous friends made a move to
subscribe the requisite amount to ensure their release.
The Wiltons objected to this procedure. At the end of
42 day-s, one evening at 8 o’clock, Alex. Forbes, the
gaoler, came to Wilton with the intimation that if he
would accept release he could have it. “ Yes,” he
answered; “if there are no conditions attached.” The
prisoners were at once released.

BEALE PIANOS.
TONE QUALITY in a Piano depends upon many things, of which the first and most important are the SCALE and the SOUNDING-BOARD.
HE SCALE of a piano is the scientific plan of its sound-producing parts, the length, thickness, and radiation of the strings, and the striking-line of
the hammers. The calculation of the scale is by far the most difficult part of pih'no manufacture, and can only be accomplished after many years’
experience. Most of the makers of foreign pianos simply. buy. their frames (or “plates”) from foundry people who are in most cases quite ignorant
of the mathematical requirements, and devoid of all scientific knowledge. One piano “manufacturer,” in writing a “Handbook of Piano-Manu-
facture,” recommends that a frame should be taken, out of some well-known piano, and a casting be ' made from that, thus saving the trouble of
calculating a scale ! But this cannot be done successfully, for there is an exact allowance to be made for shrinkage in casting. The only correct and
scientific way is to first calculate the scale, making allowance for two shrinkages, then cast a pattern, and from that cast the frame to be used. This is the
method followed in the production of the BEALE AUSTRALIAN PIANOS. . '
The SOUNDING-BOARD is the second important feature. No ordinary care is required in its manufacture; the highest skill and the most
perfect knowledge only will assure satisfactory results. The timber must be carefully selected, of the very best quality, and the grain must be graded from
fine in the treble to coarse in the bass. The strips of timber, never, more than five inches in width, must-also be tapered in thickness from treble to bass.
The amount of camber, or convexity (like a violin-belly), of the complete sounding-board must be arrived at to a hair s-breadth, so as to assure the best
quality of sound when the strings are vibrated.
In the Beale Australian Pianos all these details are most carefully watched; by specialists of many years’ training, in their turn guided by a man whose knowledge
of the whole result to be obtained—and the method of obtaining it—is unsurpassed im any'part of the world. The materials are the very best that money can buy. Our
great aim is always “ How much better can we make it ? ” And to this end we spare no pains, giving our every thought to the perfecting of an Australian product that is
already far ahead of its old-world competitors. That this is so you can verify for yourself by comparison. 1 Call and inspect our pianos, and then examine the goods of our
competitors. Our faith in our product is shown by the fact that we guarantee every Beale Australian Piano for Twenty-five years, whereas no importer will guarantee his
goods for more than Ten. Try, and see.
BEALE & COMPANY, LTD.

ONE HUNDRED AND FIFTEENTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT OF
The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney,
LIMITED.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting held at the Head Banking House, George Street,
Sydney, on Friday, 19th January, 1906.
TpHE Directors submit to the Shareholders a Balance Sheet showing the Liabilities and Assets of the Bank on the
* 31st December, 1905, and present the following report: —
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on current bills and accrued interest
on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying note tax, providing for land and income taxes and bad and
doubtful debts, as well as granting to the Officers a bonus on their salaries, amounts to .. .. .. £86,856 9 2
out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations :
To Payment of a Dividend foi the half-year at the rate of 10 percept. peraritttlm..£so,ooo 0 0
~ Transfer to Reserve Fund .. .. .. .. .. .. .. 15,000 0 0
„ Balance being Undivided Profit 21,856 9 2
£86.856 9 2
During the half-year a Branch has been established at Gladstone In this State, while the Branch at Emerald in
the State of Queensland has been closed.
The Dividend, free of Income Tax, is now payable.
GEORGE J. OOHEN, Chairman.

Sydney, 10th January, 1906.
We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances and counted the coin in the Head
Office of The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, Limited, and have compared the returns of the Branches, and
have found the same as specified in the foregoing Balance Sheet.
A. H. K. MAXWELL,!.
W. L. DOCKER, j-auditors.
The following resolutions were carried unanimously :
“ That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated among
the Proprietors.”
“ That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager and Officers of the Bank
for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the institution.
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
The Chairman (Mr. G. J. COHEN), in moving the adoption of the report, said: “ I have again the pleasure to
congratulate the shareholders on the very strong balance-sheet now presented to them. Comparing it, as usual, with
that of 12 months ago, you will notice that progression is shown in every line, with the exception of one, that is, bills
discounted and all debts due to the bank, which has decreased from £8,708,000 to £8,318,000. Having anticipated
this decline, the directors transferred £1,375,000 to London, where these moneys can be more profitably employed,
and have increased the investments under the heading of British Consols and Other Governnient securities by £202,000.
Going into detail, you will find that the reserve fund has been augmented by £30,000, and now stands at £1,100,000,
or £lOO,OOO in excess of the paid-up capital of the bank. The notes in circulation show an increase of £54,000, which
indicates a greater expenditure in connection with the productive interests of the State. Bills in circulation also
show an increase of £220,000, due in a large measure to the quantity and the enhanced value of our wool arid wheat
harvest. The increase of the deposits by £1,563,000 is also effected by the same causes, they having risen from
£11,602,000 to £13,165,000. Turning to the credit side, we show a total of coin and cash balances of £7,877,000, equal
to 10s. lOd. in the £on our total liabilities to the public. The volume of our business is shown by the totals of the
balance-sheet, now amounting to £16,654,000, an increase for the past year of £1,869,000. This is a record amount, so
far as this bank is concerned. I only trust that wise legislation in the future may create a greater demand for money
in these States, by inducing the investing public to embark in enteprises which will find more employment for the
people.”
NEW ZEALAND
GOVERNMENT LOAN
o, 1500,000
In 4 Per Cent. Debentures Due 1921.
THE Loan is issued, being balance of Loan of £1,000,000 authorised under The Aid to Public
Works and Land Settlement Act, 1905, for Land Settlement, .Railway Construction, and
various Public Works.
The Debentures will bear interest at 4 per cent, per annum, payable half-yearly on Ist January
and Ist July.
Principal and Interest will be payable in London, Melbourne, Sydney, and Wellington, to be
declared in tender.
The first coupon for six months’ interest is payable on Ist January, 1907. The Bank’s receipt
showing payment prior to Ist July, 1906, will entitle’the holders to interest on their payments at the
rate of 4 per cent, per annum from the date of such payment to Ist July, 1906.
Upon all payments made on or subsequently to the Ist July, 1906, accepted tenders must rebate
the proportional accrued interest represented in the coupon.
Tenders will be received by the Bank of New Zealand in Melbourne and Sydney up to 15tli
February, 1906, accompanied by a deposit in cash or by a bank cheque equal to 10 per cent, of the
face value of the Debentures applied for, and if the tender be accepted balance must be paid on or
before 31st August, 1906.
Holders of principal and interest payable in London, have the option at any time
during the currency of the Debentures of converting into 3-i per cent. Inscribed Stock, pari passu with
that quoted on the London Stock Exchange and due 1940 in London, at the rate of £lO3 of 3| per cent.
Inscribed Stock for every £lOO Debenture, and Government have option of converting in J uly, 1911,
those payable in London on giving 6 months’ notice.
The Debentures will be issued in denominations of .£lOO, £5OO, and £lOOO, and applicants
must state in their tenders the number and value of the Debentures they desire to receive.
No tender below £lollss. per £lOO will be entertained.
Tenders of the highest premium have preference, and to tenders of even rates Debentures will he
allotted proportionately to the applications. Tenders at a price including a fraction of a shilling other
than sixpence will not be preferentially accepted in respect of such fraction.
Failure to pay the balance on the final date will cause the deposit of 10 per cent, to be forfeited.
Members of recognised Stock Exchanges of Australasia will be allowed brokerage of per cent,
upon their accepted tenders.
For further information or particulars apply to Fred. Thonemann and Co., or Bank of New
Zealand.

Glass windows are rare in the interior of
Japan, and, according to N.S.W. Railway
Record, the windows of railway oars are streaked
with paint to restrain the way-back Jap from
shoving Ins head through the glass, under the
mistaken idea that there is nothing but air in the
aperture.
“ J-” : During a dispute on an “ all-night
train m Sydney recently a man drew a revolver,
and announced that he was only going to use it if
attacked by a bigger and stronger man than him-
ri' the other party to the dispute
slid on the seat onto the floor and tried to tear the
bottom out of the car to hide under. Everyone
swore, and of the nine remaining occupants of the
compartment three drew revolvers, and threatened
to start bombarding immediately unless the first
weapon was put away. The man who had roused
the demonstration took one glance at the massed
battery, and dropped into the street. The
peace-makers looked at one another, and then the
weapons disappeared. The chief interest of the
incident is the light that it throws on the increase
in the number of men who ha ve taken to going
about armed. Writer interviewed a man in the
trade and was assured that the demand in Sydney
tor cheap and not too reliable revolvers was a
steadily increasing one. Why ? Anyone who takes
■1'r tr £r ,, to Wrtlk from King-street, through
either VVoo loomooloo, Paddington, Darlinghurstor
Surrey Hills and to count the policemen he meets
will understand at once. Sydney seems the most
under-policed city in the world. In addition to the
paucity of their numbers the members of the force
are employed m a thousand and one other occupa-
tions and are not fully available for patrol purposes.
I lie consequence is that the people whose
business keeps them out late take steps to protect
themselves, and the sale of the cheap revolver
goes up by leaps and bounds. AVriter was walk-
ing home recently, and, owing to an attack of
rheumatism, was limping. A clean-limbed ruffian
snatched his time-payment Panama hat and fled
down Liverpool-street into the wilds of Woolloo-
niooloo. To lose a hat under any circumstances is
irritating, but to have one snatched before the
thing is paid for is an outrage that made the
victim forget all about the rheumatism and get up
.speed that astonished himself. He caught the
ihug a quarter of a mile away, and for some 30
minutes fought a running fight over another half
mile of streets and lanes and dark recesses. Then
the hat was recovered. No policeman was seen
the while. Next morning writer too bought a
cheap revolver.

“ Tommy Ruff ” : Being a constant reader
of The Bulletin when I ought to be doing other
things, and having had various inward rows with
it on the question of the Jap-man and other
matters too numerous to mention, and having also
noted its attitude in regard to the Australian
birth-rate, and the damnation in store for those
employers who advertise for married couples
“ without encumbrance,” and, being anxious to
avoid the aforesaid punishment, I acted accord-
ingly. I wanted a man, and was not long in find-
ing an applicant who seemed suitable, but he had
six children. I felt a bit staggered, but. being at
the time strongly under the influence of The Bul-
letin, I gave him the iob and thought no more
about the family. Seven years later kids seemed
a bit thick about the house. Made a count, and
tallied 12, but of course this may not be accurate,
as I may have missed some. Then I had a farm
and wanted a tenant, but did not want too many
scratcliers of furniture, &c. But, being still under
Bulletin influence, I let the farm to a man with
five kids. Latest returns are not to hand, but
counted 13 there a year ago. I have another
family in a cottage. Took a walk one Sunday
afternoon to help to digest my dinner and some
strong Bulletin statements, and visited the cot-
tage. An infant, aged one year, was at the door-
step. Then the mother, aged 25, appeared. Then
her mother, aged 49. Then her mother, aged 70.
Then HER mother, aged 91. These had assem-
bled to have their photos taken. The great bulk
of the family was having a picnic in the adjoin-
ing paddock, but some samples had crawled back
through the wire. I went away from there in a
reflective mood, wondering what The Bulletin’s
influence would lead me into next.
“F.H.” tells about a horrid jest
Wilcannia in the eighties. The river a banker. A
Chinaman had a garden about 20 miles down the river,
and he boarded an upward-bound steamer to come t
Wilcannia to bank his savings—nearly £10 ,
explained to the skipper. The steamer men wanted a
joke, so they asked the Chow to draw up a bucket, of
water. Now it takes an experienced river man to diav
water, when the boat is travelling up stream and a switt
current is running’ down.
The bucket is attached to about 10ft. of rope, and a man
that knows throws it up stream, and before the rope as
time to get taut, hauls it up again. Else he gets the full
force of the current in the bucket, plus the speed of the
boat, and he has either to let go the rope, or be jerked
overboard. ,, , ., . „
John threw the bucket overboard, and followed it a few
seconds later. The boat pulled into the bapk, and all
bands pottered about for an hour or two without lesult ,
then they went up to Wilcannia and reported the accident
t0 The news spread round the town m . 'IviMi' ''except'ional
bush telegraph must have been worked with ex0e P r ; lo th
vigor, for the day following, and for many days the
swaggies from up the river continued to hurry tmou B i
th TheTnhabitants suddenly developed a mania for Ashing,
and citizens could be seen at all hours making down
stream, ostentatiously carrying Ashing rods.
The parson organised a Sunday-school picnic anu
offered a reward to the child that fo “ n d^ k d ® £ f Ur t he’pore
as he said, “It was a sad thing to think of the pore
heathen lying out there lacking decent burial.
When the river went down the police found
remains. The mud around bore the imprint of many feet,
but the £100 had vanished.

Business, Robbery, etc.
One of Melbourne’s “ captains of industry ”
complained piteously a little while ago through the
local dailies about the conduct of the local Savings
Bank in not keeping him posted as to the state
of the account of an embezzling clerk. The said
clerk was in the habit of taking the captain’s
cheques and paying’ them into his own account at
the Savings Bank, and the victim contends that the
authorities of that institution should have known that
these were stolen, an l given him the information. For
sheer cheek, this demand would be hard to beat.
There is no reason why the Savings Bank should violate its
implied pledge of secrecy any more than any other bank.
To demand that the institution should run a detective
bureau to ascertain what every depositor does in his
working and leisure hours would be an incursion into
Socialistic legislation that the victim’s fellow captains of
industry would view with grave alarm.
* * * *
A Sydney grocer has struck a new idea in cash
coupons—he gives each ss. or 10s. a week customer a
“free” insurance policy in the A.M.P. An insurance
policy is a better thing to have about the premises than a
blue and yellow vase ; but whether it will do insurance
much good to be given away with a pound of tea is an-
other matter. As to being “free”—well, storekeepers
don’t sell sugar for the good of their health, and
they can no more supply free insurance policies than
they can supply free groceries. The storekeeper has
to pay for the insurance, and the customer has to
pay the storekeeper ; and why the customer shouldn’t
buy from the insurance society straight-out, instead
of buying insurance from the grocer, and incidentally
getting in a quantity of groceries, is a matter that may
be left to the customer to think over.
» * * •
The Stinking Fish party, as seen through the
eye of a Yankee capitalist now in Australia:—“Your
business men ought to get a move on before they catch
cold. They’ve gotten to holts with the Labor men, and
their only idea of fighting is to tumble down themselves
in the hope that some of the mud’ll get on him too. Nine
out of ten of them ought to be peddling hairpins for all
they know’ about business. They talk about the Labor man
grafting for Government interference, and instead of
putting up a fight against him they go about whining to
the Government to interfere for them. Of course, the
Labor man wants Government interference. It’s his
game. But it’s the game of the capitalist to let the
Government alone and fight other way*. We know
better than that over in Amurrioa. You don't find
our boards of trade hustling round deputationising
and yawping in public about measures that affect their
interests. The public never swallows gup like that when
it knows the man who’s talking. Our capitalists put up
the dough and let it do the talking. If I was settled in
Australia, the Labor party could have all the legislation.
I’d have the administration. When you’ve got a good
fighting fund of a hundred thousand dollars you can get
heads of public departments to listen to a lot of reason.
Your capitalists here want the Government to do every-
thing for them. They’re so silly, they howl out a lot of
stuff that spoils their own markets, and they’re so darned
mean they won’t cough up for their own interests. They
want the whole body of the people to do their fighting
for them, and they forget that nine-tenths of the same
people are on the other side and are their market. It’s a
sweet proposition for a few shrewd business-men to
come over here and put your great business people out
of a job.’*
• * * *
It is hard to believe that the result of the
N.S. W. Wines Adulteration Act—one prosecution in three
years—shows effective administration. Some very vile
wine is sold in Sydney and suburbs —wine which ought to
be liable to prosecution if it isn’t. Certainly the unfor-
tunates who drink it run a very good chance of prosecu-
tion for disorderly behavior, for there are seven separate
individual devils in each bottle. What system of search
and analysis is followed by the inspector under the Act ?
* * * <
Said that the recent rise in the price of boots
will be a permanent one. Many causes conspire to make
leather dearer. The area of the world devoted to grazing
oxen and sheep gradually dwindles, and the use of leather
is extending through the new demand from Asia, which
kicks off its grass shoes and dons leather ones. Perhaps
the rise in leather values will at last make Australian
cattle-owners consider the branding question. A vast
proportion of Australian hides are seriously deteriorated
by foolish branding.
* * * *
Quotations in Sydney live snake market now
run from 6d. to Is. a head. The Zoo, the Museum, and
general dealers are buyers, and for anything very special
spring- an extra coin or two. Dead snakes are “off.”
Away in the past there was a Government reward, but
the dead snake now fetches nothing.

* * » *
“ Viator ” : One of the most curious employ-
ments in America is that of the railway detective, known
as a tracer. He is never after people. His business is to
hunt up a piece of rolling stock, usually a truck, which
has been lost in the ramifications, and it may trickle all
over the country. It is a point of honor with railroad
managers never to lose a bit of stock. The
tracer would be kept all his lifetime after one
truck if he couldn’t find it sooner. Some years
ago an infuriated Victorian M.L.A. came back from
a trip in S. Australia to report that he had discovered
some of Victoria’s lost rolling-stock running quite com-
fortably on one of the Crow-eater’s local trains, from
which it would seem that Victoria didn’t keep a tracer in
these days and that it wanted one badly. When the new
invention for altering the gauge of rolling-stock comes into
general use, so that a Vic. or N.S. W. carriage can escape on
to the narrow-gauge lines of North Queensland, or take to
itself wings and flee to Oodnadatta, a tracer, or an attack
of honesty, will be much wanted.
• * * *
“The union label,” said the furniture broker
with derision, “ won’t make my world weep. All we’ll
do, if unlabelled stuff becomes a drug on the market, is
send it to be auctioned out among the magnificent
household fittings of J. Smith, Esq., who has been
suddenly called to England. Its newness is explained by
the fact'that it only recently arrived to his order fiom
European art furnishers. At present I regularly put
through sales of Chinese-made furniture in our breaking-
up auctions, and get far better prices than in the open
market. Ricketty things, catalogued as the well-pre-
served furniture of the Hon. J. Smith, go better than the
best made things at open sale."
Out-back bank managers are often driven to
their wits’ end dodging questions put by big clients con-
cerning the little fellow’s account. But the average bank
official, l.kc the medico, wraps as much dignity and silence
abmt Little Fellow’s 2Jd. as about Big Head’s £lO,OOO
balance. A N. Queensland man told writer once that the
late James Tyson asked the bank to acquaint him with the
exact standing of a certain small squatter. The manager
wrapped himself in gloom and declined to oblige even
Tyson. Writer heard the following dialogue while stand-
ing near a banker’s den one day:
“ Has he £3OO in your bank ?”
“ Really, Mr. Largehead— er—really ”
Silence.
“ If I present his cheque for £3OO will it be met ?
“ Wouldn’t it be better to present it ? ”
More silence.
* * * «
A recent Gazette gives the koala nine months’
protection throughout N.S.W. A needed regulation and
one that will, if strictly enforced, enable the little tribe
to fill up family blanks. Native bear fur is growing in
demand, and unless the little creature with the foolish,
friendly countenance is protected by law it will wholly
disappear in a few years. The regulation must therefore
be strictly enforced, and a good way to do that is to keep
an official eye on metropolitan fur shops, and on auction
sales and exports. “ What price for 300 native bear skins
in good order and condition is the substance of a letter
a city firm received from a pair of rabbiters within the
last month.
* * * *
Dr. Ercole, one of the little group of Italian
medical men that have come this way, has written a
pamphlet in which he wants to know what Australia
means by not growing its own castor-oil. Ercole has only
been a very few years in Australia, and most of them were *
spent out in White Cliffs; but'even out in White Cliffs
the shock of Australia’s importing disease reaches the
intelligent foreigner. The castor-oil plant is alleged to be
one of the few things that flourish on drought; and Aus-
tralia’s supply of drought ought to satisfy even the
castor-oil plant. The viscid stuff that the howling small
boy has to swallow after the birthday party is only a
fraction of what can be got out of the tree, Dr. Ercole
says ; Australia might feed flocks of silkworms on the
leaves, and, after getting half-a-dozen sorts of oil out of
the seeds, might fatten flights of pigs on the residue
cake. Some patient Australian with a foreign name will
probably try it some day ; meantime Australia buys the
foreigner’s castor-oil—just as for years it foolishly bought
the foreigner’s alleged olive-oil until it found out that it
could make purer olive-oil itself.
* • * *
“ Tim ” : Findin’ dorgs and gettin’ the rewards
ain’t part of me profession now. It happened this way. I
snavelled a lovely collie out Toorak way. T reckoned that
Jigger would ’ave give me three bloomin’ quid for ’im. It
is very risky with ’igh-bred animals though, so I decided,
if the reward wur near a quid, to do the ’onerable. Well,
a couple of days later ’e wur advertised, and £5 reward
offered. My ! this wur orlright. I got ’is nibs orl nice
and clean and took ’im over to Toorak in the afternoon.
I wur disappointed with the ’ouse though ; it wur only a
cottage. A big, red-’eaded cove comes out to see me.
“ Evenin’, gov’nor,” sez I, takin’ orf me ’at, “ I’ve brought
yer dorg. ’E strayed into my place at Richmond, and
I’ve looked after ’im like a baby.” The red-’eaded bloke
stared coldly at me and the dorg for arf a mo. “Sorry
for yer trouble, old man, but that ain’t my dorg,”
sez ’e. “ Wot ? ” sez I, growin’ excited, “ ain’t this house
Boggs Yale, and ain’t this dorg a collie, and ain’t it
got a ring round its neck and a blotch on its
nose. It must be yourn.” ’Owever, ’e wur positive it
wurn’t. Lor’, but I wur disappointed over them five
quids, an’ goin’ ’ome I could ’ave kicked the tail orf the
flamin’ tyke. Well, early next mornin’ there comes a
’orrible rattety-bang-bang to the door. I ’adn’t been
doin’ nuffin lately, so I opens the door. Strewth ! I did
get a shock. It wur the big red-’eaded cove from Toorak,
f only ’e wur in policeman’s clothes now; an’ to make
matters worse, the blarsted collie rushes out an’ starts
climbin’ orl over a fat chap ’e ’ad with ’im. The fat bloke
took the dorg away, an’ orl I got wur a lecture from the
red-’eaded John, an’ ’is solemn promise to look back in a
week to see if I wur workin’. An’ I ’ad spent Is. 3d. on
dorg’s meat, an’ sunk arf a dollar in beer on the strength
of that £5 reward. The cove that puts in a false adver-
tisement an’ gets yer to take ’is dorg to a red-’eaded cop’s
’ouse, so as ’e can foller yer an’ get yer address an’ drop
on yer, is only fit ter be in the Upper ’Ouse.
FORTY MILLION POUNDS’ WORTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Forty million pounds’ worth—
Made in foreign slums
And in Merrie England’s slave dens—
Through the Customs comes.
Australian workmen rushing
To other lands away ;
And forty million pounds’ worth
Coming in to-day.
Forty million pounds’ worth—
Pleasant little div.—
Fattens the importers
(Parasites must live).
Splendid is the harvest,
The Foreign-traders glean ;
Yet we vote for Robinson
And for Thomas Skene.
Forty million pounds’ worth—
Think of it, ye chumps
Who sit and smoke cigar butts
And bawl “ What ho ! she bumps !
Australian workmen idle,
Nought to do but pray—
And forty million pounds’ worth
Coming in to-day.

During December, Westralia actually sup-

“Paul Cupid” : A fairly accurate idea of
the general disposition of a horseman can be
formed by observing the paces of the quadruped
he rides. If you see a moke jogging along half
asleep with a piece of his tongue hanging out, it
is safe to bet that his rider is a man who wouldn’t
care to rise before his usual time even if the house
were on fire. Also he is a man who takes a long
time to decide to do a thing, and another long
time to do it. If a horse shows nervousness at
imaginary things and shies badly at an ant on the
road, and at other times jogs along in a methodical
way, his rider is bound to be a high-strung indi-
vidual. easily startled, though at other times of a
desirable disposition. Then there is the horse
that strides along at a fast, swinging walk with
his head up. The man on him has plenty of con-
fidence in himself, dislikes dulness, and is fond of
good appearance and applause. When a horse
throws his head about, prances and runs sideways,
his rider is generally a brainless, conceited ass.
The reason why the horse is a guide in the matter
is because a, man, when free to select, takes that
nearest to his taste. For instance, if the first man
got on the last horse you would see him with his
hat turned, under at the sides or tied down with a
handkerchief, and he’d rather walk than ride that
horse again.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
MEDICATOR
Catarrh is inflammation of g
the lining membrane of the |
nose and adjoining passages.
If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the pas-
sages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the
sight and hearing, destroys
ihe sense of taste and smell,
renders the breath offensive,
breaks down the affected tis-
sues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the
small frontal bones of the
skull. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want
relief and cure? If so, try
our great remedy.

The Tory.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN “ the early eocene,” when the mammoth
strode, serene.
O’er the tertiary green, monopolising most
o’ things ;
Rose a creature called a man, with a system and a
plan,
Claiming rights, and straight began to organise a
host o’ things.
Said the creature, queer of shape—once an anthro-
poidal ape—
“ Things are in a shocking scrape; I’m bent upon
improving them.
lam going to expand. There are troubles in
the land;
I intend to take a hand reforming or removing
them.”
Then a Tory mastodon, fat and fierce to gaze
upon,
In whose rheumy eye there shone a light of
, shocked propriety,
Cried, with indignation vast: “Socialist! Icono-
clast !
He’ll disorganise and blast respectable society! ”
But the resolute ex-ape moulded matters into
shape ;
Seeking ever to escape a state of things frus-
traneous;
And the dear old mastodon—Tory of the ages
gone—
Rests his ancient bones upon the strata subter-
raneous. /
In the days when godly men thought their wives
—say nine or ten—
Wholly insufficient when they could afford a score
or so;
When Solomon (who owned the mines) couldn’t
count his concubines;
And all moneyed masculines could boast at least
of four or so
Rose there then, within the land, one who made a
strange demand :
This no less that they should hand all surplus
femininity—
Concubine and paramour—round to the deserving
poor,
Holding to themselves secu.b each one a lone
affinity.
Then polygamists, with fat quaking, asked, by
this and that,
What the Devil was he at ? This was stark in-
sanity !
“Fool!” they cried, in shocked surprise, “Are
you seeking—dash your eyes !
To ruin private enterprise to satisfy your vanity ?”
And the old monopolists ramped about and shook
their fists,
Shouted, hooted, cursed, and hissed. Men of
proven piety
Swore at him with language bad. “ Socialist ! ”
they yelled, “You’re mad !
Visionary ! Why your fad’s an insult to society /”
But those dear old Tory chaps have departed ;
' and, perhaps,
Modern marriage—bar mishaps—now satisfies
democracy.
flius, at last—at least they say— that reformer
got his way;
And polygamy to-day revolts the aristocracy.
In the feudal days of yore, a baron—Tory to the
core—
Owned a thousand serfs or more ; and land ?—he
had the run of it
For many leagues about. But lo ! a foolish, low-
born lout
Bewailed his lot, and blurted out he “couldn’t
see the fun of it.”
Roared the good old baron then, calling on his
serving men;
Clapped the caitiff in a den ; and swore with loud
impiety..
“Zounds! Gad zooks ! ” the baron cried, when
the rack had been applied.
“A Socialist! ’Tis well he died! A menace to
society 1 ”
jSo on, down throughout the ages, that old Tory
* person rages.
Note the cry, in all its stages, heird in every
latitude.
Though the primal type has gone, hear the same
old mastodon
Rave, and snort, and trumpet on the same old
Tory platitude.
“ Stop the Socialistic fraud ! Private Enterprise !
Good Lord !
(Let the news not get abroad.) It’s Blasphemy !!
Impiety !!!
It seeks my ‘ sacred rights ’ to wrest ; land, gold—
all that is best.
Arrest it! It is menacing respectable society ! ”
Same old, tough polygamist; same old, fat mono-
polist ;
Greedy eye and grasping fist; air of smug pro-
priety.
Mastodon or merchant robber; feudal lord or
Crown-lands jobber—
It’s the same old Tory slobber, same old whine
about “society.”
S.A.
C. J. DENNIS.
Clodd’s Factotum.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ rpiNG-A-LING-LING.”
I Clodd and Co.’s accountant, cashier, and
bookkeeper dashed down a line of
figures, scrawled a signature, threw down the pen
and swung off the stool.
“ Are you there?.”
“Yes.”
“ Ticket's spoakin’. Take down an order, will
v’ ?”
“All right. Goon.”
“Forty-eight men’s, sample 145, reg’lar sizes—
-12 pairs 207, 6 four, 6 five, 24 pairs 208, reg’lar
size. Want ’em got through at once, tell Mr.
Clodd.”
“All right, Mr. Tickel.”
“ And a pair children’s 2-bar shoes, by 11
o’clock.”
“Got none in stock, I’m afraid.”
“Well, must have ’em by 11.”
“ It’s 10 to 11 now.”
“Don’t matter — must have ’em.”
I’ll send them out by 4 o’clock.”
“ All right, but mind it is four to-day, and not
four this day next century.”
“ All right,” weariedly, putting up the receiver.
The accountant climbed back on her high stool,
and directed and stamped some envelopes at top
speed.
Upstairs the engine was working, the clanking
machinery sending a thrill through the whole
factory. Annette Crane did not hear it. she
only noticed it when it stopped and a ghastly
silence seized the building. She got out the
ledger and began posting up. A man came in
and left an order. Another came, gave a mes-
sage, and took away a parcel. The Boss stalked
in from the clicker's room.
“Say, Miss Crane, posted up the ledger yet?”
“ No” (morosely). It was 96 in the office, and
she had been consistently interrupted in her regu-
lar work since entering it at nine that morning.
“Well, why the damn ain’t y’? Anyone been
in ? ”
Miss Crane gave the message and orders, written
and verbal.
“ Oh, well, write out all the country orders and
let me have ’em by three, will y’ ?' And finish
that postin’. And say, I’m goin’ out to Mackville
and I want you to call round for Daley’s cheque
durin’ your dinner-hour. It ’ll do y’ good and y’
c’n look at the shops, millingry and that.”
He winked and went out.
“ Ting-a-ling-ling!”
“Ye-e-es.”
‘ ‘ Beale and Day speaking. Have you got size
9 in 917 Bal. in stock ?. ”
“ Wait on, and I’ll see.”
Annette put up the receiver and went into the
next room, where hundreds of white cardboard
boxes were, ranged round it on the shelves.
Glancing rapidly over a row at one end she re-
turned to the office.
“No, Mr. Venn.”
“ Well, get us a pair by 4 o’clock, will you, Miss
Crane ? ”
“ All right, Mr. Venn, I’ll try.”
She rang otf, and climbed the stool once more.
Before her pen reached the ink the telephone
went again.
“ 0 d—ash ! ”
“Well, what do you want?” she demanded,
ill-temperedly. “o—-ah, that you, Mr. Hazell?”
her tone changing miraculously as she recognised
the voice of their biggest customer.
“Yes, it is; and I’d like to know what the
devil you mean by not sending along that pair of
boots for Mr. Wills? You said we should have
them by half-past nine this morning ? He’s wait-
ing here now for them.”
“Well, I’m sure I don’t know, Mr. Hazell.
The ord.er was sent up all right, and I thought the
boots had gone.”
“ Well, they haven’t; and ” -
“ If you’ll wait, I’ll inquire about them. ”
“Look smart then. I can’t wait all day.”
“ Old beast,” remarked Miss Crane to herself as
she scratched off a few lines to the foreman and
sent it upstairs by a small boy. In answer to her
query as to what she should tell Mr. Hazell, the
foreman wrote back :
“ Tell him any infernal lie you like. The boots
aren’t done, and not likely to be before to-morrow.”
After a great deal of trouble and a subtle
evasion of the infernal lie, she got rid of the
obstreperous Hazell, and returned to her desk.
At half-past 12 a bell rang, and the machinery
overhead stopped. A roaring buzz of voices took
its place, and a crowd of girls and men poured
down the cranky stairs to their dinner.
Annette Crane ate hers hurriedly, put on her
hat and gloves, took her parasol, and walked down
Rundle-street to Micky Daley’s. It was plea-
santer than the stuffy office, but she did not follow
Mr. Clodd’s advice and examine the “ millingry ”
behind the plate-glass windows. She had no
intention of making comparisons between the
dainty confections— “straight from Paris”—and
the bargain-sale last-year Leghorn pinned firmly
on to her fair curly locks.
Mr. Daley, in leathern apron and much-grimed
hands, received her with fatherly pleasantries.
Annette liked Micky—the supply of his good
temper and sympathy was equal to an endless
demand, and she found his rich brogue soothing
after the brazen tones of Mr. Clodd, which
twanged in her ears from morning till night.
Putting his cheque for £5O in her purse, she
sauntered plowly back to the factory—sauntered
because she was doing Clodd and Co.’s business
in her own private time, and cared nothing for
the extra ten minutes occupied in transacting it.
For the same reason Mr. Clodd did not remark
on her absence from her post during those ten
minutes.
As she prepared _to enter Daley’s cheque in the
cash-book she noticed it was an open one, and
resolved to, cross it. Before the entry was made,
however, a customer entered and asked for Mr.
Clodd. Annette hastily thrust the cheque into an
empty pigeon-hole above the desk, and went in
search of the Boss.
It was about an hour later that she suddenly
recollected the cheque. With a slight thrill of
alarm she went to get it from the pigeon-hole.
It was nqt there !
The compartment stared blankly at her. With
a feeling that the pains of Hell had got hold of her,
she put a small, ink-stained hand into the pigeon-
hole and fingered unresponsive sides, then took it
out, dirtier by the addition of much dust. With
feverish energy she dragged every scrap of paper,
together with bits of string, rubber bands, pen-
holders, boxes of boot-buttons, and stars,
from the several compartments, and put each back
separately after a careful examination. All the
time she knew the search was hopeless—knew
which particular hole had betrayed its trust, and
that these others were innocent.
Fifty pounds and an open cheque ! The thing
was so sudden that she could not accurately place
her position.
She sat down suddenly and heavily upon a
chair, and stared hard at the dirty floor, with its
litter of brown paper and boot boxes. Thoughts,
foolish and sane, raced like an express train
through her mind. Her home, with the hard-
working mother ; the sister who was a lady-help
and had a Savings Bank account of £3 ; the clerk
brother, who spent too much on cigarettes, and
“shouts,” and cricket and lacrosse clubs, and
place-totes, and bicycles to ever have a penny in
hand —they all stood out like a flashlight picture
before her. She imagined herself flinging the
bombshell of her news into their midst that even-
ing, and an icy hand grasped her heart.
A brazen voice, sounding above the roar of the
machinery in the next room, sent a stab of fear
through the girl. She pictured—at least she tried
to picture—herself telling Mr. Clodd. But here
her imagination fainted. The bank on which the
cheque was drawn ought to be warned of the loss,
but she shrank from this final acknowledgment of
her desperation, and besides by this it would be
too late if her worst surmise were correct.
Annette sprang to her feet, and a wild hunt
round the room, under the desk, and on a parcel-
strewn table, ensued. But no miracle had oc-
curred, and Micky Daley’s cheque did not come
to light.
Then it must have been stolen.
Annette endeavored to recall the numerous
visitors she had had that afternoon, and the cus-
tomer who had been the cause of her not crossing
the cheque flashed across her mind. He must
have seen her put it in the pigeon-hole. He could
easily have abstracted it during her absence while
going for Mr. Clodd. She had not witnessed his
subsequent demeanor, because she had remained
in the stock-room attending to the vagaries of an
appallingly stupid stock-girl, taken on the pre-
vious day, until he left.
Yet she had always thought Mr. Brownson
honest. If he frequently kept them waiting for
their money he always paid it eventually, and no
one had ever breathed a word against his fair
fame. Perhaps the sudden temptation—Miss
Crane sat down and groaned. She was ruined,
There would be an awful row, and she would lose
her handsome weekly remuneration of 15s. and
the privilege of getting her footwear at wholesale
prices. She would probably be a grey-haired old
hag before another billet came her way.
She would tell Mr. Clodd when he came in
next. She heard his step moving about in the
adjoining room. She would tell him. But what
should she say ? How would she put it ? Her
dry lips moved, expressing the opening phrase in
an infinitude of ways Tears were in her eyes—
despair in her heart.
She heard Mr. Clodd come towards the door
and rose to her feet, clenching her teeth.
He came in with an armful of boots and shoes
and dropped them on the untidy table.
“ Them’s got to be fellowed up soon’s y’ can do
it,” he said, and turned to go out again.
“ Mr. Clodd !”
“Eh ?” He stopped and looked round.
Annette Crane’s feet touched bottom in the
gulf of dark despair.
“ Mr. Clodd —what —what did you do with that
cheque of Mr. Daley’s I put in the pigeon-hole ? ”
she burst out.
“That? 0, I put it in the safe—for safety
like. Y’ shouldn’t leave them things lyin’ round
like that.’’
He went upstairs to the finisher’s room to
denounce one of the men for putting a fiddle waist
on a common pair of boots just sent down.
S.P.
The Voice of Nature.
By “Maes.”
Whenever anyone suffers from rheumatism, gout, neuralgia, lumbago, sciatica, backache, blood
disorders, anaamia, indigestion, jaundice, biliousness, sick headache, general debility, gravel, stone,
bladder disease, or Bright’s Disease, it is but Nature crying aloud for help and saying, “Your liver is
out of order ! Your kidneys are not doing their work! Restore them to health, or your suffering
cannot cease.” Nature will not be outraged. So long as the urinary and biliary poisons, which cause
the disorders mentioned, remain in the system you must puffer. When the kidneys and liver are
fulfilling actively their appointed functions the poisons which cause the disorders are expelled in a
natural manner, and suffering ceases.
The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly
an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily all this solid matter leaves the body
dissolved in the urine, but if, through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins,
contaminating the" whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning, such as
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Gravel, Stone,
and Bladder Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are healthy is to place some
urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning.
If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural color, or has particles floating
about in it, the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must immediately be taken to restore their
vigor, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifestations of uric poisoning will result.
The Liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made
from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes
sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it
to the blood as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble,
into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles which have
lived their life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from some
form of biliary poisoning, such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Genera
Debility, and Blood Disorders. I
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys and that done by the liver, that
where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys the liver becomes affected in sympathy and vice
versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of these vital organs
which resulted in the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as Warner’s Safe
Cure. Certain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine could be
found which would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an exhaustive
search for such a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in com-
pounding a medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe Cure
exhibits a marvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys and
liver, and restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it of necessity cures all complaints
due to the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the kidneys
and liver naturally eliminates the poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons cease.
Cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent, simply because they are natural. [l2

To the Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A DAY and a night I’ve harkened
Across the parching plain,
To the ringing of the metals,
To the roaring of the train ;
The smoky distance held the miles,
And ever, as we drew near,
The pounding engine caught them
And flung them to the rear.
The heat haze dimmed the sunlight,
And wrapt the pallid day,
The bush-fire swept before us
To bar our shining way ;
The west wind roared pursuit of us,
And ever, as we won through,
The white dust rose and followed
The way the west wind blew.
The night glowed to the dawning,
The dawning burned to noon,
And still the pounding engine
Beat out its ruthless tune.
The red sun sinks, the afterglow
Lives still in the east, and we,
Leaving the grey bush twilight,
Curve down beside the sea.
A sea all naked and hurried
Beneath the fading sky,
Coppery hills and hollows,
Foam-crests that hiss and fly.
Forgot be fierce and parching heats,
Forgot be dust and drouth,
For a chill wind, a wet wind,
Is blowing from the south.
The sandhills lift a bulwark
Against the raging seas,
The sword-grass and the bent-grass
Thresh to an eager breeze,
The tide hums past the shuddering piles
And laps below the ships,
For the south wind, the sea wind,
Blows salt upon my lips.
The lightship at her moorings
Dips like a shooting star,
Far o.ut the restless breakers
Leap to the whitened bar.
Thin as a web the spindrift flies
Above the churning race
As the salt wind, the sea wind,
Blows full upon my face.
QUEENSLAND.
A.W.
Evolution.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHE w:as born in a shanty at the Back o’
Beyond. She existed. She slept, she
woke, she wept, she fed, she slept again.
Later she lay for hours on her back on an old
shawl, gnawing at a tiny fist, her vague, blue
eyes wandering dully from corner to corner of the
sloping ceiling. She slept in odd corners, and
woke and wailed j for what she did not know.
Ther6 were certain things that ministered to her
senses—warmth, freedom from swathing of
clothes, her mother’s breasts; but she did not
realise these things. She was only conscious
unreasoningly, when they came her way, of a
sense of comfort, in the same way that she felt
vaguely the physical discomfort of a misplaced
pin, without knowing the source of either, or the
remedy. She was a little animal, less developed
than a puppy of the same age.
There were on her horizon of life at this time
three figures that she began dimly to distinguish
from the inanimate objects around her, without
distinguishing between the functions of the three.
One, on something of her level, was rough, hairy,
sniffing, and touched her face and hands with
something smooth and thin and wet. Another
towered above her, but occasionally bore her aloft,
sometimes fed her, sometimes caressed, and some-
times slapped. The third figure came and went
with strange burning odors and uncouth sounds,
sometimes stumbling over her, sometimes rolling
her aside with the toe of a hob-nailed boot.
This little world of hers was bounded by the four
walls of the living room she knew. But herself in
relation to herself did not exist as yet. These
others all had places, no matter how ill-defined, in
the scheme of existence, but she was no more
than a cypher and had no knowledge of identity.
But one day her Ego came to her. She woke
and howled. She wanted something. The dog
came and thrust a cold nose into her hand. She
beat at him impatiently. She did not want him
and she knew it. Her father dandled her, shook
her, tried to feed her. She repelled his well-
meant efforts with louder howls. She wanted
none of these things. Then her mother dawned
upon her tears and an arrested sob became a
strangled chuckle. She knew what she wanted.
A tiny embryo of a human soul put out a doubtful
feeler to the light.
Later, as she brawled about the room, she
learned several things. She came to know that,
if one put a curious finger on that fascinating red
thing that smoked and blazed in a corner of the
room, pain, swift, sure, inevitable, followed. And
she learned the hopelessness of grasping at the
elusive quicksilver of the sunlight that danced on
the wall. She grew into a little wild thing,
uncouth, greedy—fighting with her sisters and
brothers for scraps of food. Her only pastime
was to swing on a,.wire fence and speed a passing
train with wild yells. She had no sense or
questioning.as to what a train might be or do, but
the noise and speed Wakened a sort of ferocity of
exultation in her, and she was impelled to hoot
and shriek in sympathy. Her only love was her
m’other and fat bacon. Her mother came first ;
she could rtot reason why. " The bacon was grate-
ful to her palate and her stomach, and never took
violent revenges on her, whereas her mother fre-
quently cuffed and beat her. But the spark of
humanity, burning feebly, never quite quenched
within her, triumphed over the senses, and her
mother came first in her love.
The longest step upward she took, between the
ages of two years and ton, was when her sense of
humor woke up. There is no humor in the brute ;
it is a quality essentially human. She had often
shrieked with senseless laughter in her rough-and-
tumble games with her companions, but one day
she came across a comic cut in a paper, and she
laughed out in the sudden surprised enjoyment of
it. It gave her such an unexpected elation that
she was always looking for the humorous side of
things afterwards.
Her mother died. It was her first sense of
grief, and it was a shock and a horror to her. On
the death-bed, the mother, smitten with com-
punction, spoke of God, and, sitting beside the
grave, she offered her first prayer: “God, take
my mother out o’ that hole.” Death meant no
more to her then, but later it brought an uneasy
sense of mystery and the unseen. Even in the
sunlight she shivered. She howled and moaned
like a bereaved animal, with no hope or know-
ledge of the Hereafter. Her father first brought
her the rough comfort of his philosophy ; then he
coaxed her with fat bacon, and finally he beat her.
She took no notice of his comfort, she let the dogs
eat the bacon, and she scarcely felt the strokes of
his leathern belt on her bare legs. But when she
realised that God was heedless and the grave fast-
sealed, she ceased her lamentations without any
resentment. She was only an animal still, and
she accepted, with the dull patience of the animal,
the strokes of Fate.
But she looked on life with a difference. She
was afraid of the dark now and of the utter silence
of any open space. There was something above,
or beyond, or around her, and she knew if that
grave were opened she would not find her mother
there. Sometimes she was terrified. It was the
birth-pangs of her imagination.
The years went on. Coming back from the
township she met —a man. He was only a meagre
boy, with an unshaven chin and a slouching gait,
but their eyes met. Her heart suddenly beat
thick and fast, but she kept on her way. She
knew instinctively that he followed, and looking
back found she had not been mistaken. He spoke
to her, and they walked together a short distance,
saying little. He looked at her furtively from
time to time, and she shuffled the dust with her
bare toes, and occasionally giggled. When they
parted she hardly knew why she felt so exultant;
but it was the mysterious call. The next few
days she was restless and unsettled. It was the
crude, unformed soul of her —big with awakening
instincts, striving to deliver itself.
They met again, and he made her a present of
a tiny pocket mirror. The moment that she
looked into it her woman’s soul was born in her.
She gazed leng. and sighed and smiled, and knew
herself to be beautiful.
She laughed out at the discovery ; at the clear
reds and whites of her skin, the tender curves of
neck and bosom. She gloated, almost drunken
with the delight of it. Gazing in her own eyes,
she thought that some magic had changed her —
wild, elfish, unkempt —into something beautiful
and gracious. But it was her woman’s soul being
born there —immature, before its time, but never
to sleep again. That night she curled her hair.
God knows where she got that instinct, but in its
vanity it was a distinct step in the scheme of her
development; and the next day she met the boy
with a bush of yellow frizzes round her head. She
walked with him to the township. But she was
restless, dissatisfied, loathing her bare legs and
scanty skirts. She clothed herself in the best of
her mother’s clothes, and the next time they met
her lover praised hhr, and she tossed her head ;
and when he asked her for a kiss she said, “ Like
your cheek ! ”
“Like your cheek!” This wild child of the
bush had never hgsrd of the modesty or the
- coquetry of women,'but she was following out her
primaeval instincts as surely as the most accom-
plished flirt of the cities. When he attempted to
put a hand on her she fled, half hysterical with
fear lest he should catch her, half sick with disap-
pointment when he gave up the chase. Sex was
awake at last. She earned money in the town-
ship, and clothed herself in gay dresses, tawdry
jewels and feathered head-gear, and paraded arm-
in-arm with other girls of like tastes. Now she
was a type. The clean, primitive animalism in
her nature had deteriorated into a phase of the
human, less loveable. But Love raised her. She
gasped and shook for the ecstasy of it—and the
agony. She thrilled, and dreamed, and laughed,
and wept over it. She wove a marvellous fabric,
dyed in Love’s alcliemies, and threw it over the
shoulders of her lover, glorifying the under-sized
boy with the scrubby chin.
There was a wedding, and motherhood came to
her. The Divine-in-the-Human woke in her.
She had loved her mother, she had loved her
lover, she supposed she loved God as an abstract
good, far-off, not to be approached in the affairs
of every-day life. This was different. This fierce,
sweet ache at her heart was bliss and pain
unspeakable. Divine —yes; but after all a thread
divided here the animal and the Divine, for the
love with which she gazed on the tiny creature,
and fingered its limbs, and gloated over its minia-
ture perfections was divinely animal. It was
hers —had she not been stricken through and
through with mortal pangs that it might lie
against her breast, warm, living, breathing ? And
stricken, gladly—gladly !
It was then she reached the height of her de-
velopment—then that her dwarfed soul, with a last
effort, rose out ot its narrowness. Then she slipped
bade, down, down the links of the chain of
evolution she had forged. Other children came
to iler, money was scarce, cares multiplied. She
toiled for husband and children. Her husband
neglected her, her sons grew up worthless. She
wept until she had no tears left, and her sensi-
bilities dried up. With those she toiled for it was
. sometimes a blow for her, sometimes a caress. It
was uncertain and equally unmeaning, and she
neither resented nor welcomed one nor the other.
She grew withered, silent, a little vacant.
Then she was dying. She had lived long and
had had all that Life had to give her. She had
not asked for more, or expected more, nor con-
sciously had she wanted more. Her only protest,
now and then, had been her tears, and they had
been neither supplicating nor resentful, but spon-
taneous. They, too, had dried at the fountain
head. So she lay dying. Her little world was
closing in upon her in the four walls of the room
where she lay. Again there were three figures
upon her horizon—one, a hairy creature that
licked her feeble hand ; and one, a woman who
fed and tended her; and another, a figure that
came with burning odors and uncouth noises to
her bedside. And sometimes she turned and
murmured—for what she did not know. One day
she moaned and muttered sorely. They brought
her food—she pushed it from her. They brought
her husband and her dissolute youngest son, but
she looked beyond them and moaned. Then her
God dawned upon her tears and her moans were
changed to a last faint, tremulous laughter.
